Maturidi Position on Takwin and Mukawwan
Maturidi view on takwin and mukawwan eliminating need for jihāt al-fiʿl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi distinction between takwin and mukawwan eliminates the need for aspects of action (jihāt al-fiʿl).

Core Idea
Creation is distinct from creature; therefore, created evil does not taint God's creative act.

The Maturidi school holds a distinctive position on divine action and moral attribution. They argue that the act of creating is distinct from the thing created, which has important consequences for how we understand evil and God's role. In this passage, Imām al-Nasafī explains this view and shows why it renders unnecessary the concept of 'aspects of action' found in other theological schools. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the key terms takwin and mukawwan.
2
Identify the core distinction
Notice that the passage begins by stating that takwin is other than mukawwan, and then applies this to disbelief (kufr). Identify how this distinction is used.
This is the foundational point.
3
Trace the consequence for ugliness
The passage argues that the ugliness of the effect does not imply ugliness of the cause, because of a praiseworthy outcome. See how this follows from the distinction.
4
Understand the elimination of jihāt al-fiʿl
Finally, the passage concludes that Maturidis have no need for 'aspects of action' because the distinction already handles the problem. Understand why this is so.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلموا - رحمكم الله - أن أصحابنا - رحمهم الله – لما كان من مذهبهم أن التكوين غير المكون والكفر مكون وتكوينه غيره فلم يكن هو عندنا فعل الله تعالى بل هو مفعوله وكون المفعول قبيحًا لا يوجب قبح التكوين إذا كانت فيه عاقبة حميدة، فلم يكن بنا على هذا المذهب حاجة إلى القول بجهات الفعل وأنه بجهة أنه فعل الله تعالى ليس بقبيح وبجهة أنه فعل العبد قبيح، وإنما الحاجة إلى ذلك لمن يزعم أن التكوين هو عين المكون.
And know - may God have mercy on you - that our companions - may God have mercy on them - since their doctrine is that creation (takwīn) is other than the created (mukawwan), and disbelief (kufr) is created and its creation is other than it, then it (kufr) is not in our view an act (fiʿl) of God Almighty, but rather His effect (mafʿūl). And the fact that the effect is ugly does not necessitate the ugliness of the creation if there is in it a praiseworthy outcome. So we, according to this doctrine, have no need to speak of aspects of action (jihāt al-fiʿl) - that it is not ugly by virtue of being God's act, and ugly by virtue of being the servant's act. Rather, the need for that is only for one who claims that creation is identical to the created.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage states the Maturidi position on a key theological distinction. The author begins by addressing the audience with an appeal to divine mercy, then explains the Maturidi doctrine: creation (takwīn) is distinct from the created being (mukawwan). Disbelief (kufr) is a created thing, and the act of creating it is other than the disbelief itself. Therefore, disbelief is not an act of God but an effect (mafʿūl) brought about by God's creating act. The ugliness of the effect does not entail ugliness in the act of creation because the creation has a praiseworthy outcome (e.g., testing or just punishment). Consequently, Maturidis do not need to posit 'aspects of action' (jihāt al-fiʿl) - the idea that an action is ugly from one perspective (servant's acquisition) and not ugly from another (God's creation). This need only arises for those who identify the act of creation with the created thing, such as the Ashʿarites or others. The passage thus distinguishes the Maturidi school from others on the issue of divine action and moral attribution.

Key Terms
تكوين
مكون
فعل
مفعول
جهات الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidis deny that God creates evil.
Correction: They distinguish between God's creative act (takwin) and the created evil (mukawwan); God creates evil in the sense of bringing it into existence, but the act of creation itself is not evil.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidis, why is there no need to speak of 'aspects of action' (jihāt al-fiʿl)?
A) Because God does not create evil
B) Because takwin and mukawwan are identical
C) Because takwin is distinct from mukawwan, so the ugliness of the effect does not affect the act
D) Because actions have no moral qualities

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi distinction between creation and creature resolves the problem of evil without appealing to aspects of action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials or see evil in the world, do you instinctively attribute that evil to God as if He directly did something bad? How does the distinction between God's act of creation and the created thing change how you understand His role in your trials?
?
Can you recall a specific situation where you blamed God for something that happened? In light of this lesson, can you separate God's perfect act of creating from the imperfect created reality, and see a praiseworthy outcome in that situation?
?
If you truly believe that God's act of creation is always good, how should that affect your response to personal suffering? Are you willing to accept that the ugliness you experience may be part of a praiseworthy outcome, even if you cannot see it now?

Essential vs. Relational Attributes
Explains why essential attributes are invariant while relational attributes vary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between attributes that are essential to a being and those that depend on relations, and why only the latter can vary.

Core Idea
Essential attributes are fixed; relational attributes change with relations.

In this passage, the author provides a logical principle for distinguishing attributes that are intrinsic from those that are relative. He explains that what is deserved by essence does not change because its cause (the essence) is constant, while what is deserved by relation varies because the relation changes. This principle is key to understanding how certain attributes like obedience or wisdom are not inherent to a being but arise from external connections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall structure. Then read the English translation to understand the logical argument.
Notice the cause-effect language used throughout.
2
Identify the two types of attributes
Find where the author contrasts 'what is deserved by essence' (استحقاق لذاته) and 'what is deserved by relation' (استحقاق بالمضايفة). Note how each is defined and why one is invariant and the other variant.
3
Apply the analogy
The author gives the analogy of family relations like father and son. Reflect on how such terms depend on perspective, reinforcing the concept of relational attributes.
This helps internalize the abstract principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يختلف ما استحقه لذاته لأن علته لم تختلف فلا يختلف المعلول، وما كان استحقاقه باختلاف ما يضايقه يختلف باختلاف المتضايف، لأن علته تختلف فيختلف المعلول، وما أثبتنا اختلاف الجهات فيه كان من الأوصاف الثابتة باعتبار الإضافة كالطاعة والمعصية والحكمة والسفه والشر والخير وأشباه ذلك، فكان ذلك نظير الأب والابن والعم والخال وأشباه ذلك، والله الموفق.
And what is deserved by essence does not differ because its cause does not differ, so the effect does not differ; and what is deserved by differing relations differs with differing relations, because its cause differs so the effect differs. And what we have established of differing aspects in it was from the attributes established by way of relation, like obedience and disobedience, wisdom and foolishness, evil and good, and the like of that. So that became similar to father, son, paternal uncle, maternal uncle, and the like of that. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a logical explanation of the difference between attributes that are essential and those that are relational. The author begins by stating a principle: that which is deserved by virtue of essence (استحقاق لذاته) does not vary, because the cause (the essence) does not change, so the effect (the attribute) does not change. Then he states the opposite: that which is deserved by virtue of a relation (استحقاق بالمضايفة) varies with the relation, because the cause (the relation) changes, so the effect changes. He then applies this to the theological context: the attributes for which differing aspects (جهات) have been established fall under the second category; they are attributes established by relation, such as obedience/disobedience, wisdom/foolishness, evil/good. These are not essential to the being but depend on an external relation. He gives an analogy: just as the terms 'father' and 'son' are relative and change with the perspective, so too these attributes are relative. The passage concludes with a prayer for divine assistance.

Key Terms
استحقاق لذاته
استحقاق بالمضايفة
أوصاف ثابتة باعتبار الإضافة
الطاعة والمعصية
الحكمة والسفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All attributes of God are equally essential and unchanging.
Correction: This passage shows that some attributes are relational and do not imply change in the essence, because they depend on external relations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do attributes that are based on relation (باعتبار الإضافة) vary?
A) Because the essence changes
B) Because the cause (the relation) varies
C) Because they are not real attributes
D) Because God's will changes

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes are invariant because they arise directly from the nature of the being, while relational attributes vary because they depend on external relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often describe God as ‘pleased’ or ‘angry’ based on your actions. But this lesson teaches that such attributes are relational, not essential. Does your fear of God’s anger pass when your circumstances improve, or do you anchor your reverence in the unchanging perfection of His essence? Where is your heart’s security really placed?
?
Consider a recent sin you committed. Did you assume God’s displeasure meant He had changed toward you? This passage insists His essence does not change—it is your relation that shifted. Will you now seek to restore that relation without doubting His essential goodness?
?
If relational attributes like ‘wisdom’ or ‘goodness’ can be ascribed differently depending on context, then your own actions are not identical to God’s essence. How does that truth humble you today? What attribute of God that you thought you possessed are you now required to seek only from Him?

Refuting Ash'ari Denial of Aspects
Refutes Ash'ari's denial of 'aspects of action' showing it is merely verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Ash'ari's denial of 'aspects of action' is a verbal disagreement rather than a substantive theological difference.

Core Idea
The dispute over aspects is about words, not reality.

In this passage, the author addresses a key point of contention between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools. Al-Ash'ari denied that actions have distinct 'aspects' (جهات) from which they can be considered. The author argues that this denial is merely verbal because the Maturidis use 'aspect' to mean different descriptive categories, not physical directions. Understanding this helps resolve a seeming theological conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice the flow: first al-Ash'ari's position, then the Maturidi response.
2
Identify the two positions
Contrast al-Ash'ari's denial (no aspects for actions) with the Maturidi affirmation (aspects are descriptive categories). Understand the point of disagreement.
The Maturidis do not mean physical aspects like bodies.
3
Analyze the key phrase 'denial from wording'
The author concludes that the denial is 'from the standpoint of wording, not the reality of meaning.' Reflect on why this is a significant resolution.
It means the disagreement is terminological, not doctrinal.
4
Examine the supporting quote from al-Ash'ari
The author cites al-Ash'ari's own statement about disbelief being false 'not because it is a proof.' See how this implicitly acknowledges aspects.
This strengthens the argument that the denial is baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲) والأشعري وإن كان يجعل الخلق والمخلوق واحدًا لا يقول بجهات الفعل، ويقول : إن قول القائل أن الإنسان يعذب عليه من جهة كذا ولا يعذب عليه من جهة كذا كلام مستحيل متناقض، لأنه لا جهات للفعل قال وحقيقة الجواب عندنا أن الله تعالى يعذب على الكفر الذي هو خلق وعلى الخلق الذي هو كفر لا لأنه خلق وقول القائل : من جهة كذا ومن جهة كذا فاسد، وهذا في الحقيقة ما يقوله من يقول بجهات الفعل لما مر أنهم لا يريدون بذلك إثبات جهات كجهات الأجسام إنما يريدون أن الفعل يوصف بأنه كفر ويوصف بأنه خلق ويوصف بأنه شيء وبأنه عرض وبأنه اعتقاد وبأنه محدث، ولا يعذب عليه إلا أنه كفر، ولا يعذب لأنه عرض أو اعتقاد أو شيء أو محدث، فلا معنى لهذا الإنكار إذ هو إنكار من حيث اللفظ دون حقيقة المعنى، ولا مشاحة في العبارات. وأطلق الأشعري ذلك أيضًا فقال : الكفر الذي هو حجة الله باطل لا من حيث كان حجة، فدل أن لا وجه لإنكاره.
(2) And al-Ash'ari, although he makes creation and the created one, does not affirm aspects of action, and says: The statement of the speaker that 'a person is punished from one aspect and not punished from another aspect' is impossible and contradictory, because there are no aspects for the action. He said: And the real answer according to us is that God most high punishes for the disbelief which is creation and for the creation which is disbelief, not because it is creation; and the statement of the speaker 'from one aspect and another aspect' is invalid. And this is in reality what the one who affirms aspects of action says, because it has passed that they do not intend by that the affirmation of aspects like the aspects of bodies; rather they intend that the action is described as disbelief, described as creation, described as a thing, as an accident, as a belief, as an originated thing; and he is not punished except for it being disbelief, and he is not punished because it is an accident or belief or thing or originated. So there is no meaning to this denial, since it is denial in terms of wording without the reality of meaning, and there is no contention over expressions. And al-Ash'ari also stated that saying: 'Disbelief which is God's proof is false, not because it is a proof.' So it indicates that there is no basis for its denial.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Imam al-Ash'ari's denial of 'aspects of action' (جهات الفعل). The author first presents al-Ash'ari's position: he equates creation and the created, denies that actions have distinct aspects, and considers it contradictory to say a person is punished from one aspect and not from another. Then the author presents the real answer: God punishes for the disbelief which is also creation, but not because it is creation per se, but because it is disbelief. The statement 'from one aspect' etc. is thus not invalid in meaning. The author explains that those who affirm aspects do not mean physical aspects like those of bodies; rather, they mean that the action can be described in multiple ways (as disbelief, creation, accident, etc.) and the punishment is attached only to certain descriptors. Therefore, al-Ash'ari's denial is merely verbal—a dispute over wording, not over reality. The author further supports this by citing al-Ash'ari's own statement that disbelief (which is God's proof) is false, not because it is a proof. This shows that al-Ash'ari himself implicitly acknowledges that the same thing can be considered from different aspects. Thus the denial is baseless.

Key Terms
جهات الفعل
الخلق والمخلوق واحد
الكفر الذي هو حجة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari and Maturidi positions on this issue are fundamentally different in substance.
Correction: The author shows it is a verbal difference—both schools agree on the underlying reality; the disagreement is only about the terminology 'aspects'.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about al-Ash'ari's denial of aspects (جهات الفعل)?
A) It is a serious theological error
B) It is merely a verbal disagreement
C) It contradicts the Quran
D) It is based on a misunderstanding of logic

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari denial of aspects is a verbal dispute, as the Maturidis use 'aspect' to refer to different descriptions of an action, not physical dimensions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the dispute over aspects is a verbal one, and that punishment is attached to disbelief, not to the act's creation. Are there areas of your life where you punish yourself for the 'wrong aspect'—for example, blaming your nature for a sin instead of your will? Will you today identify one sin and repent for the disbelief in it, not for the fact that it was created by Allah?
?
The passage shows even respected scholars like al-Ash'ari can be inconsistent, saying one thing and implying another. When have you held a belief that, on reflection, contradicts your own deeper convictions? Are you willing to admit that inconsistency and resolve it, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
The author says 'there is no contention over expressions.' Think of a recent argument you had about religion or practice that was really about words, not about the heart of the matter. Can you let go of that dispute today and focus on the truth that both sides actually agree upon?

Refuting the Charge of Shirk: The Analogy of Ownership
Refutation of the Mu'tazila claim using the ownership analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi view of acquisition (kash) does not imply shirk, as clarified by the analogy of dual ownership.

Core Idea
An act can be within both Allah's and the servant's power without partnership.

One of the most frequent objections to the Ash'ari/Maturidi position on human agency is that it makes the servant a partner in creation. The Mu'tazila argued that if the servant originates an act and Allah has no power over it, then the two are partners. In this passage, the author refutes that charge by using a simple and compelling analogy from everyday life: ownership.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Read the first part of the passage where the author presents the opposing view. Notice that it claims the Maturidi position leads to partnership in creating the world.
This is the charge that must be refuted.
2
See the author's refutation
The author responds by stating that in the Maturidi view, the same act falls under the power of both Allah and the servant, but in different ways. This does not constitute partnership.
Key phrase: 'by His empowering (iqdar)'.
3
Analyze the analogy of ownership
The author draws a parallel: a servant's property is also Allah's property, yet no rational person calls that partnership. Similarly, the act's being within both powers does not imply shirk.
This analogy is meant to show the absurdity of the Mu'tazila conclusion.
4
Reflect on the consensus claim
Notice the author asserts consensus of rational people that ownership by both does not entail partnership. This strengthens the argument.
This is a call to common sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفعل يخترعه العبد لا قدرة الله تعالى عليه وحصول الفعل بمجموع مقدوريهما، فكان الأحياء شركاء الله تعالى في تخليق العالم ، وكل حي من كل فعل يفعله شريك الله تعالى. فأما ما قلنا فلا يوجب الشركة إذ عين ما هو مقدور الله تعالى مقدور العبد بإقداره، وما هو مقدور العبد مقدور الله تعالى، وهذا لا يعقل شركة كما في الملك إذ هو ما ملك العبد، عينه ملك الله وما هو ملك الله تعالى من الأعيان التي جعلها ملكًا لعباده عينه ملك العبد ولم يعد ذلك شركة في الملك بإجماع العقلاء.
And a deed that the servant originates, Allah Almighty has no power over it, and the occurrence of the deed is by the combination of the two powers, then the living beings would be partners of Allah Almighty in creating the world, and every living being from every deed it does is a partner of Allah Almighty. As for what we say, it does not necessitate partnership, because the very thing that is within the power of Allah Almighty is within the power of the servant by His empowering, and what is within the power of the servant is within the power of Allah Almighty, and this is not intelligible as partnership, just as in ownership: for what the servant owns, the very same thing is owned by Allah, and what belongs to Allah Almighty among the entities that He made as property for His servants, the very same thing is owned by the servant, and that is not considered partnership in ownership by consensus of the rational people.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that the Maturidi/Ash'ari view (that the act is created by Allah but acquired by the servant) implies shirk (partnership) by making the servant a partner in creation. The author first states the Mu'tazila argument: if the servant originates an act that Allah has no power over, and the act occurs by the combination of the two powers, then living beings would be partners in creation. Then he responds: our view does not entail partnership because the very act that is within Allah's power is also within the servant's power by Allah's empowering (iqdar), and vice versa. This is not partnership, analogous to ownership (milk): a servant owns something but it is also owned by Allah, and no one calls that partnership. This is agreed upon by all rational people.

Key Terms
الشركة
المقدور
الإقدار
الملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi view of kash makes the servant a partner in creation with Allah.
Correction: The act is within both powers but in different ways: Allah creates, the servant acquires by Allah's empowering. Analogy: ownership does not become partnership just because both own.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to refute the claim that the Maturidi view implies partnership?
A) The analogy of a king and his subjects.
B) The analogy of ownership (milk).
C) The analogy of light and darkness.
D) The analogy of fire and burning.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi view avoids shirk because an act can be within both divine and human power without partnership, just as property is owned by both without it being partnership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you habitually act as if you are the independent creator of your own success, forgetting that every ability, every breath, is from Allah? What specific step will you take today to consciously acknowledge His empowering?
?
When you look at your possessions—your home, your wealth, your skills—do you truly see yourself as a trustee of Allah's property, or do you feel a sense of ultimate ownership? If you internalized this lesson, how would it change your attitude toward sharing, charity, and letting go?
?
The Mu'tazila were concerned about protecting tawhid, yet they fell into a misunderstanding that denied Allah's complete control. Are there areas in your faith where you overcorrect—where you are so afraid of shirk that you end up limiting Allah's attributes? Where do you need to restore balance and trust in the sound understanding of the scholars?

God's Knowledge Does Not Compel Human Actions
Nasafi refutes the claim that God's foreknowledge compels human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's foreknowledge does not compel human actions, as argued by Imam Nasafi using the analogy of God's own knowledge.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of our actions does not make them compelled—just as His own knowledge does not compel Him.

This passage tackles a common objection in theology: if God knows everything we will do, are we really free? Imam Nasafi refutes this by drawing a powerful analogy: God knows His own actions yet is not compelled, so His knowledge of our actions likewise leaves our free will intact.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Start by reading the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow: an objection about human inability to escape God's creation, and Nasafi's response using divine self-awareness.
Notice the rhetorical structure: 'If they claim... we say...'
2
Follow the divine analogy
Nasafi argues that God knows what He does but is not compelled. If knowledge compelled, God would be compelled—an impossibility. See how he applies this to the servant.
Key point: God's knowledge is of actions as chosen, not as compelled.
3
Understand the impossibility of ignorance
If the servant were compelled, God's knowledge would be false, implying ignorance—impossible for God. This refutes the objection.
4
Extend the analogy to creation
Nasafi says creating an action does not compel the agent, just as creating the heavens does not compel them. So disbelief and natural objects are alike in not forcing the servant.
This ties back to the broader Maturidi position on kasb (acquisition).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The opponent's view leads to God being compelled, which is impossible. Therefore, human free will remains intact despite divine foreknowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباري مضطرًا في تخليقه، فإن زعموا أن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عما خلقه فكيف يكون مختارا؟ قلنا : العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن معلوم الباري، فكيف يكون مختارا والباري لا يخرج أيضا عن معلومه فكيف يكون مختارا؟ وجاء من هذا أنه تعالى لما لم يكن في فعله مضطرًا وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه لأنه وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه فمعلومه أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره فلو صار بعلمه مضطرًا لانقلب جاهلاً حيث علم أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره، فلم يفعل بل فعل مضطرًا فكذا عبده لا يصير مضطرًا بعلمه وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه لأن معلومه تعالى أن العبد يفعل ما يفعل باختياره غير مضطر، فلو صار مضطرًا لانقلب علم الباري جهلاً وذلك محال، فبقى العبد مختارا فكذا لما خلق بعلمه الاختياري يبقى مختاراً؛ إذ لو انقلب بذلك مضطرًا لم يحصل ما خلق على ما خلق بل على غير ما خلق وهو أمارة اضطراره، فكان القول بما قالوا موجبا كون فعل الله تعالى بالاضطرار وهذا محال، وإذا لم يصر العبد بتخليق فعله مضطرًا في فعله كما لم يصر بتخليق السماوات والأرض فكان خلق كفره وخلق السماوات والأرضين والجبال والبحار سواء من أن لا يوجب اضطرار العبد، والله الموفق.
The Creator is compelled in His creation? If they claim that the servant cannot exit from what He created, then how can he be free? We say: The servant cannot exit from the known of the Creator, so how can he be free? And the Creator also does not exit from His known, so how can He be free? And it follows from this that He, exalted, was not compelled in His act, even though He does not exit from His known, because even though He does not exit from His known, His known is that He does what He does by His choice. If He were compelled by His knowledge, He would become ignorant, since He knows that He does what He does by choice, but He did not do it, rather He did it compelled. So likewise His servant is not compelled by His knowledge, even though he does not exit from His known, because the known of the Exalted is that the servant does what he does by his choice, not compelled. If he were compelled, the knowledge of the Creator would become ignorance, and that is impossible. So the servant remains free. So likewise, when He created him with His knowledge, the free one remains free; for if he were thereby turned into compelled, what He created would not occur as He created it, but contrary to what He created, and that is a sign of his compulsion. So the statement of what they said necessitates that the act of God, exalted, be by compulsion, and that is impossible. And if the servant does not become compelled by the creation of his act, just as he does not become compelled by the creation of the heavens and the earth, then the creation of his disbelief and the creation of the heavens and earths and mountains and seas are equal in that they do not necessitate the compulsion of the servant. And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that divine foreknowledge compels human actions. The objection states: if the servant cannot escape what God creates, how can he be free? The author responds by drawing an analogy with God Himself: God knows what He will do, yet He is not compelled by His knowledge. If God's knowledge compelled actions, then God would be compelled, which is absurd (since He knows He acts by choice). Similarly, God's knowledge of the servant's actions does not compel the servant; otherwise God would know something false (that the servant acts by choice) but the servant would be compelled, making God's knowledge ignorance. The author then argues that creating an action does not make the agent compelled, just as creating the heavens does not compel them. Therefore, the opponent's view leads to attributing compulsion to God, which is impossible. The passage concludes by affirming that the creation of disbelief and the creation of natural objects are alike in not compelling the servant.

Key Terms
مضطر (compelled)
اختيار (choice)
معلوم (known)
علم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's foreknowledge of a human action makes that action compelled, negating human free will.
Correction: God's knowledge does not cause compulsion; it simply knows what the agent freely chooses. If knowledge compelled, God would be compelled by His own knowledge, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to show that God's knowledge does not compel human actions?
A) The analogy of God creating the heavens and the earth without compulsion.
B) The analogy of God Himself: He knows His own actions but is not compelled by that knowledge.
C) The analogy of a human being who knows his own intentions.
D) The analogy of the sun giving light without choice.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of human actions does not compel them; otherwise God would be compelled, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you ever tempted to use God's foreknowledge as an excuse for your sins, thinking 'I couldn't help it'? How does this passage challenge that tendency, and what will you do when that thought arises today?
?
If you truly believe that God's knowledge of your choices does not compel them, how should that change the way you approach a habitual sin you feel trapped in? What concrete step will you take this week to assert your free will?
?
Reflect on a time you blamed circumstances or 'fate' for a wrong decision. Does this teaching free you from that blame? In light of this passage, what will you say to yourself the next time you are tempted to shift responsibility?

Nasafi's Refutation of the Mu'tazila on the Appointed Term
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila claim on the appointed term and shows agreement on God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's response to the Mu'tazila's objection that killing does not affect the appointed term, and how he argues that both sides agree on God's exhaustive knowledge.

Core Idea
Even in killing, God's appointed term holds, and His knowledge covers all possibilities.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a Mu'tazila argument that if a person dies at his appointed term, then retaliatory execution and indemnity would not apply, because the killer's act would not be the cause of death. Nasafi provides a nuanced answer and then shows that both schools actually agree on the scope of God's knowledge. Let's read his argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Start by reading the Mu'tazila objection attributed to al-Farra': if the death is at its appointed term, why would qisas and daman be obligatory? Consider the example of slaughtering a sheep.
This objection sets up the problem.
2
Understand Nasafi's Answer
Nasafi responds that the obligation comes from the killer's acquisition of the act that God customarily creates death after, not from a meaning contrary to life in the victim.
This ties into the theory of 'tawallud' (generation).
3
Follow the Verification Argument
The fact that qisas and daman are obligatory, when they would not be if the outcome were doubtful, shows that the killing is indeed the cause of death.
This logical step strengthens the answer.
4
See the Reconciliation
Nasafi then clarifies that there is no real disagreement: both the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna affirm that God knows all actual and hypothetical outcomes. The dispute is only apparent.
The key is God's knowledge of counterfactuals.
5
Reflect on the Core Issue
Consider why this matters: it preserves divine omniscience while explaining moral and legal responsibility for killing.
This connects to broader theological topics (qadar).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منهم الفراء ، ويقولون : لو كان مات بأجله لم يجب القصاص ولا وجب الضمان إذا كان شاه فذبحها لأنه أحسن إليه ، والجواب : أن وجوب القصاص والضمان لارتكابه النهي باكتسابه الفعل الذي أجرى الله تعالى العادة بتخليقه الموت عقبه لا على ما وجد في المحل من المعنى المنافي للحياة على ما مر في مسألة التولد. يحققه أن عندهم كان من الجائز أن يكون هذا أجله إن لم يكن واجبًا، والضمان لا يجب مع الشك ولا القصاص، ومع وجب هاهنا دل أنه إنما وجب ذلك لما ذكرنا ثم الحقيقة أنه لا خلاف في هذه المسألة لأنهم يقولون: إن معلوم الله تعالى في انتهاء عمره غير هذا إذ لا يظن هذا بمن خالف هشام بن عمرو من تجويز كون المعدوم معلومًا، وإنما يقولون : إن الله تعالى يعلم أنه يقتل لا محالة ويعلم أنه لو لم يقتل لعاش إلى وقت كذا على الأصل أنه تعالى يعلم ما يكون، ويعلم أن ما لا يكون لو كان كيف كان يكون على ما قال في حق الكفار :: { وَلَوْ رُدُّوا لَعَادُوا لِمَا نُهُوا عَنْهُ } وإن كان يعلم أنهم لا يردون ونحن ننكر هذا وإنما ننكر كون ذلك مشكوكاً مجهولاً عنده كما يكون ممن يجهل العواقب وهم أيضا يأبون هذا والله الموفق.
Among them is al-Farra', and they say: If he had died at his appointed term, retaliatory execution would not be obligatory nor would indemnity be obligatory if he slaughtered a sheep because he did it as a kindness. The answer: The obligation of retaliatory execution and indemnity is due to his committing the prohibition by his acquisition of the act which God Almighty made the custom to create death after, not due to what existed in the locus of the meaning contrary to life, as has passed in the issue of generation (tawallud). This is verified by the fact that according to them it would have been possible for this to be his appointed term if it were not obligatory, and indemnity is not obligatory with doubt, nor is retaliatory execution; and when it is obligatory here, it indicates that it was only obligatory for what we mentioned. Then the truth is that there is no disagreement in this issue because they say: Indeed, what God Almighty knows concerning the end of his life is other than this, for this is not thought of anyone who opposed Hisham ibn 'Amr regarding the possibility of the non-existent being known. Rather, they say: God Almighty knows that he will inevitably be killed, and He knows that if he had not been killed he would have lived until such-and-such a time, on the principle that He Almighty knows what will be, and He knows that what will not be, if it were, how it would be, as He said concerning the disbelievers: {And if they were returned, they would return to what they were forbidden} – even though He knows that they will not be returned. And we deny this; we only deny that it is doubtful and unknown to Him, as is the case with one who is ignorant of outcomes. And they too refuse this. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a clarification. It begins with the Mu'tazila objection: If the killed person had died at his natural appointed term, then retaliatory execution (qisas) and indemnity (daman) would not be obligatory, because the act of killing would not be the cause of death. For example, if someone slaughters a sheep, he is actually doing it a kindness if its natural term has come. The answer is that the obligation of qisas and daman is due to the killer committing the prohibition by acquiring the act that God customarily creates death after, not because death was caused by a meaning contrary to life in the victim (as in the theory of generation). The author then explains that according to the Mu'tazila's own principles, this could have been the person's appointed term if it were not obligatory for him to die at that time, but since qisas and daman are obligatory, it shows that the killing was the cause. Then Nasafi clarifies that there is no real disagreement: both sides agree that God knows everything, including what would have happened if the person had not been killed. The Mu'tazila say that God knows that if he had not been killed he would have lived longer, just as God knows that disbelievers would return to sin if they were returned. The Ahl al-Sunna also affirm God's knowledge, but they deny that the outcome is doubtful or unknown to God. The core of the issue is that both groups affirm that God knows all possibilities, so the dispute is only apparent.

Key Terms
أجل
قصاص
ضمان
معلوم
مشكوك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna fundamentally disagree on whether a killed person dies at his appointed term.
Correction: Nasafi shows that both affirm God's knowledge of all possibilities, including counterfactuals, so the disagreement is only apparent.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why are retaliatory execution and indemnity obligatory even if the killed person died at his appointed term?
A) Because the killer intended to harm
B) Because the killer acquired the act that God customarily creates death after
C) Because the victim's life was cut short
D) Because God decreed the killing before creation

Key Takeaway
The obligation of qisas and daman does not contradict the appointed term; rather, God's knowledge encompasses both actual and hypothetical outcomes, harmonizing the apparent dispute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that the exact moment of your death is fixed and known to God, would you still rush to save yourself from danger? Would your heart be at peace with His decree, or does your anxiety reveal a hidden lack of trust? Reflect on a time you feared death—does that fear align with this lesson's teaching that God's knowledge encompasses all possibilities, or does it show you rely on your own planning? Commit today to surrendering your life’s timeline to Allah, trusting that even if you avoid a situation, He knows what would have happened.
?
When you sin, do you ever tell yourself that it doesn’t matter because Allah already knew you would do it? This lesson shows that while Allah knows all, you are still accountable for your acquisition of the act. How does that change your view of personal responsibility? Think of a sin you rationalized. Will you now repent and take ownership, knowing that your choice is real and you are blamed for it, even though God’s knowledge is complete?

Definition of Provision and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Nasafi defines rizq and argues that haram food is not rizq in the sense of provision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that rizq (provision) has two distinct meanings—ownership and nourishment—and that the unlawful is not considered provision in the sense of ownership based on Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Provision is measured sustenance, and the unlawful is not provision for the wrongdoer.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi lays out the Ahl al-Sunna position on provision (rizq) and refutes the Mu'tazila who argue that a person may not eat his own provision and may eat another's. He begins by defining rizq linguistically, showing it has two meanings, and then argues, using Quranic verses, that the unlawful cannot be considered provision in the sense of ownership. This lesson will clarify the correct understanding of rizq and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for flow, then read the full translation. Pay attention to the positions of Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila.
2
Understand the linguistic definition of rizq
Note that rizq in language means measured sustenance (al-qut al-muqaddar). The author gives an example of stipends for frontier guards to illustrate this.
3
Identify the two meanings of rizq
The passage explains that rizq can mean absolute ownership (al-milk al-mutlaq) or nourishment (al-ghidha'). See how each meaning affects whether haram is considered rizq.
This distinction is key to the argument.
4
Analyze the Quranic proof from spending verses
The author cites two verses. Read the commentary to understand how they prove that the unlawful is not provision for the wrongdoer in terms of ownership and that believers are praised for spending from what Allah provided.
5
Review the conclusion
Synthesize: Haram food is nourishment (Allah creates the growth), but it is not provision in the sense of ownership, so it cannot be spent in obedience. The Mu'tazila are refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يماثل هذه المسألة مسألة الأرزاق ثم عندنا كل يستوفى رزق نفسه ويأكله حلالاً كان أو حرامًا ولا يتصور أن لا يأكل إنسان ما جعل رزقا له ولا أن يأكل غيره رزقه أو يأكل هو رزق غيره، وقالت المعتزلة: من الجائز أن لا يأكل رزقه ويأكل رزق غيره ويأكل رزقه غيره، والرزق في اللغة اسم للقوت المقدر، ولهذا يسمى من يجري عليه السلطان من المرابطين في كل شهر مقدار مرتزقة، وقد يستعمل ويراد به الملك المطلق، وقد يستعمل ويراد به الغذاء. قال الله تعالى: { وَمَا مِن دَابَّةٍ فِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَّا عَلَى اللهِ رِزْقُهَا } والدواب لا ملك لها بالأسباب المشروعة، فكان المراد منه ما يحصل لها به الاغتذاء فإن حمل على الملك لم يكن الحرام رزقا والإنسان قد يأكل رزق غيره أي ملكه ويأكل غيره رزقه أي ملكه، وإن حمل على الغذاء كان الحرام رزقًا لأن الله تعالى يغذينا أي يخلق التغذي والنمو في أبداننا، وهو تعالى يخلقه متفرد لا صنع للعبد فيه، ومن المحال إطلاق اسم الرزق على الملك خاصة دون الغذاء بل هو يقع عليها جميعًا ثم المحرم تناوله لا يجوز أن يكون رزقًا للظالم يدل على ذلك قول الله تعالى: { أَنفِقُوا مِمَّا رَزَقْنَاكُم } فلو كان المحرم رزقا لمن تناوله لكان مأمورًا بالإنفاق منه إما على جهة الندب أو الإباحة أو الوجوب، وفي علمنا بأنه منهي عن ذلك دلالة ما قلناه. وقوله تعالى: { الَّذِينَ يُؤْمِنُونَ بِالْغَيْبِ وَيُقِيمُونَ الصَّلَاةَ وَمَا رَزَقْنَاهُمْ يُنفِقُونَ } يدل على ذلك من وجهين: أحدهما أنهم مدحوا بأنهم ينفقون مما رزقوا فلو كان الحرام رزقا لكان ممدوحا بالإنفاق منه على بعض الوجوه وفي كونه مذموماً على ذلك دلالة على أنه ليس برزق له. والثاني: أنه تعالى قال: { وَمَا رَزَقْنَاهُمْ يُنفِقُونَ } فلولا أن هناك شيء ينفق لم يرزقوه لم يكن لهذا القول معنى، وهذا يبين أن المنفق من المحرمات لم يكن منفقا مما رزقه الله وقد جعل الله تعالى الإنفاق مما رزقه المؤمن من صفات المؤمنين. راجع المغني لعبد الجبار جـ 11 ص 35.
And similar to this issue is the issue of provisions. According to us, everyone consumes his own provision and eats it, whether lawful or unlawful, and it is inconceivable that a person does not eat what is made a provision for him, or that another eats his provision, or that he eats another's provision. The Mu'tazila said: It is possible that one does not eat his provision and eats another's provision, and another eats his provision. Provision (al-rizq) in the language is a name for the measured sustenance. For this reason, those upon whom the ruler disburses among the frontier guards every month are called 'murtaziqa' (stipendaries). It is sometimes used to mean absolute ownership, and sometimes used to mean nourishment. God Almighty said: {There is no creature on earth except that upon God is its provision}. And creatures do not have ownership by legal means, so what is meant is what makes nourishment possible for them. If it is interpreted as ownership, then the unlawful is not provision; a person may eat another's provision, i.e., his ownership, and another may eat his provision, i.e., his ownership. If it is interpreted as nourishment, then the unlawful is provision because God Almighty nourishes us, i.e., He creates nourishment and growth in our bodies, and He alone creates it, no act of the servant in it. And it is impossible to apply the term provision only to ownership without nourishment; rather, it applies to both. Then: That which is unlawful to consume cannot be a provision for the wrongdoer. This is indicated by God Almighty's saying: {Spend from what We have provided you}. For if the unlawful were a provision for the one who consumes it, he would be commanded to spend from it, either by way of recommendation, permission, or obligation. And our knowledge that he is prohibited from that is evidence for what we said. And His saying: {Those who believe in the unseen, establish prayer, and spend from what We have provided them} indicates this in two ways: First, they are praised for spending from what they were provided. So if the unlawful were a provision, they would be praised for spending from it in some respects, but they are blamed for that, indicating that it is not a provision for them. Second, He said: {and spend from what We have provided them}. Were it not that there is something they spend that they were not provided, this statement would have no meaning. And this shows that the one who spends from unlawfully gained goods is not spending from what God provided him. And God Almighty has made spending from what He provided the believer a characteristic of the believers. Refer to al-Mughni by 'Abd al-Jabbar, vol. 11, p. 35.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argumentation about provision (rizq). It begins by stating the Sunni position: each person consumes his own provision, whether lawful or unlawful; it is impossible for a person not to eat his provision or to eat another's. The Mu'tazila hold that it is possible for a person not to eat his provision and to eat another's. The author then defines 'rizq' linguistically as measured sustenance (qut muqaddar), citing the usage for stipends. It has two meanings: absolute ownership (milk mutlaq) and nourishment (ghidha'). The verse about all creatures having their provision from God shows that the intended meaning is nourishment, since animals do not own property. If taken as ownership, the unlawful is not provision; a person can eat another's property. If taken as nourishment, the unlawful is provision because God creates nourishment. The author then argues that it is impossible to restrict rizq to ownership only; it applies to both. The second part (from the footnote) provides a proof that the unlawful is not provision. Using the verse 'Spend from what We have provided you', if the unlawful were provision, one would be commanded to spend from it, but we know it is prohibited. Another verse praises believers for spending from what they are provided; if the unlawful were provision, they would be praised for spending from it, but they are blamed. Also, the phrase 'from what We have provided them' implies that there is something they spend that is not provided by God, showing that unlawful wealth is not from God's provision.

Key Terms
رزق
قوت
ملك
غذاء
مرتزقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that provision (rizq) refers only to lawful sustenance, or that the Mu'tazila are simply wrong about the possibility of eating another's provision without understanding the definition.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that rizq has two valid meanings (ownership and nourishment). The dispute is about which meaning is used when. The Mu'tazila's position is based on a different understanding of how provision is assigned. The Sunnis affirm that everyone gets his own provision, but the unlawful is not provision in terms of ownership.

Checkpoint
What two meanings does Nasafi give for the word 'rizq'?
A) Possession and inheritance
B) Ownership and nourishment
C) Lawful and unlawful
D) Sustenance and charity

Key Takeaway
The unlawful is not provision (rizq) in the sense of ownership, but it is still nourishment created by God; however, the wrongdoer is not spending from God's provision when he spends unlawfully.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obtain wealth through methods you know are doubtful or forbidden, do you truly believe in your heart that it is not provision from Allah? How does this belief—or lack of it—affect your appetite for such wealth? What would it take for you to have certainty that it is not from Him, and to abandon it without hesitation?
?
The passage teaches that the wrongdoer who spends from unlawfully gained goods is not spending from what Allah provided him. Reflect on your own spending today. How much of what you earn and spend can you honestly attribute as provision from Allah? If you identified a portion that is not, would you be willing to give it up? What is stopping you?
?
The believers are praised for spending from what Allah provided them. Imagine standing before Allah and being asked about your spending. Would He recognize it as spending from His provision, or from what you took without His sanction? What one change can you make today to align your earning and spending with this teaching?

Allah's Will Encompasses All Things
Nasafi states the Sunni principle on divine will and contrasts with Mu'tazilite views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine that Allah's will encompasses all originated events, and how it differs from the Mu'tazilite view, particularly the Baghdadis' metaphorical interpretation.

Core Idea
Allah's will covers everything that happens, good or evil.

In this passage, Nasafi summarizes the Sunni position on divine will and directly contrasts it with the Mu'tazilite school. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Islamic theology of divine sovereignty and human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
2
Identify the Sunni principle
Notice the opening statement: every originated event occurs by Allah's will, regardless of moral value or category. This is the core Sunni claim.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite restriction
The Mu'tazila limit divine will to good and obedient actions. They deny that Allah wills evil or disobedience. Within them, the Baghdadis interpret will metaphorically.
Pay attention to the Baghdadi view: will for others means command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد سبق منا الكلام أن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة أزلية وأن المشيئة والإرادة لفظان ينبئان عن معنى واحد وهي صفة قائمة بذاته، وقد بينا الخلاف بيننا وبين طوائف المعتزلة فلا نعيد ونحتاج في هذا الباب إلى ذكر ما يصح دخوله تحت إرادة الله تعالى. فنقول : الأصل عندنا أن كل حادث حدث بإرادة الله تعالى خيرًا كان أو شرًا، حسنًا كان أو قبيحاً ، جوهرًا كان أو عرضاً ، وزعمت المعتزلة أن الله تعالى يريد من أفعالنا ما هو حكمة وطاعة ولا يريد ما هو معصية وقبيح، واختلفوا في المباحات فمنهم من زعم أن الله تعالى مريد لها، ومنهم من زعم أن الله تعالى غير مريد لها، وينبغي أن يكون هذا على قول البغداديين منهم فإنهم يزعمون أن الله لا يوصف بالإرادة في الحقيقة وإنما يوصف بها مجازا ، فما يقال إنه تعالى أراده فإن كان ذلك من أفعاله فمعناه أنه يفعله أو فعله، وما كان من أفعال غيره فالمراد منه أنه أمر به فلما كانت الإرادة عندهم أمرًا والمباح ليس بمأمور به فلا يكون مرادا
We have already discussed that Allah Most High is willing with an eternal will, and that al-mashī'ah (volition) and al-irādah (will) are two terms that signify one meaning, and it is an attribute subsisting in His essence. And we have explained the disagreement between us and the sects of the Mu'tazila, so we will not repeat. And in this section we need to mention what properly falls under the will of Allah Most High. So we say: The principle with us is that every originated thing occurs by the will of Allah Most High, whether good or evil, whether beautiful or ugly, whether substance or accident. And the Mu'tazila claimed that Allah Most High wills from our actions that which is wisdom and obedience, and does not will that which is disobedience and ugly. And they differed regarding permissible actions (mubāḥāt): some claimed that Allah Most High wills them, and some claimed that Allah Most High does not will them. And this should be according to the view of the Baghdadis among them, for they claim that Allah is not described as having will in reality, but is only described with it metaphorically. So when it is said that He willed something, if it is from His actions, its meaning is that He does it or did it; and if it is from the actions of others, its meaning is that He commanded it. Since for them will is a command, and the permissible is not commanded, it is not willed.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the Sunni doctrine of divine will, followed by a refutation of the Mu'tazilite view. The author begins by summarizing his earlier discussion that Allah is willing with an eternal will, and that the terms 'mashī'ah' and 'irādah' denote the same attribute subsisting in His essence. He then sets out the Sunni principle: every originated event, regardless of its moral or ontological category (good or evil, beautiful or ugly, substance or accident), occurs by Allah's will. He then contrasts this with the Mu'tazilite claim that Allah wills only wisdom and obedience from human actions, not disobedience or ugliness. Among the Mu'tazila, there is disagreement regarding permissible actions (mubāḥāt). The author attributes to the Baghdadis the view that Allah is not described with will in reality but only metaphorically. He explains their interpretation: when Allah is said to will an action, if it is His own act, it means He does it; if it is an act of another, it means He commands it. Since for them will is equivalent to command, and permissible actions are not commanded, they are not willed. This effectively refutes the Mu'tazilite restriction of divine will.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المشيئة
المعتزلة
البغداديون
المباحات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will only applies to good or religiously approved actions.
Correction: The Sunni position is that Allah's will encompasses all events, including evil and disobedience.

Checkpoint
According to the Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila, what does it mean when Allah is said to will an action from His servants?
A) He does it Himself.
B) He commands it.
C) He allows it.
D) He creates it.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position is that Allah's will encompasses all events, contrary to Mu'tazilite restrictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that Allah's will encompasses every event—including your moments of disobedience—does that lead you to take sin more lightly or to rely more on His mercy? What concrete step will you take this week to ensure your awareness of His will deepens your repentance, not your complacency?
?
The Mu'tazilites feared that attributing evil to Allah's will would compromise His justice. Have you ever struggled to accept that Allah wills something you consider unjust? How does this lesson challenge you to submit your own sense of fairness to His absolute wisdom? What will you do the next time life feels unfair?
?
If every originated thing occurs by Allah's will, then your very reading of this lesson is willed by Him. Does that thought fill you with awe or with indifference? What does your reaction reveal about your relationship with the Creator? Commit this week to saying 'Mā shā’ Allāh' (whatever Allah wills) genuinely when something undesirable happens, and observe how it changes your heart.

Every Event Occurs by God's Will
Al-Nasafi states the Sunni principle on divine will and refutes Mu'tazila views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni (Maturidi) principle that all events, good or evil, occur by God's will, and how this differs from the Mu'tazila view.

Core Idea
All events without exception occur by God's will.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi lays out the foundational Sunni principle about the scope of divine will. After clarifying that God's will is an eternal attribute, the author states that every occurring event, regardless of its moral or ontological status, happens by God's will. He then contrasts this with the Mu'tazila who restrict God's will to only good acts, and delves into their internal disagreements, especially the Baghdadis who reduce will to command. This passage clarifies a key distinction between Sunni and Mu'tazila theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully. Notice the structure: recap, principle, opposition, and conclusion.
Pay attention to the key sentence starting with 'الأصل عندنا'.
2
Identify the Sunni Principle
Locate the sentence that states the Sunni principle. Understand that it includes every event, regardless of moral or ontological category.
3
Contrast with Mu'tazila
Note how the Mu'tazila restrict divine will to only acts of wisdom and obedience, excluding disobedience. See also their disagreement on permissible acts.
The Baghdadis have a unique view on this.
4
Understand the Baghdadis' Position
Learn how the Baghdadis interpret 'will' metaphorically as command. Understand why this leads them to deny that God wills permissible acts.
This is a key distinction within the Mu'tazila.
5
Reflect on Implications
Consider why this theological difference matters for understanding God's sovereignty and human responsibility.
It affects how we view the relationship between God's will and human actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد سبق منا الكلام أن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة أزلية وأن المشيئة والإرادة لفظان ينبئان عن معنى واحد وهي صفة قائمة بذاته، وقد بينا الخلاف بيننا وبين طوائف المعتزلة فلا نعيد ونحتاج في هذا الباب إلى ذكر ما يصح دخوله تحت إرادة الله تعالى. إرادة الله شاملة لكل شيء: فنقول : الأصل عندنا أن كل حادث حدث بإرادة الله تعالى خيرًا كان أو شرًا، حسنًا كان أو قبيحاً ، جوهرًا كان أو عرضاً ، وزعمت المعتزلة أن الله تعالى يريد من أفعالنا ما هو حكمة وطاعة ولا يريد ما هو معصية وقبيح، واختلفوا في المباحات فمنهم من زعم أن الله تعالى مريد لها، ومنهم من زعم أن الله تعالى غير مريد لها، وينبغي أن يكون هذا على قول البغداديين منهم فإنهم يزعمون أن الله لا يوصف بالإرادة في الحقيقة وإنما يوصف بها مجازا ، فما يقال إنه تعالى أراده فإن كان ذلك من أفعاله فمعناه أنه يفعله أو فعله، وما كان من أفعال غيره فالمراد منه أنه أمر به فلما كانت الإرادة عندهم أمرًا والمباح ليس بمأمور به فلا يكون مرادا. والحاصل عندنا أن كل حادث حدث بإرادة الله تعالى على أي وصف كان
We have already spoken that God Most High is willing (murīd) with an eternal will (irāda azaliyya), and that voluntary choice (mashī'a) and will (irāda) are two terms that indicate one meaning, and it is an attribute subsisting in His essence. And we have already explained the disagreement between us and the groups of the Mu'tazila, so we will not repeat. And we need in this chapter to mention what properly enters under the will (irāda) of God Most High. God's will encompasses everything: So we say: The principle with us is that every occurring event (ḥādith) occurs by the will of God Most High, whether good or evil, beautiful or ugly, substance or accident. And the Mu'tazila claimed that God Most High wills from our actions only what is wisdom and obedience, and does not will what is disobedience and ugly. And they differed concerning permissible acts (mubāḥāt): some of them claimed that God Most High is willing of them, and some claimed that God Most High is not willing of them. And this should be according to the view of the Baghdadis among them, for they claim that God is not described with will (irāda) in reality, but is described with it metaphorically (majāzan). So it is said that He has willed something: if it is from His acts, its meaning is that He does it or did it; and if it is from the acts of others, then the intended meaning is that He commanded it. Thus, since will with them is command, and the permissible is not commanded, it is not willed. And the conclusion with us is that every occurring event occurs by the will of God Most High, whatever description it may have.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage defines the Sunni (Maturidi) position on the scope of divine will (irāda). The author begins by recalling previous discussions that God is willing with an eternal will, and that 'mashī'a' and 'irāda' are synonymous terms for the same attribute subsisting in God's essence. He then states the central principle: every entity that occurs (ḥādith) in the created world, regardless of its moral evaluation (good or evil, beautiful or ugly) or its ontological category (substance or accident), occurs by God's will. This is the foundational axiom. He contrasts this with the Mu'tazila, who restrict God's will to only what is wise and obedient, excluding disobedience and ugly acts. He notes internal Mu'tazila disagreement about permissible acts (mubāḥāt): some hold God wills them, some do not. He explains that the Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila deny that God has a real attribute of will; rather, 'will' is metaphorical. For them, when we say God willed an act of His, it means He performs it; when we say He willed an act of another, it means He commanded it. Thus, for the Baghdadis, will reduces to command, and since permissible acts are not commanded, they are not willed. The author concludes by reiterating the Sunni position: every occurring event, in whatever description, occurs by God's will.

المشيئة (mashī'a)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
البغداديون (al-Baghdādiyyūn)
المباح (mubāḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will for evil actions implies He approves of them.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between God's will and His approval; will does not necessarily entail approval.

Checkpoint

B) Only acts commanded by God
C) Every occurring event, whether good or evil, substance or accident
D) Only what is wise and obedient

Key Takeaway
Every occurring event, good or evil, occurs by God's will; this is a fundamental Sunni (Maturidi) principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila found it troubling that God would will evil—do you share that discomfort? Be honest: Do you secretly wish God were not involved in the evil you have done or seen? What would it mean for your trust in Him if you fully embraced that He wills all things without approving of sin?
?
Knowing that God's will covers your next choice—whether obedience or disobedience—how does that affect your sense of responsibility? Will you use this truth as an excuse to be careless, or as a reason to turn to Him in deeper dependence? Commit now to one concrete change in your daily life based on this truth.

Distinction Between Obedience and Sin in God's Attributes
Details how obedience and sin relate differently to God's attributes, contrasting Maturidi and Ash'ari views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidis distinguish between God's will, pleasure, love, and command in relation to human actions, and how this differs from the Ash'ari view.

Core Idea
God wills sin but does not love or command it.

This lesson explores a key theological distinction: how God's attributes attach to obedience versus sin. While both are in accordance with God's will, the Maturid school preserves God's love and pleasure solely for what is good. We will read Imam Nasafi's clear exposition and contrast it with the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note the list of attributes for obedience and sin.
2
Identify the difference
Notice that obedience is linked to more attributes: will, pleasure, love, command, decree, power. Sin lacks command, pleasure, love.
3
Understand the reason
The passage explains that love and pleasure depend on the thing being considered good (mustaḥsan) by God.
4
Contrast with Ash'ari
See how al-Ash'ari equates love and pleasure with will, making them apply to everything. Maturidi explicitly rejects this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ما كان من ذلك طاعة فهو بمشيئته وإرادته ورضاه ومحبته وأمره وقضائه وقدرته، وما كان معصية فهو بمشيئة الله تعالى وإرادته وقضائه وقدره، وليس بأمر الله ولا برضاه ومحبته، وذلك لأن محبته ورضاه ترجع إلى كون الشيء عنده مستحسنًا، على هذا قدماء أصحابنا، وهو الظاهر من قول مشايخنا في ديارنا الشائع في عوامنا وخواصنا، ونص عليه شيخنا أبو منصور الماتريدي. وزعم أبو الحسن الأشعري أن المحبة والرضى بمعنى الإرادة، ويعمان كل موجود كما تعم الإرادة فكل ما أراد وجوده فقد رضي بوجوده، وأحب وجوده على الوجه الذي أراد.
Then what is from that obedience, it is by His will (mashī'a), His will (irāda), His pleasure (riḍā), His love (maḥabba), His command (amr), His decree (qaḍā'), and His power (qudra). And what is disobedience, it is by the will of God Most High, His will (irāda), His decree (qaḍā') and His determining (qadar), but it is not by God's command nor by His pleasure nor His love. That is because His love and His pleasure revert to the thing being considered good (mustaḥsan) by Him. Upon this are our early (masters), and it is the apparent from the saying of our shaykhs in our country, prevalent among our common and elite, and our shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi explicitly stated it. And Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari claimed that love and pleasure are synonymous with will (irāda), and they encompass every existent just as will encompasses, so whatever He willed its existence, He has been pleased with its existence and loved its existence in the manner He willed.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage classifies the relationship between God's attributes and human actions, distinguishing obedience from sin. The author states that obedience is associated with God's will (mashī'a and irāda), pleasure (riḍā), love (maḥabba), command (amr), decree (qaḍā'), and power (qudra). Disobedience, however, is only by God's will, decree, and power, but not by His command, pleasure, or love. The reason given is that God's love and pleasure depend on something being considered good (mustaḥsan) according to Him. This is the Maturidi position, attributed to the early masters and to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. The author then contrasts this with Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's view, who equates love and pleasure with will (irāda), so they encompass everything that God wills to exist. Thus, for Ash'ari, God loves and is pleased with everything He wills, including sinful acts, whereas for Maturidi, sin is willed but not loved or pleased with.

Key Terms
الطاعة (ṭā'a)
المعصية (ma'ṣiya)
الرضا (riḍā)
المحبة (maḥabba)
القضاء (qaḍā')
القدر (qadar)
المستحسن (mustaḥsan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since God wills sin, He must also be pleased with and love sin.
Correction: Maturidis distinguish between will and pleasure/love. God wills sin but does not love it because love is contingent on the act being good (mustaḥsan).

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi view presented in this passage, which attributes apply to a sinful act?
A) God's command, pleasure, and love
B) God's will, decree, and power
C) God's command and will only
D) God's love and pleasure only

Key Takeaway
For the Maturidi school, God wills all actions but only loves and commands what is good, whereas sin occurs by His will but not His love or command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin that you know God has willed, do you truly believe in your heart that God does not love that act? Or do you harbor a secret thought that since He allowed it, He must be okay with it? This lesson demands you accept that His will and His love are separate—how will you change your thinking about the sins you struggle with?
?
You claim to love God, but do you love what He loves? This passage shows that He loves only what is good (mustaḥsan). Where is your own love directed—toward acts He loves or toward things that please your nafs? What one action today can you choose that you know God loves, even if it is difficult?
?
If God's love for an act depends on it being good, then your actions matter eternally. Is there a sin in your life that you have rationalized by saying 'God willed it'? The lesson strips away that excuse: He wills it, but He hates it. Will you continue in that sin, or will you repent because you know it displeases the One whose love you seek above all else?

God Wills What He Knows Will Exist
Maturidi principle on divine will and knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi teaching that God's will aligns with His eternal knowledge, not with His commands or moral evaluations.

Core Idea
God wills what He knows will happen, regardless of moral value.

This lesson explores a foundational Maturidi principle: that God's will is determined by His eternal knowledge of what will actually occur, not by His commands or the moral quality of actions. The text presents a pedagogical formulation from the Maturidi shaykhs, using examples of disbelievers like Pharaoh and Abu Jahl to illustrate the consistency between God's knowledge and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once and then its literal translation. Notice the key phrasing: 'ما علم الله أن يوجد أراد وجوده' and its counterpart for non-existence.
The passage is concise; read it slowly to capture the structure.
2
Understand the principle
Identify the two parts of the principle: (1) Whatever God knows will exist, He wills it to exist. (2) Whatever He knows will not exist, He wills it not to exist. Note that this applies to all actions, good or evil.
This principle is a pedagogical simplification for learners.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider the examples of Pharaoh and Abu Jahl. God knew they would disbelieve, so He willed their disbelief, even though He forbade it. He knew they would not believe, so He willed that they not believe, even though He commanded belief. This shows God's will follows His knowledge, not His command.
The examples clarify the distinction between divine will and divine command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما علم وجوده أراد أن يوجد عند الماتريدية: ومشايخنا - رحمهم الله - يقولون تيسيراً على المتعلمين بأن ما علم الله أن يوجد أراد وجوده شرًا كان أو خيرًا، قبيحًا كان أو حسنًا، طاعة كان أو معصية (وما علم أنه لا يكون أراد أن لا يكون شرًا كان أو خيرًا، قبيحا كان أو حسنًا، طاعة كان أو معصية). فالله تعالى لما علم أنه يوجد من فرعون وأبي جهل وغيرهما من الكفرة الكفر، أراد منهم الكفر وإن نهاهم عنه، ولما علم أن لا يوجد منهم الإيمان أراد أن لا يوجد منهم الإيمان وإن أمرهم به.
What He knows will exist, He wills it to exist according to the Maturidis: And our shaykhs - may God have mercy on them - say as a facilitation for the learners: that whatever God knew that it will exist, He willed its existence, whether evil or good, ugly or beautiful, obedience or disobedience. And whatever He knew that it will not exist, He willed that it not exist, whether evil or good, ugly or beautiful, obedience or disobedience. So God Most High, since He knew that from Pharaoh and Abu Jahl and others of the disbelievers there will be disbelief, He willed from them disbelief, even though He forbade them from it. And since He knew that faith will not exist from them, He willed that faith not exist from them, even though He commanded them to it.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents a pedagogical formulation from the Maturidi shaykhs for ease of learning. They state a principle: whatever God knows will exist, He wills it to exist, regardless of its moral value (good or evil, obedience or disobedience). Similarly, whatever He knows will not exist, He wills it not to exist. This means God's will aligns with His eternal knowledge of what will actually occur. The author provides the examples of Pharaoh and Abu Jahl: God knew that disbelief would occur from them, so He willed that disbelief from them, even though He forbade them from it. Conversely, He knew that faith would not occur from them, so He willed that faith not occur from them, even though He commanded them to it. This shows that God's will is not dependent on His command; He may command something but will that it not happen, or forbid something but will that it happen.

Key Terms
ما علم (mā ʿalima)
أراد (arāda)
تيسيراً على المتعلمين (taysīran ʿalā al-mutaʿallimīn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's command determines His will.
Correction: This passage shows that God's will follows His knowledge, not His command. He may command something but will that it not happen, or forbid something but will that it happen.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi teaching in this passage, what is the basis for God's willing something to exist?
A) God's command
B) God's knowledge that it will exist
C) The moral value of the action
D) Human choice

Key Takeaway
God's will aligns with His eternal knowledge: He wills what He knows will occur, regardless of moral value or His commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have free will choices every day. If God already knows and wills everything you will do, including your sins, how does this affect your sense of personal responsibility? Are you tempted to use this as an excuse for disobedience? What does the passage say about God still forbidding what He wills? (Read: 'He willed from them disbelief, even though He forbade them from it.') How can you reconcile this with your own accountability?
?

When you feel overwhelmed by your failures, does this lead you to despair or to a deeper trust in God's wisdom? How does knowing that God's will encompasses even your flaws shape your repentance?
?
The passage uses Pharaoh and Abu Jahl as examples. They were condemned because God willed their disbelief. Do you believe that God could will disbelief for anyone? If you were born into circumstances that make faith difficult, how does this passage challenge or comfort you? What does it teach you about God's justice and mercy, especially since He still commands faith?
