The Mu'tazila Principle: God Wills What He Commands
States the Mu'tazila view that God wills what He commands and dislikes what He forbids, with the example of Pharaoh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila view that God's will is directly tied to His command, such that He wills the existence of what He commands and dislikes the existence of what He forbids, regardless of His foreknowledge of actual occurrence.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, God's command defines His will, not His foreknowledge.

The Mu'tazila school held a distinctive view on the relationship between God's command and His will. In this passage, they assert that whatever God commands, He must will its existence, even if He knows it will never happen. Conversely, whatever He forbids, He must dislike and will against its existence, even if He knows it will occur. This principle has significant implications for understanding divine justice and the nature of evil. Let us examine their claim with the example of Pharaoh.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the core assertion.
Notice the contrast between what God knows and what He wills.
2
Analyze the logical structure
The Mu'tazila present a universal rule: God's command implies His will, and God's prohibition implies His dislike. They add the surprising twist that this holds even, or especially, when His knowledge contradicts what He wills.
This is central to their doctrine of divine justice.
3
Consider the example of Pharaoh
The passage begins an example with Pharaoh, likely to illustrate that God commanded Pharaoh to do something (perhaps believe) and willed it, even though He knew Pharaoh would not comply. Reflect on how this example supports the rule.
The text is cut off, but the principle is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما أمر بوجوده أراد أن يوجد عند المعتزلة: وزعمت المعتزلة أن ما أمر الله تعالى به أراد وجوده، وإن علم أنه لا يوجد وما نهى عنه كره وجوده، وأراد أن لا يوجد وإن علم وجوده فلما كان أمر فرعون
What He commands to exist, He wills it to exist according to the Mu'tazila: And the Mu'tazila claimed that what God Most High has commanded, He willed its existence, even if He knows it will not exist. And what He has forbidden, He disliked its existence and willed that it not exist, even if He knows it will exist. For since Pharaoh was commanded...
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila position on the relationship between divine will and command. They hold that God wills whatever He commands, regardless of whether He knows it will actually occur. Conversely, God dislikes (and wills against) whatever He forbids, even if He knows it will happen. The example of Pharaoh is introduced but cut off. This contrasts sharply with the Maturidi view that God's will is based on His knowledge of what will actually happen, not on His command. For the Mu'tazila, God's command indicates His will, so a forbidden act cannot be willed by God, even if it occurs.

Key Terms
الأمر (amr)
النهي (nahy)
الكَراهة (karāha)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that God does not will anything evil, so all evil happens without His will.
Correction: The Mu'tazila tie will to command: God wills what He commands and dislikes what He forbids. Forbidden acts are not willed, but they can still occur (e.g., Pharaoh's disobedience). So evil actions are not willed by God, but this passage shows that His will is based on command, not foreknowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila principle in this passage, if God commands something, what is His will concerning it?
A) He wills it to exist, even if He knows it will not exist
B) He wills it not to exist
C) He neither wills nor dislikes it
D) He wills it only if He knows it will exist

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila maintain that God's will is determined by His command, not by His foreknowledge of actual events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to obey a clear command of Allah, do you truly believe that He wills your obedience even if you fail? How does that belief affect your striving?
?
The Mu'tazila state that God dislikes what He forbids. When you sin, do you sense that you are doing something Allah dislikes? How does that reality weigh on your repentance?
?
If God's will is tied to His command rather than outcomes, what does that mean for your sincere but unanswered prayers? Will you still believe He willed what you asked for, even if it never happened?

The Maturidi Position on Divine Will and Its Two Scholarly Expressions
Al-Nasafi contrasts Maturidi and Mu'tazili views on divine will and presents two Maturidi expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on divine will (irada) and the two scholarly expressions used to articulate it without causing misunderstanding.

Core Idea
Divine will conforms to knowledge, not command; its expression can be general or qualified.

This passage explores a central divide between Maturidis and Mu'tazila on divine will. Al-Nasafi presents the Maturidi stance and then shows how early scholars expressed it in two ways to avoid implying error or misunderstanding. The first expression affirms divine will in general terms, while the second adds a qualification when speaking in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the flow: first the contrast with Mu'tazila, then the two expressions.
Pay attention to the key terms: إرادة, موافقة للعلم, موافقة للأمر.
2
Identify the core difference with Mu'tazila
The passage states that for Maturidis, divine will conforms to knowledge, while for Mu'tazila it conforms to command. Understand this distinction.
This is the foundation of the whole discussion.
3
Examine the two scholarly expressions
First expression: affirm will in general (jumla) but avoid detailed mention of sins. Second expression: add a qualification when being detailed to avoid implying error. Notice the analogies given (e.g., Creator of filth).
Both expressions are valid and there is no real disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالإيمان أراد منه الإيمان وإن علم أنه لا يوجد منه، ولما نهاه عن الكفر أراد أن لا يوجد منه الكفر وإن علم أنه يوجد فكانت إرادة الله عندنا موافقة للعلم وعندهم موافقة للأمر، وعلى هذا يدور الخلاف. ثم اختلفت عبارات أصحابنا في ذلك فمنهم من قال : أقول في الجملة: إن الله تعالی مرید حدوث كل ما علم حدوثه ولا يكون في سلطانه إلا ما يريد كونه ولا ينتفي عنه ما أراد كما أطلقه المسلمين بقولهم : ما شاء الله كان، وما لم يشأ لم يكن قال: ولا أقول بالتفصيل أنه أراد الكفر والفرية عليه وشتم نفسه وغير ذلك من المعاصي، كما يقال إنه خالق العالم، ولا يقال على التفصيل أنه خالق الأقذار والأنتان والشياطين والعقارب، وإن كانت هذه التفاصيل داخلة تحت الجملة فكذا هذا، وهذا كما يقال كل ما سوى الله ضعيف على الجملة، ولا يقال دين الله ضعيف على التفصيل، ويقال : كل مخلوق حجة الله تعالى ويقال : إن هذه الخشبة منكسرة ولا يقال حجة الله منكسرة، ويقال : الليلة مظلمة، ولا يقال حجة الله مظلمة فكذا هذا، وإليه ذهب قدماء أصحابنا منهم عبد الله بن سعيد القطان. ومنهم من يقول : لا أقتصر عند التفصيل أن أقول: إن الله تعالى أراد الكفر والزنا بل أزيد عليه قرينة، فأقول : أراد من الكافر الكفر كسبًا له قبيحًا منه مذموماً وكذا في غيره من المعاصي لئلا يؤدي إلى إيهام الخطأ وهو اختيار الأشعري وهو قريب مما اختاره شيخنا أبو منصور، ولا خلاف بين أصحابنا في المسألة في الحقيقة بل يختار البعض عبارة لا يسبق منها إلى وهم سامع معنى لم يقصده المتكلم.
He intended faith from him [the believer] even though He knew that it would not occur from him, and when He forbade him from disbelief He intended that disbelief not occur from him even though He knew that it would occur. Thus God's will, according to us, conforms to knowledge, and according to them, conforms to command, and upon this revolves the disagreement. Then the expressions of our companions varied concerning that. Some of them said: I say in general: God is the willer of the occurrence of everything whose occurrence He knows, and nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills to be, and what He wills is not prevented from Him, as the Muslims have expressed by their saying: 'What God wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.' He said: I do not say in detail that He willed disbelief, slander against Him, cursing of Himself, and other sins, just as it is said that He is the Creator of the world, but it is not said in detail that He is the Creator of filth, foul things, devils, and scorpions, even though these details are included under the general statement. So likewise this is the case. This is like saying: everything other than God is weak in general, but it is not said that the religion of God is weak in detail; and it is said: every created thing is God's proof, but it is not said that this piece of wood is broken and God's proof is broken; and it is said: the night is dark, but it is not said that God's proof is dark. Thus this is the case. And this is the view of the early ones among our companions, among them 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan. And among them is one who says: I do not restrict myself, when being detailed, to saying that God willed disbelief and adultery; rather I add a qualification to it, so I say: He willed from the disbeliever disbelief as an acquisition from him, blameworthy and condemned from him, and likewise in other sins, so that it does not lead to the illusion of error. And this is the choice of al-Ash'ari, and it is close to what our teacher Abu Mansur chose. And there is no disagreement among our companions in the matter in reality; rather, some choose an expression from which no meaning that the speaker did not intend comes to the mind of a listener.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi position on divine will (irada) and contrasts it with the Mu'tazili view. The author states that when God intended faith from the believer, He intended it even though He knew it would not occur from him. Then for the command to avoid disbelief, He intended that disbelief not occur even though He knew it would occur. Thus, for the Maturidis, God's will conforms to His knowledge, while for the Mu'tazila, it conforms to His command. This is the core difference. Next, the author notes that Maturidi scholars varied in their expressions. The first view says one should affirm divine will in general (jumla) as encompassing all occurrences, but avoid stating in detail (tafsil) that God wills specific sins, akin to saying God is Creator of the world in general but not specifying creation of foul things. This view is attributed to 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan. The second view allows detailed statement with a qualification: one says God willed disbelief from the disbeliever as an acquisition, blameworthy, to avoid implying error. This is close to al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. The author concludes there is no real disagreement; expressions are chosen to prevent misunderstanding.

Key Terms
إرادة
موافقة للعلم
موافقة للأمر
الجملة
التفصيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidis deny that God wills evil in any sense.
Correction: They affirm it in general (jumla) that God wills all occurrences, but they either avoid explicit mention of evil in detail or add a qualification to prevent misunderstanding.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi position, God's will (irada) conforms to what?
A) His command
B) His knowledge
C) His pleasure
D) His wisdom

Key Takeaway
Maturidis affirm that God's will encompasses all occurrences in general, but when speaking in detail, they either avoid explicit mention of evil or add a qualification to prevent misunderstanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a trial or tragedy, do you sincerely believe that it is by God's will and that His will is good, even if you cannot see the wisdom? How can you train your heart to submit to His will in the face of hardship?
?
Have you ever spoken about God in a way that could lead others to misunderstand His will, such as attributing evil to Him without proper qualification? How will you guard your tongue to honor His majesty?
?
The lesson shows that God's will encompasses everything, including disbelief and sin. Does this truth make you more fearful of His majesty or more reliant on His mercy? What specific change in your daily life should this awareness produce?

Proper Phrasing of Allah's Will for Evil Acts
Nasafi reports opinions on phrasing Allah's will for evil and the need for qualifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why certain expressions about Allah's will for evil are avoided and how to properly qualify such statements.

Core Idea
Speech about Allah's will must avoid implying imperfection.

This lesson examines the early Maturidi position on how to speak of Allah's will regarding evil acts. Nasafi reports that some scholars, like Abdullah ibn Said al-Qattan, strictly avoided certain analogies, while others, like al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur, recommended adding qualifiers to prevent misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the examples given: 'the night is dark' vs. 'Allah's proof is broken'.
2
Identify the opinions
Identify the two approaches: the early Maturidi position of avoiding analogies, and the Ash'ari/Abu Mansur position of adding qualifying phrases.
3
Understand the rationale
Reflect on why the qualifier is needed—to avoid suggesting that Allah's knowledge or power is deficient.
4
Consider the unity
Note that the author says there is no real disagreement, but a choice of expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منكسرة ولا يقال حجة الله منكسرة، ويقال : الليلة مظلمة، ولا يقال حجة الله مظلمة فكذا هذا، وإليه ذهب قدماء أصحابنا منهم عبد الله بن سعيد القطان. ومنهم من يقول : لا أقتصر عند التفصيل أن أقول: إن الله تعالى أراد الكفر والزنا بل أزيد عليه قرينة، فأقول : أراد من الكافر الكفر كسبًا له قبيحًا منه مذموماً وكذا في غيره من المعاصي لئلا يؤدي إلى إيهام الخطأ وهو اختيار الأشعري وهو قريب مما اختاره شيخنا أبو منصور، ولا خلاف بين أصحابنا في المسألة في الحقيقة بل يختار البعض عبارة لا يسبق منها إلى وهم سامع معنى لم يقصده المتكلم. ثم قال شيخنا أبو منصور : إذا سئلنا عن هذه المسألة فلها وجهان: أحدهما الإطلاق على المفهوم من الإرادة. والثاني: منع الإطلاق إذا لم يفهم مراد السائل أو خشي التعنت، فيه وهو أن : يقال: إن للمشيئة معاني فيما يتعارف أحدها للتمني، والثاني الأمر والدعاء إليه، والثالث الرضا به، والرابع نفي الغلبة وخروج الفعل على ما يقدره ويريده. فالأول منفي في كل شيء، لأن التمني إرادة ما علم أنه لا يكون وهو ضعف أو إرادة ما شك في كونه وهو جهل. والثاني والثالث صفتان في كل فعل يذم عليه. والرابع به نقول، وقد أجمعنا نحن ومن خالفنا على معناه وإن اختلفنا في اللفظ. ثم هذه المسألة في الحقيقة عين مسألة خلق الأفعال فإنا إذا أثبتنا بالدليل أن الله تعالى خالق أفعال العباد كفرًا كان أو معصية أو طاعة ، والله تعالى مختار في فعله غير مضطر كان مريدًا في تخليقه إياه فلا حاجة بنا بعد ذلك إلى إقامة دليل مبتدأ إلا أن السلف لما تكلموا في المسألة على طريق الأصالة أتبعناهم وتكلمنا فيها.
Broken, and it is not said 'Allah's proof is broken', and it is said 'the night is dark', and it is not said 'Allah's proof is dark'; so also this. And to this went the early ones of our companions, among them 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan. And among them is one who says: I do not confine myself in detail to saying 'Indeed Allah willed disbelief and adultery' but I add to it a qualifying phrase, so I say: 'He willed from the disbeliever disbelief as an acquisition of his, ugly from him, blameworthy,' and similarly in other sins, lest it lead to suggesting error. This is the choice of al-Ash'ari, and it is close to what our shaykh Abu Mansur chose. And there is no disagreement among our companions in the matter in reality, but some choose an expression from which no unintended meaning occurs to the mind of a hearer. Then our shaykh Abu Mansur said: If we are asked about this matter, it has two aspects: first, absolute statement based on the understood meaning of will; second, prohibition of absolute statement if the questioner's intent is not understood or oppression is feared. And it is that it is said: Indeed al-mashi'a (will) has meanings in conventional usage: one of them is for tamanni (wishing), the second is for command and summoning to it, the third is for rida (pleasure) with it, and the fourth is nafy al-ghalaba (negation of coercion) and the occurrence of the act according to what He determines and wills. The first is negated in everything, because tamanni is willing what one knows will not be, and that is weakness, or willing what one doubts about its occurrence, and that is ignorance. The second and third are attributes in every blameworthy act. The fourth is what we affirm, and we and those who oppose us have agreed on its meaning even if we differ in wording. Then this matter is in reality the same as the matter of the creation of actions. For when we establish by evidence that Allah is the Creator of the actions of servants, whether disbelief, disobedience, or obedience, and Allah is free choice in His action, not compelled, He is willer in His creating it. So after that we have no need to establish a new proof, except that the predecessors discussed the matter as a primary topic, so we followed them and discussed it.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the proper phrasing of Allah's will with respect to evil acts, including a classification of the meanings of al-mashi'a (will). It begins by noting that certain expressions are inappropriate, such as saying 'Allah's proof is broken' by analogy with 'the night is dark.' The early Maturidis, like 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, held that one should avoid such analogies. Others, including al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, recommend adding a qualifying phrase when saying Allah willed disbelief or adultery, to avoid misunderstanding. The core of the passage is then the four conventional meanings of al-mashi'a: 1) tamanni (wish) – willing what cannot be or is uncertain, which is weakness or ignorance; 2) amr (command) and da'wa (summons); 3) rida (pleasure); 4) nafy al-ghalaba (negation of coercion) and the occurrence of the act as Allah determines. The first meaning is negated regarding Allah, the second and third are attributes of blameworthy acts (i.e., they imply command or pleasure in evil, which does not apply to Allah), while the fourth is what the Maturidis affirm: that Allah wills evil in the sense of His creative will and determination, not as command or pleasure. The passage then connects this to the issue of khalaq al-af'al (creation of actions): since Allah creates all actions, He wills them in the sense of creative decree, and no separate proof is needed beyond that. The discussion follows the method of the salaf.

Key Terms
المشيئة
التمني
الأمر
الرضا
نفي الغلبة
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is permissible to say 'Allah willed disbelief' without any qualification.
Correction: In fact, one should add a qualifier to avoid implying that Allah commands or approves of evil.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the view of Abdullah ibn Said al-Qattan?
A) It is permissible to say 'Allah's proof is broken' if one adds a qualifier.
B) One should avoid analogies like 'the night is dark' for Allah's proof.
C) One must always add a qualifying phrase when speaking of Allah's will.
D) He allowed absolute statements without qualification.

Key Takeaway
When speaking of Allah's will for evil, one must use qualifiers to avoid attributing imperfection to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself wrestling with the existence of evil in the world, do you speak about Allah's will in a way that safeguards His perfection, or do you inadvertently let frustration shape your words? What specific phrase will you correct in your own speech starting today?
?
The early scholars chose expressions to protect the hearer from misunderstanding. In your conversations about divine decree, do you prioritize theological precision or the spiritual well-being of those listening? How might this lesson change your approach?
?
Imagine a friend asks you, 'Does Allah will every sin that happens?' Without using this lesson, how would you answer? Now, after reading it, what qualifier must you add so that no one wrongly assumes Allah approves of the sin? Will you commit to using that qualifier?

The Four Meanings of al-Mashi'a
Nasafi classifies the four conventional meanings of al-mashi'a and identifies which can be attributed to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four conventional meanings of al-mashi'a and which of these apply to Allah.

Core Idea
Allah's will (mashi'a) does not mean wish or pleasure in evil; it means creative decree.

In this lesson, Nasafi, following the lead of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, classifies the four distinct conventional meanings of the term 'mashi'a'. He shows that only one of these—the negation of coercion and the determination of acts—can be attributed to Allah, while the others would imply imperfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that the passage begins by stating two aspects: absolute statement and prohibition of absolute statement.
2
Identify the four meanings
List the four meanings: tamanni, amr/da'wa, rida, nafy al-ghalaba. Understand the definition of each.
3
Analyze which apply to Allah
Note that the first (tamanni) is negated because it implies weakness or ignorance. The second and third (command and pleasure) are attributes of blameworthy acts and thus not for Allah. The fourth is what Maturidis affirm.
4
Connect to creation of actions
See how this classification is tied to the doctrine that Allah creates all actions, so His will is creative decree.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then this matter is in reality the same as the matter of the creation of actions. For when we establish by evidence that Allah is the Creator of the actions of servants, whether disbelief, disobedience, or obedience, and Allah is free choice in His action, not compelled, He is willer in His creating it. So after that we have no need to establish a new proof, except that the predecessors discussed the matter as a primary topic, so we followed them and discussed it.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the proper phrasing of Allah's will with respect to evil acts, including a classification of the meanings of al-mashi'a (will). It begins by noting that certain expressions are inappropriate, such as saying 'Allah's proof is broken' by analogy with 'the night is dark.' The early Maturidis, like 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, held that one should avoid such analogies. Others, including al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, recommend adding a qualifying phrase when saying Allah willed disbelief or adultery, to avoid misunderstanding. The core of the passage is then the four conventional meanings of al-mashi'a: 1) tamanni (wish) – willing what cannot be or is uncertain, which is weakness or ignorance; 2) amr (command) and da'wa (summons); 3) rida (pleasure); 4) nafy al-ghalaba (negation of coercion) and the occurrence of the act as Allah determines. The first meaning is negated regarding Allah, the second and third are attributes of blameworthy acts (i.e., they imply command or pleasure in evil, which does not apply to Allah), while the fourth is what the Maturidis affirm: that Allah wills evil in the sense of His creative will and determination, not as command or pleasure. The passage then connects this to the issue of khalaq al-af'al (creation of actions): since Allah creates all actions, He wills them in the sense of creative decree, and no separate proof is needed beyond that. The discussion follows the method of the salaf.

Key Terms
المشيئة
التمني
الأمر
الرضا
نفي الغلبة
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When Allah wills something, He must be pleased with it and command it.
Correction: Allah's will (mashi'a) can mean creative decree without implying pleasure or command, as shown by the classification.

Checkpoint
Which meaning of al-mashi'a do Maturidis affirm according to this passage?
A) Tamanni
B) Amr
C) Rida
D) Nafy al-ghalaba

Key Takeaway
Allah's will for evil acts is His creative determination, not wish, command, or pleasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall a past sin, do you secretly feel that Allah 'wished' it in the sense of wanting you to commit it? How does knowing that His will for your sin was creative determination, not pleasure or wish, change your view of your own accountability and your relationship with Him? What will you do with that guilt now?
?
If you saw a terrible injustice in the world, would you be tempted to think that Allah must somehow approve of it if He willed it? After this lesson, how will you speak to your own heart about the difference between Allah's creative decree and His pleasure? Write down a specific sentence you will tell yourself next time you face that doubt.
?
The passage says that command and pleasure are attributes of blameworthy acts, so they cannot be applied to Allah in evil. Does this truth confront any hidden assumption you had about Allah's involvement in your daily failures? Will you now repent differently, knowing that Allah created your action but did not love it? Commit to one change in how you repent based on this lesson.

The Mu'tazila's Scriptural Objections to Divine Will
The author lists four scriptural arguments from the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four Quranic verses the Mu'tazila use to argue that God cannot will disbelief and sin.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila cite scripture to limit God's will to good alone.

The Mu'tazila, like the Ahl al-Sunna, appeal to the Quran, but they interpret certain verses to deny that God wills evil. In this lesson, we examine four key verses they present as proof that God's will cannot encompass disbelief or sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for scriptural doubts
Read the portion of the passage covering the scriptural arguments. Focus on the verses cited and the Mu'tazila's inference from each.
The author lists four verses; note how each is used to argue against the possibility of God willing evil.
2
Identify the four verses
List the four verses: creation for worship (51:56), desire for ease (2:185), polytheists' claim (6:148/16:35), and no injustice for servants (3:108/22:10). Understand the literal meaning of each.
These are the scriptural proofs the Mu'tazila rely on.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila's inference
For each verse, explain how the Mu'tazila deduce that God does not will disbelief or sin. For example, from 'I created only to worship' they infer that if God willed disbelief, He would not have created for worship.
The author presents these as objections to the Sunni position.
4
See the author's framing
Notice that the author attributes to the Mu'tazila the accusation that the Sunni view contradicts these verses. Reflect on how a Sunni theologian might respond.
This lesson presents the objection; the response will come later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: إن المعتزلة يتعلقون بشبه بعضها سمعية وبعضها عقلية. فأما السمعية فنحو قوله تعالى : ( وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ) أخبر الله تعالى أنه خلقهم جميعًا للعبادة، وأنتم تقولون إنه خلق الكفرة منهم والعصاة لا للعبادة بل للكفر والمعصية وقوله تعالى : ( يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ ) والكفر أعسر العسر، وقوله تعالى: ( سَيَقُولُ الَّذِينَ أَشْرَكُوا لَوْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ مَا أَشْرَكْنَا ). فالله تعالى أخبر أنهم يقولون : لو شاء الله ما أشركنا ثم كذبهم في ذلك ورد قولهم بقوله : ( كَذَلِكَ كَذَّبَ الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ ) وقوله : ( كَذَلِكَ فَعَلَ الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ ) وأنتم تساعدون في المقالة من كذبه الله تعالى بنص كتابه وهو باطل، وقوله: ( وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ ) وعندكم يريد كل ظلم كان ويكون، وهو خلاف ما في الكتاب، وإكذاب الله تعالى في ما أخبر. وأما العقلية فكقولهم: مريد السفه سفيه اعتبارًا بالشاهد، ومريد الكفر كافر ومريد المعصية عاصي ويعتبر بالشاهد، ومريد شتم نفسه والافتراء عليه سفيه أيضا في الشاهد فكذا في الغائب إذ الشاهد دليل الغائب، ولأن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عما أراده الله تعالى على زعمكم فيصير الكافر مجبورا على كفره، ممنوعًا عن الإيمان بالإرادة التي هي أبرم وأحكم من الحديد في المنع، فإما أن يعذر على ذلك والقول به تكذيب للكتاب وخروج عن الإجماع، وإما أن يعاقب عليه ولا يعذر وهو القول بتكليف ما ليس في الوسع، ولأن إرادة ما لا يرضى به سفه والأمر بما لا يريده سفه في الشاهد فكذا في الغائب إلى مثل هذه الشبهات يذهبون.
We say: The Mu'tazila cling to doubts, some of which are scriptural and some rational. As for the scriptural: like His statement, the Exalted: 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me.' Allah the Exalted informed that He created all of them for worship, and you (Mu'tazila) say that He created the disbelievers and sinners among them not for worship but for disbelief and sin. And His statement: 'Allah desires ease for you and does not desire hardship for you.' And disbelief is the hardest hardship. And His statement: 'Those who associate partners will say: If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners.' So Allah the Exalted informed that they say: 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners.' Then He declared them liars in that and rejected their statement with His words: 'Thus did those before them deny' and His words: 'Thus did those before them do.' And you (Mu'tazila) support in the statement the one whom Allah declared a liar by the text of His Book, and that is false. And His statement: 'And Allah does not desire injustice for the servants.' And according to you, He desires every injustice that was and will be, and that contradicts what is in the Book, and it is giving the lie to Allah in what He informed. As for the rational: like their statement: The one who wills foolishness is foolish, by analogy with the witnessed realm; and the one who wills disbelief is a disbeliever, and the one who wills sin is a sinner, and that is considered by analogy with the witnessed; and the one who wills insulting himself and slandering himself is also foolish in the witnessed, so likewise in the unseen, for the witnessed is evidence of the unseen. And because the servant cannot escape from what Allah has willed, according to your claim, so the disbeliever becomes compelled in his disbelief, prevented from faith by the will which is more binding and stronger than iron in prevention. So either he is excused for that — and holding that view is giving the lie to the Book and departing from consensus — or he is punished for it and not excused, and that is the doctrine of imposing an obligation beyond capacity. And because willing what one is not pleased with is foolish, and commanding what one does not will is foolish in the witnessed, so likewise in the unseen. To such doubts they go.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage enumerates the two categories of doubts (شبه) that the Mu'tazila employ to argue against the Maturidi/Ash'ari position that God wills all things, including disbelief and sin. The author first notes that these doubts are of two types: scriptural (سمعية) and rational (عقلية). He then lists four scriptural arguments: (1) the verse 'I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship' implies that if God created everyone for worship, He cannot will disbelief; (2) 'Allah desires ease for you and does not desire hardship' and disbelief is the greatest hardship; (3) the polytheists say 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners' — the Mu'tazila argue that God rebuked them, so the statement that God wills shirk must be false; (4) 'And Allah does not desire injustice for the servants' — the Mu'tazila argue God cannot will any injustice. The author then turns to rational doubts, which are analogical arguments from the witnessed world (الشاهد) to the unseen (الغائب). For example, if a person wills foolishness, he is called foolish; so if God wills foolishness, He would be foolish — impossible. Similarly, if a person wills disbelief, he is a disbeliever; so if God wills disbelief, He would be a disbeliever. Also, if a person wills to insult himself, that is foolishness; likewise for God. Furthermore, they argue that if God's will is so powerful that nothing can contradict it, then the disbeliever is compelled (مجبر) to disbelieve, so he should be excused. But if he is punished, that is imposing an obligation beyond capacity (تكليف ما لا يطاق). The author ends by stating that the Mu'tazila resort to such doubts.

Key Terms
شبه
سمعية
إرادة
كفر
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila only use rational arguments, not scripture.
Correction: This passage shows they extensively use Quranic verses to support their position.

Checkpoint
Which verse do the Mu'tazila use to argue that if God created everyone for worship, He cannot will disbelief?
A) 'And Allah does not desire injustice for the servants.'
B) 'Allah desires ease for you and does not desire hardship.'
C) 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me.'
D) 'Those who associate partners will say: If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners.'

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use Quranic verses to build a case that God's will is limited to what is good and cannot include evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila used verses like 'God desires ease for you' to argue that He cannot will disbelief. Have you ever taken a single verse out of context to justify your own desires or avoid a difficult truth? How will you commit to seeking comprehensive understanding of Scripture from now on?
?
If God truly wills everything that happens—including hardships and trials—how does that reshape your response when life is painful? Will you continue to complain against His decree, or will you surrender to His wisdom and ask for patience?
?
The Mu'tazila's argument made logical sense to them, yet it contradicted the broader teaching of the Quran. When have you trusted your own reasoning over clear revelation? What steps will you take to hold your intellect accountable to the Book of Allah?

The Mu'tazila's Rational Objections to Divine Will
The author presents the Mu'tazila's rational arguments from analogy and compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogical arguments the Mu'tazila use from the witnessed world to argue that God cannot will evil or sin.

Core Idea
From human analogies, the Mu'tazila claim that willing evil makes one evil, even for God.

Beyond scripture, the Mu'tazila employ rational arguments based on analogy (qiyas) between the witnessed realm (al-shahid) and the unseen (al-gha'ib). They argue that if a human who wills foolishness is foolish, then God willing foolishness would be foolish—an impossibility. In this lesson, we explore these rational objections and the problem of human compulsion they raise.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational arguments section
Read the portion of the passage starting with 'وأما العقلية'. Note the analogies presented.
Focus on the structure: each argument begins with an observation from the witnessed world.
2
Understand the analogy from shahid to gha'ib
Identify the key analogies: willing foolishness implies foolishness, willing disbelief implies disbelief, willing self-insult implies foolishness. The Mu'tazila apply these to God.
They assume that what is true in the witnessed applies equally to the unseen.
3
Analyze the problem of human compulsion
The Mu'tazila argue that if God wills all actions, then humans are compelled (majbur). This leads to either excusing the disbeliever (which is against the Quran and consensus) or punishing beyond capacity (taklif ma la yutaq).
This is a classic argument against predestination.
4
Recognize the author's presentation
The author presents these as 'doubts' (shubuhat) that the Mu'tazila resort to. Reflect on why the Sunni theologian considers them invalid.
The lesson presents the objection; the response will be addressed elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: إن المعتزلة يتعلقون بشبه بعضها سمعية وبعضها عقلية. فأما السمعية فنحو قوله تعالى : ( وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ) أخبر الله تعالى أنه خلقهم جميعًا للعبادة، وأنتم تقولون إنه خلق الكفرة منهم والعصاة لا للعبادة بل للكفر والمعصية وقوله تعالى : ( يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ ) والكفر أعسر العسر، وقوله تعالى: ( سَيَقُولُ الَّذِينَ أَشْرَكُوا لَوْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ مَا أَشْرَكْنَا ). فالله تعالى أخبر أنهم يقولون : لو شاء الله ما أشركنا ثم كذبهم في ذلك ورد قولهم بقوله : ( كَذَلِكَ كَذَّبَ الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ ) وقوله : ( كَذَلِكَ فَعَلَ الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ ) وأنتم تساعدون في المقالة من كذبه الله تعالى بنص كتابه وهو باطل، وقوله: ( وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ ) وعندكم يريد كل ظلم كان ويكون، وهو خلاف ما في الكتاب، وإكذاب الله تعالى في ما أخبر. وأما العقلية فكقولهم: مريد السفه سفيه اعتبارًا بالشاهد، ومريد الكفر كافر ومريد المعصية عاصي ويعتبر بالشاهد، ومريد شتم نفسه والافتراء عليه سفيه أيضا في الشاهد فكذا في الغائب إذ الشاهد دليل الغائب، ولأن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عما أراده الله تعالى على زعمكم فيصير الكافر مجبورا على كفره، ممنوعًا عن الإيمان بالإرادة التي هي أبرم وأحكم من الحديد في المنع، فإما أن يعذر على ذلك والقول به تكذيب للكتاب وخروج عن الإجماع، وإما أن يعاقب عليه ولا يعذر وهو القول بتكليف ما ليس في الوسع، ولأن إرادة ما لا يرضى به سفه والأمر بما لا يريده سفه في الشاهد فكذا في الغائب إلى مثل هذه الشبهات يذهبون.

?

Will you actively work to submit your intellect to revelation in such moments?
?

Have you ever felt that someone's disbelief or sin was somehow excusable due to circumstances, subtly questioning divine justice? How does this passage compel you to hold both divine will and human responsibility without making excuses? What steps will you take to avoid falling into such rationalizations?
?

Are there areas in your own understanding of God where you unconsciously apply human standards? Will you examine your beliefs to ensure they are based on revelation and sound methodology, not just what appears rational to you?

Refuting the Lam al-‘Aqiba Interpretation: Ahl al-Haqq's Quranic Proof
Ahl al-Haqq cite verses proving God wills disbelief and refute the Mu'tazila's lam al-‘aqiba interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Haqq use Quranic verses to prove that God wills disbelief and sin, and how they refute the Mu'tazila's claim that the lam in these verses indicates consequence rather than purpose.

Core Idea
The lam in Quranic verses about divine will indicates purpose, not mere consequence.

The Mu'tazila argued that when God says He gives respite to disbelievers 'so that they may increase in sin' (Ali Imran 3:178), the lam (ل) is for 'consequence' (العاقبة), not 'purpose' (التعليل), to avoid implying that God wills sin. But the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) demonstrate that the verses themselves prove divine purpose. In this lesson, we examine their evidence and logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Read the two verses cited by Ahl al-Haqq and note the use of lam. The first is from Surah Ali Imran (3:178) and the second from Surah Al-A'raf (7:179).
Pay attention to the lam before the verbs 'liyazdādū' and 'li-jahannam'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Learn why the Mu'tazila claim that the lam is for consequence (lam al-‘aqiba). They argue that just as Pharaoh's family did not intend for Moses to become an enemy, God does not intend for disbelievers to increase in sin; rather, it is the natural consequence.
They support their claim with the analogy of 'born for death' and 'built for ruin'.
3
Follow the Ahl al-Haqq refutation
See how Ahl al-Haqq argue that the context of the verses indicates divine purpose. In the first verse, God explicitly states 'We only grant them respite so that they may increase in sin,' which is a clear purpose clause. In the second, creating for Hell implies willful intent.
They also point out that if the lam were for consequence, it would imply injustice, which is impossible for God.
4
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the 'born for death' example used by the Mu'tazila. Ahl al-Haqq counter that such analogies apply only to human actions where intention and consequence differ, but with God, His decree and purpose are one.
This reinforces that the lam in the divine verses indicates purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأهل الحق يحتجون بقوله تعالى: ( وَلَا يَحْسَبَنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا أَنَّمَا نُمْلِي هُمْ خَيْرٌ لأَنْفُسِهِمْ إِنَّمَا نُمْلِي لَهُمْ لِيَزْدَادُوا إِثْمًا ) دلت الآية أنه أراد منهم ازدياد الإثم حيث أملى لهم ليزدادوا إثما، وقال تعالى : ( وَلَقَدْ ذَرَأْنَا لِجَهَنَّمَ كَثِيرًا مِّنَ الْجِنِّ وَالْإِنسِ ) ومن ذرأه لجهنم أراد منه ما به يصير لجهنم إذ لو ذرأه لجهنم مع إرادة ما به يصير للجنة فقد أراد فيه ما يصير بإدخاله ما ذرأه له ظالمًا، وهذا محال. وزعمت المعتزلة أن اللام في الآيتين جميعًا لام العاقبة لا لام التعليل كما في قوله تعالى: ( فَالْتَقَطَهُ آلُ فِرْعَوْنَ لِيَكُونَ لَهُمْ عَدُوًا وَحَزَنًا ) وهم ما التقطوه لهذا وإنما التقطوه ليكون لهم ولدًا وقرة عين، ولكن لما كان عاقبة أمره أن صار لهم عدوا ذكر هذه اللفظة وكما يقال : ولدوا للموت وبنوا للخراب، وأحدٌ لا يلد للموت بل يطلب بقاء الولد ولا يبني للخراب ولكن للانتفاع بها، ولكن لما كان
And the People of Truth adduce His statement, the Exalted: 'And let not those who disbelieve think that Our granting them respite is good for themselves; We only grant them respite so that they may increase in sin.' The verse indicates that He willed from them an increase in sin, since He granted them respite so that they may increase in sin. And He said, the Exalted: 'And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind.' And whoever He created for Hell, He willed from him that by which he becomes for Hell; for if He created him for Hell while willing that by which he becomes for Paradise, then He would have willed for him that by which he enters what He created him for, being unjust, and that is impossible. And the Mu'tazila claimed that the lam in both verses is the lam of consequence, not the lam of purpose, as in His statement: 'So the family of Pharaoh picked him up to be for them an enemy and a grief.' And they did not pick him up for this, but rather they picked him up so that he would be for them a son and a comfort, but when the consequence of his affair became that he was an enemy to them, this expression was used. And as it is said: 'They give birth for death and build for ruin.' And no one gives birth for death, but rather seeks the survival of the child, and does not build for ruin but for benefit from it, but when the consequence is...
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents the response of the People of Truth (أهل الحق) — the Maturidi/Ash'ari school — to the Mu'tazila's scriptural doubts. The author cites two Quranic verses that explicitly indicate that God wills disbelief and sin. First, Surah Al 'Imran 3:178: 'We only grant them respite so that they may increase in sin' — the lam (ل) here indicates purpose (التعليل), not mere consequence. If the Mu'tazila claim that the lam is for consequence (العاقبة), the author refutes this by analyzing a verse they themselves use: the story of Moses being picked up by Pharaoh's family 'to be for them an enemy and a grief' (Surah Al-Qasas 28:8). While the family intended to adopt him, the ultimate consequence was enmity. However, the author argues that in the case of the disbelievers, the lam indeed indicates purpose because God explicitly says 'so that they may increase in sin', and He is all-knowing and all-powerful. The author also uses the common saying 'they give birth for death' (ولدوا للموت) — though no one gives birth intending death, the phrase uses 'lam' to indicate consequence, not purpose. But in the divine verses, the context proves divine purpose. The passage is cut off, but the argument is clear: the Mu'tazila's interpretation is refuted by the explicit wording of the verses.

Correction: Ahl al-Haqq show that the lam indicates purpose because God explicitly states the reason for respite, and the context shows He wills the increase of sin. The Mu'tazila's analogy with 'picked up to be an enemy' is invalid because in that case the subject's intention was different; but with God, His decree is purposeful.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verses clearly indicate that God wills the increase of sin for disbelievers as a purposeful act, refuting the Mu'tazila's interpretation of lam as mere consequence.

Examine your heart: do you trust that His will is entirely just and wise, even when it includes allowing sin to increase? What does this mean for your own sins—are they also under His purposeful will? How should this change your attitude towards repentance?
?

Sit with that discomfort: is it based on your own sense of fairness, or on submission to the Quran? What practical step can you take to align your heart with the truth that God's will is perfect, even when it includes what we perceive as evil?

Why the Lam of Consequence Does Not Apply to God
Al-Nasafi refutes the use of lam al-aqiba for divine actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the lam of consequence (lam al-aqiba) cannot be applied to God's actions, based on the argument that it presupposes ignorance of outcomes.

Core Idea
The lam of consequence only works for the ignorant; God knows all outcomes, so its use is unsound.

Al-Nasafi here tackles a subtle linguistic-theological issue: the Quranic use of the 'lam of consequence' in verses like 'We have created for Hell…'. The Mu'tazila argued this shows God's will produces consequences without direct intention. Al-Nasafi refutes this by analyzing the logic behind the lam of consequence: it only makes sense for someone unaware of future outcomes. Since God knows all, the lam is inapplicable. In this lesson, we will follow his argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the examples given and the logical flow of the refutation.
Pay attention to the examples of the child's death and the house ruin.
2
Identify the core logic
The author argues that lam al-aqiba (the lam of consequence) is used when someone desires a consequence but the actual outcome is different. This only applies if the person is ignorant of the true outcome.
3
Understand the farmer analogy
The analogy of the farmer who sows knowing the crop won't grow illustrates the absurdity of using lam al-aqiba for an omniscient being. A farmer who knows the seed won't grow would be foolish to expect growth.
This strengthens the point that ignorance is necessary for the lam of consequence to make sense.
4
Apply to God
Since God is the Knower of the Unseen (علام الغيوب), He cannot be ignorant of consequences. Therefore, using lam al-aqiba for His actions is logically unsound.
This refutes the Mu'tazili interpretation of certain Quranic verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عاقبة أمر الولد الموت وعاقبة أمر الدار الخراب يقال : هذا لبيان العاقبة. الرد على زعمهم والجواب إنما يتصور أن يريد أحد من الآخر ما لا يكون منه، ويفعل فعلاً له ولا يكون هو بل يكون غيره أو ضده، إن كان الفاعل جاهلاً بالعواقب فيفعل فعلاً لغرض فلا يحصل ذلك بل يحصل غيره، فأما من لا يجهل العواقب كيف يريد عاقبة لفعل يعلم حقيقة أنها لا تكون على ما يريد؟ وهل يفعل هذا إلا سفيه؟ ومثله في الشاهد كمن يبذر بذرا في الأرض ويريد خروج الزرع وحصول النماء، علمه أنه لا ينبت ولا يخرج، وكذا استعمال لام العاقبة فمن يجوز عليه الجهل بالعواقب فيفعل فعلاً لقصد فيحصل له ضد تلك العاقبة، ولا يحصل له ما هو المقصود، فيقال على طريق تعريف عاقبة فعله الوخيمة الحاصلة لا على وفق قصده ليتنبه لذلك فلا يعود إلى العمل بمثله فيما يستأنف من عمره، فأما في حق من هو علام الغيوب فاستعماله فاسد والله الموفق.
The consequence of the child's affair is death, and the consequence of the house's affair is ruin. It is said: this is to express the consequence. The response to their claim: The answer is that it is only conceivable that one wants from another what does not come from him, and does an act for him that does not happen but rather something else or its opposite occurs, if the doer is ignorant of consequences, so he does an act for a purpose, but that purpose is not achieved, rather something else happens. As for one who is not ignorant of consequences, how can he desire a consequence for an act while he knows truly that it will not be as he wants? And does anyone do this except a fool? An example in the witnessed world is like one who sows seeds in the ground and desires the growth of the crop and the attainment of vegetation, while knowing that it will not grow nor come forth. Likewise, the use of the lam of consequence is only for one who is subject to ignorance of consequences, so he does an act for a purpose, then the opposite of that consequence occurs for him, and what was intended does not occur, so it is said by way of informing of the grave consequence of his act that occurs not in accordance with his intention, so that he may take heed and not repeat the like of it in what remains of his life. As for the case of One who is the Knower of the Unseen, its use is unsound. And God is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili claim that certain Quranic verses (e.g. 'And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind') use the lam of consequence (لام العاقبة), meaning that the act results in a certain consequence without being directly intended. The author first gives an example of lam al-aqiba: the death of a child and the ruin of a house are consequences, not necessarily intended purposes. Then he presents the response: the lam of consequence is only valid for someone who is ignorant of future outcomes. A person may act with a purpose but get the opposite result, so he is warned about the bad consequence. However, for God, who knows all outcomes perfectly, it is impossible for Him to intend a consequence that He knows will not occur, as that would be foolish. The author illustrates with a farmer who sows knowing the seed won't grow; such an act is irrational. Thus, applying lam al-aqiba to God is unsound. This refutation upholds the principle that God's will is directly effective and not subject to ignorance.

Key Terms

عاقبة
جاهل بالعواقب
علام الغيوب
سفيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Quranic verses using the lam of consequence (e.g., 'We created for Hell') imply that God's will is not directly effective, or that He intends one thing but another happens.
Correction: The lam of consequence is a human linguistic device used when the doer is ignorant of outcomes. Since God knows all, such a device is inapplicable to Him. God's will is always effective and directly related to His intention.

Checkpoint

B) Because it is not found in the Quran
C) Because it contradicts free will

Key Takeaway
The lam of consequence is fundamentally about ignorance; since God is omniscient, it cannot be applied to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Divine Guidance and Misguidance in the Quran
Al-Nasafi explains the verse and refutes two Mu'tazili objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi interprets the verse on guidance and misguidance, and why he rejects Mu'tazili reinterpretations as reversing the causal order.

Core Idea
God's will directly creates the chest condition, which then causes faith or disbelief.

This lesson examines a key Quranic verse on divine guidance and misguidance from Surah al-An'am. Imam al-Nasafi explains the literal meaning and then refutes two Mu'tazili objections that try to reinterpret the verse as describing reward and punishment. The core issue is whether the verse describes God creating the cause of faith/disbelief or responding to human actions.

Guided Path
1
Recite the verse and its explanation
Read the verse and Al-Nasafi's commentary explaining the causal link between chest condition and faith/disbelief.
Pay attention to the words 'sabab' (cause) and 'hukm' (ruling).
2
Identify the causal sequence
Note that expansion of the chest precedes faith, and constriction precedes disbelief. This is the order taught by the verse.
3
Examine al-Ka'bi's objection
Al-Ka'bi claimed the verse describes reward after submission. Understand how he reversed the order and why Al-Nasafi calls it distortion.
Al-Nasafi argues that the verse makes the chest condition the cause (ʿilla) of the spiritual state, not the result.
4
See the Basrian objection and final refutation
The Basrians claimed 'guidance' means 'showing the way' and 'misguidance' means 'labeling'. Al-Nasafi dismisses this as extremely corrupt.
This rejection is based on the literal wording of the verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال تعالى : ( فَمَن يُرِدِ اللهُ أَن يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلْإِسْلَامِ وَمَن يُرِدْ أَن يُضِلَهُ يَجْعَلْ صَدْرَهُ ضَيِّقًا حَرَجًا ) فجعل شرح الصدر سببًا يحصل به الإيمان، وضيق الصدر وحرجه سببًا يحصل به الضلال وأخبر أنه متى أراد أن يهدي إنسانا يشرح صدره ليهتدي، ومتى أراد ضلاله يجعل صدره ضيقًا حرجًا لئلا يؤمن فيبقى على الكفر. والكعبي لتحيره اعترض بما كانت المداواة أولى به لو كان ظن هذا الاعتراض صحيحًا فقال : معنى الآية أن من أسلم آتاه الله من لطائف ما لا يقدر عليه غيره ثوابًا لطاعته، ومن كفر ضيق الله صدره عقابًا لذلك، وهو تحريف للكتاب ونقل للكلم عن مواضعها وليس بتأويل وذلك لأن الله تعالى أثبت له الإسلام إذا شرح صدره، والكفر إذا صير قلبه ضيقًا حرجًا ولم يوجب شرح القلب لأنه أسلم، ولا ضيق القلب لأنه لم يؤمن، فكان ما قاله جعل ما هو جار مجرى الحكم علة، وجعل ما هو جار مجرى العلة حكما وفساده لا يخفى. واعتراض البصريين أنه أراد بالهداية البيان وبالإضلال التسمية فاسد جدًا.
And He said, exalted: 'Whomever God wants to guide, He expands his breast for Islam; and whomever He wants to misguide, He makes his breast narrow and constrained.' He made the expansion of the breast a cause by which faith is attained, and the narrowness and constraint of the breast a cause by which misguidance is attained. And He informed that whenever He wants to guide someone, He expands his breast so that he may be guided, and whenever He wants his misguidance, He makes his breast narrow and constrained so that he may not believe and thus remain upon disbelief. And al-Ka'bi, due to his confusion, objected with what would have been more appropriate for him to treat [himself] if this objection had been correct, saying: The meaning of the verse is that whoever submits, God gives him from the subtleties that no one else can give as a reward for his obedience, and whoever disbelieves, God tightens his breast as a punishment for that. This is distortion of the Book and displacement of words from their places, not interpretation. That is because God, exalted, established Islam for him when He expanded his breast, and disbelief when He made his heart narrow and constrained, and He did not make the expansion of the heart because he submitted, nor the narrowness of the heart because he did not believe. So what he said makes what is at the level of the ruling a cause, and makes what is at the level of the cause a ruling, and its corruption is not hidden. And the objection of the Basrians that He intended by guidance the explanation and by misguidance the naming is extremely corrupt.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is an exegetical and polemical section where al-Nasafi explains a key Quranic verse (al-An'am 6:125) and refutes two Mu'tazili misinterpretations. He first explains the verse literally: God's will directly causes either expansion or constriction of the chest, and these are the effective causes (sabab) for attaining faith or remaining in disbelief. He emphasizes the causal sequence: expansion precedes faith, constriction precedes disbelief. Then he addresses al-Ka'bi's objection, which claims the verse describes reward and punishment after the act. Al-Nasafi refutes this by noting that the verse makes the chest condition the cause (ʿilla) of the spiritual state, not the result. Al-Ka'bi reversed the order, making the cause a reward. Finally, he dismisses the Basrian objection that 'guidance' and 'misguidance' mean merely 'showing the way' and 'labeling as misguided' as completely corrupt. The passage defends the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that God creates both guidance and misguidance directly, and the human response is consequent upon divine causation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse describes God rewarding faith with expansion and punishing disbelief with constriction.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the verse makes expansion the cause that leads to faith, not the result of it.

C) By saying that the verse is metaphorical and not to be taken literally
D) By citing the consensus of the early Muslims

Key Takeaway
The verse teaches that God directly creates the chest condition which brings about faith or disbelief; any reinterpretation that reverses this causal order is a distortion.

?
If your faith is ultimately caused by God's prior will and action, what does that mean for your pride in your own deeds? How can you live each moment with deeper reliance on Him, knowing that even the condition of your heart is in His hands?

The Dialectical Refutation of Iḍlāl as Mere Naming
The author interrogates the view that iḍlāl is mere naming, exposing its contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a dialectical argument to refute the Mu'tazilite claim that divine misguidance is merely a judicial declaration, exposing that it either concedes the necessary truth or leads to the absurdity of God willing falsehood.

Core Idea
If God names someone astray without causing misguidance, His naming would be false — an impossibility.

This passage presents a powerful dialectical exchange. The author takes the Mu'tazilite interpretation of 'yuḍillahu' as 'He names him astray' and presses it to its logical conclusion. Through a series of questions, he forces the opponent to either accept that God wills actual misguidance or face an absurd consequence: that God could will to be false in His naming. Let's trace the steps of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the dialectical structure. Note that it is a hypothetical dialogue with the opponent.
2
Identify the two branches
The author poses a key question: does God will the person's actual misguidance? The answer leads to two branches: 'yes' (concession) or 'no' (further questioning).
3
Follow the 'no' branch to its conclusion
If God wills only naming but not misguidance, then if guidance occurs, is the naming truthful? If yes, the person is actually astray (contradiction). If no, God willed falsehood (absurdity).
4
Reflect on the method
Notice the author's use of necessary consequences to refute a position. This is a classic kalam dialectical technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: لما كان معنى قوله: (وَمَن يُرِدْ أَن يُضِلَّهُ) أي من يرد أن يسميه ضالاً كانت تسميته ضالاً داخله تحت الإرادة، فبعد ذلك نقول: إذا أراد أن يسميه ضالاً، هل يريد ضلالته؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، تركوا مذهبهم وانقادوا للحق، وإن قالوا: لا، قيل لهم: إذا أراد تسميته ضالاً ولم يرد ضلالة بل أراد اهتداءه، فلو حصل ما أراد وهو الاهتداء ولم يحصل ما لم يرد وهو الضلال، أكان في تسميته ضالاً صادقا أم كاذبًا؟ فإن قالوا: كان صادقًا ظهرت مكابرتهم، وإن قالوا: كان كاذبًا، قيل: كان إذا مريدًا كونه كاذبًا في تسميته ضالاً وهو سفه – جل الله تعالى عن ذلك.
Then we say to them: Since the meaning of His statement, 'And whoever He wants to lead astray (yuḍillahu),' is 'whoever He wants to name astray,' his naming as astray enters under His will. After that we say: When He wants to name him astray, does He will his going-astray (ḍalālah)? If they say, 'Yes,' they have abandoned their doctrine and submitted to the truth. And if they say, 'No,' it is said to them: When He wants to name him astray and does not will misguidance but rather wills his guidance, then if what He wills—namely guidance—occurs and what He does not will—namely misguidance—does not occur, is He truthful or false in naming him astray? If they say, 'He was truthful,' their obstinacy becomes evident. And if they say, 'He was false,' it is said: Then He willed that He be false in naming him astray, which is foolishness—exalted is God above that.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of the Mu'tazilite interpretation of divine misguidance (iḍlāl) as merely naming or judging someone to be astray without actually causing misguidance. The author engages in a hypothetical dialogue: The opponent claims that 'yuḍillahu' means 'He names him astray.' The author asks: Does God will the person's actual misguidance (ḍalālah)? If they say yes, they abandon their position. If they say no, then God wills only to name him astray while willing his guidance. But if guidance occurs (as willed) and misguidance does not, then calling him astray would be either truthful or false. If truthful, the person is actually astray, contradicting their claim. If false, then God would be willing falsehood, which is foolishness (safah) and impossible for God. Thus the Mu'tazilite position leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
إضلال
إرادة
تسمية
ضلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine 'iḍlāl' in the Qur'an means only a judicial declaration, not an act of causing misguidance.
Correction: The verse implies that God's will is effective in causing misguidance, as shown by the absurdity of the alternative: if iḍlāl were mere naming, either the naming would be false (God wills falsehood) or the person is actually astray, contradicting the claim.

Checkpoint
What absurdity does the author expose if someone claims that God's 'iḍlāl' means only naming someone astray without willing actual misguidance?
A) It would make God's will ineffective.
B) It would imply God could be false in His naming.
C) It would contradict the verse about Noah.
D) It would mean God is ignorant of the future.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite interpretation of iḍlāl as mere naming leads to an inescapable contradiction: either God wills actual misguidance (conceding the point) or He wills to be false (an impossibility).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'And whoever He wants to lead astray' (Qur'an 6:125), do you immediately try to rationalize them to fit your own sense of fairness, or do you let them remind you of God's absolute sovereignty? This passage exposes the danger of reinterpreting God's words to avoid theological discomfort. Where in your heart do you resist submitting to the full weight of what God has said?
?
Imagine standing before God on the Day of Judgment, and He asks you: 'Did you accept My words as they are, or did you twist them to fit your own understanding?' This lesson shows that even well-intentioned reinterpretations can lead to attributing foolishness to God. What would it mean for you today to surrender your intellect to the text, even when it challenges your assumptions?
?
The dialectic forces a choice: either God causes misguidance (which might trouble your sense of justice) or God could be false (which is impossible). Yet the Qur'an affirms both God's justice and His absolute will. Have you ever tried to resolve this tension by denying one side? How does this passage call you to trust that God's ways are higher than your own, without compromising His words?

Refuting Mu'tazilite Interpretation of God's Will to Misguide
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite interpretation of verse 11:34 on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite interpretation of Quran 11:34 is logically inconsistent with the actual outcome.

Core Idea
If God cannot will misguidance, Noah's statement becomes meaningless — but history shows it did.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi takes on a Mu'tazilite interpretation of a key verse from Surah Hud. The Mu'tazilites claim that the verse only presents a conditional without affirming that God actually wills to misguide. Nasafi dismantles this by showing the logical consequences and how they contradict the historical reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and Mu'tazilite claim
First, read the Quranic verse and then the Mu'tazilite interpretation. Note how they acknowledge the conditional but deny that God actually wills misguidance.
The verse is from Noah's address to his people.
2
Understand the logical argument
Nasafi argues that if the condition (God willing misguidance) were impossible, then linking the negation of benefit to it would make the negation impossible, thus affirming benefit. Grasp this conditional logic.
This involves the principle that linking a negation to an impossible condition affirms the opposite.
3
Apply historical reality
Consider that Noah's advice did not benefit his people; he said this out of despair. This shows the condition was actual, not impossible.
4
Conclude the refutation
The contradiction proves the Mu'tazilite interpretation is corrupt. See how Nasafi's reasoning forces the conclusion that God indeed wills misguidance for the disbelievers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال تعالى خبرًا عن نوح عليه السلام حيث قال لقومه: ﴿ وَلَا يَنفَعُكُمْ نُصْحِي إِنْ أَرَدتُ أَنْ أَنصَحَ لَكُمْ إِن كَانَ اللهُ يُرِيدُ أَن يُغْوِيَكُمْ ﴾ ذكر أن الله يريد أن يغوي الكفرة، وتأويل المعتزلة أن في الآية أن نصحة لا ينفع إن كان الله يريد أن يغويهم وليس فيه أنه يريد، تأويل فاسد، لأنه إذا كان يستحيل منه إرادة غوايتهم أي فائدة في ذكر الآية على الأمر، لو كان كما يزعمون لكان ينبغي أن يكون مراد نوح عليه السلام من هذه الآية بيان أن نصحه ينفعهم لا محالة، لأن فيه تعليق نفي نفع النصح بما يستحيل ثبوته وهو إرادة إغوائهم، وتعليق النفي بما يستحيل ثبوته يكون تأكيدًا للإثبات، ويصير كأنه قال : لا ينفعكم نصحي، إذا كان الله يريد أن يغويكم فإذا استحال أن يريد أن يغويكم ينفعكم نصحي وحيث رأينا أنه كان لا ينفعهم وإنما قال ذلك يأسا من نفع نصحه لهم عند إرادة الله أن يغويهم، فكان ذلك دليلاً على فساد التأويل.
And He, exalted, said, reporting from Noah, peace be upon him, when he said to his people: "And my advice will not profit you if I intended to advise you, if God intends to misguide you." He mentioned that God intends to misguide the disbelievers. And the interpretation of the Mu'tazilites is that in the verse, his advice does not benefit if God intends to misguide them, and it does not say that He intends it. A corrupt interpretation, because if it is impossible for Him to intend their misguidance, what benefit is there in mentioning the verse on the matter? If it were as they claim, then Noah's intended meaning from this verse should have been a statement that his advice necessarily benefits them, because it involves linking the negation of the benefit of advice to what is impossible to occur, namely His intending their misguidance. And linking negation to what is impossible to occur is an affirmation of the positive, and it becomes as if he said: "My advice will not benefit you if God intends to misguide you." So if it is impossible for Him to intend to misguide you, then my advice benefits you. And where we see that it did not benefit them, and he only said that out of despair of his advice benefiting them when God intended to misguide them, that is evidence of the corruption of the interpretation.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite interpretation of Quran 11:34. The author begins by quoting the verse in which Noah tells his people that his advice will not profit them if God intends to misguide them. He notes that the verse explicitly states that God intends to misguide the disbelievers. The Mu'tazilites, however, interpret the verse to mean that advice does not benefit if God intends misguidance, but they deny that God actually intends misguidance; they claim the verse only states a conditional without affirming the condition. The author argues that this interpretation is corrupt. He points out that if it is impossible for God to intend misguidance, then the conditional clause is impossible, and the overall statement becomes meaningless or even affirmative. If, as the Mu'tazilites claim, God cannot intend misguidance, then the condition is impossible, and the negation of benefit would be impossible, implying that benefit necessarily occurs. But the historical context shows that Noah's advice did not benefit his people; Noah spoke out of despair because God had indeed willed their misguidance. Thus, the actual situation confirms that God's will to misguide is real, and the Mu'tazilite interpretation is false.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
إغواء (ighwa')
تأويل (ta'wil)
استحالة (istihala)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse only states a conditional without affirming that God actually wills to misguide.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if the condition were impossible, the statement would affirm the opposite, but historical reality confirms the condition is real.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the Mu'tazilite claim that the verse only states a conditional without affirming God's will to misguide?
A) By showing that the conditional is impossible, so it would affirm the opposite, but the actual outcome contradicts that.
B) By quoting another verse that explicitly states God's will to misguide.
C) By appealing to consensus of the Salaf.
D) By arguing that Noah's prophecy would be false if he based his statement on an impossible condition.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite interpretation of Quran 11:34 collapses because it would make Noah's statement affirm the opposite of what actually happened.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Noah's advice did not benefit his people because God willed their misguidance, do you believe that you could ever be one of those whom God wills to misguide? What in your life today would be evidence that you are not?
?
If you knew for certain that God had willed misguidance for a specific person you know, would you still advise them? How does Noah's example challenge your attitude toward those who reject your advice?
?
Are there areas in your life where you treat God's guidance as if it were conditional or merely hypothetical? What would it mean to truly believe that God's will to guide or misguide is real and active, and how would that change your daily choices?

The Dilemma of Redefining 'Ighwa'
Nasafi refutes al-Jubba'i's interpretation of 'ighwa' as deprivation of reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jubba'i's redefinition of 'ighwa' as deprivation of reward leads to a logical and theological contradiction.

Core Idea
Redefining 'ighwa' as deprivation forces an impossible choice: either God acts unjustly or He abandons Mu'tazilism.

Al-Jubba'i, a prominent Mu'tazilite, attempted to avoid attributing misguidance to God by reinterpreting the word 'ighwa' in the Qur'an as meaning deprivation of reward. In this passage, Imam Nasafi dismantles this interpretation using a precise logical dilemma. He first shows the linguistic weakness, then traps al-Jubba'i between two untenable positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text once. Then read the literal translation. Notice the dialectical style: Nasafi presents al-Jubba'i's claim and then refutes it.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify al-Jubba'i's claim and the linguistic critique
Al-Jubba'i claims that 'ighwa' in the verse (Quran 7:16) means 'deprivation of reward'. The first part of the refutation points out that this meaning is unknown to Arabic lexicographers.
This establishes that the interpretation is linguistically baseless.
3
Follow the dilemma: the two horns
Nasafi asks: Does God intend to deprive them of reward while also intending their faith, or while intending their disbelief? Trace each horn—the first implies God intends injustice, the second contradicts Mu'tazilite theology.
Each horn leads to a problem for al-Jubba'i.
4
Understand the conclusion
Al-Jubba'i is forced either to attribute injustice to God (which he denies) or to abandon his school and accept that God wills disbelief for disbelievers. The argument is decisive.
This refutes the reinterpretation effectively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكره الجبائي أن معنى قوله: ﴿ يُرِيدُ أَن يُغْوِيَكُمْ ﴾ أي يحرمكم الثواب فاسد، لأن أهل اللغة لم يعرفوا الإغواء عبارة عن الحرمان والخيبة، على أنا نقول: الله تعالى على زعمكم - يريد حرمانهم عن الثواب ذلك وخيبتهم عن وهو لا يريد كفرهم أو يريد ذلك وهو يريد إيمانهم؟ فإن قال: يريد ذلك وهو يريد إيمانهم فقد أخبر أنه يرد الجور على زعمهم لأنه لو تحقق ما يريد بأن وجد منهم الإيمان وحرمهم عن الثواب وخيبهم عنه كان ذلك عنده ظلمًا، فإنه أراد ذلك فقد أراد ما هو ظلم، وهو يأبى أن يريد الله تعالى ما هو ظلم من غيره لقبح الظلم، فزعم أنه يريد الظلم من نفسه أنه مع قبيح، وهذه جهالة فاحشة. وإن قال: يريد ذلك وهو يريد كفرهم ترك مذهبه وانقاد للحق.
And what al-Jubba'i mentioned that the meaning of His saying: 'He intends to misguide you' is 'He deprives you of reward' is corrupt, because the people of language do not know 'ighwa' as an expression for deprivation and disappointment. Moreover, we say: God, exalted, according to your claim – He intends their deprivation of reward and their disappointment, and He does not intend their disbelief, or does He intend that while He intends their faith? If he says: He intends that while He intends their faith, then he has informed that He intends injustice according to their claim, because if what He intended were realized, in that He found faith from them and deprived them of reward and disappointed them, that would be injustice in his view. So if He intended that, He intended what is injustice. And he (al-Jubba'i) refuses that God, exalted, intends what is injustice from others due to the ugliness of injustice, so he claims that He intends injustice from Himself – it is ugly. And this is gross ignorance. And if he says: He intends that while He intends their disbelief, then he has abandoned his school and submitted to the truth.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage refutes the interpretation of al-Jubba'i, a leading Mu'tazilite, who claimed that the word 'ighwa' (misguidance) in the verse means 'deprivation of reward' (hirmān al-thawāb). The author begins by noting that this meaning is not linguistically attested; the Arabic language does not use 'ighwa' for deprivation. Then he presents a dialectical argument. He asks al-Jubba'i: According to your claim, does God intend to deprive them of reward while also intending their faith, or while intending their disbelief? If al-Jubba'i says God intends deprivation while intending their faith, then that would mean God intends to treat the believers unjustly by depriving them of reward despite their faith. Al-Jubba'i himself holds that injustice is ugly and that God does not do ugly acts. Therefore, he would be claiming that God intends what is ugly, which he himself rejects. If instead al-Jubba'i says God intends deprivation while intending their disbelief, then he has abandoned his Mu'tazilite position (which holds that God does not create disbelief) and conceded to the truth (i.e., the Sunni position that God wills disbelief for the disbelievers). Thus, either way, al-Jubba'i's interpretation leads to a contradiction or a concession to the opposing view.

Key Terms
إغواء (ighwa')
ثواب (thawab)
حرمان (hirmān)
ظلم (zulm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'ighwa' can be reinterpreted metaphorically as deprivation of reward without theological consequences.
Correction: Nasafi shows that this reinterpretation leads to a contradiction: either God acts unjustly by depriving believers, or He must will disbelief, which al-Jubba'i's own school denies.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does the author pose to al-Jubba'i regarding the relationship between God's will to deprive of reward and His will for the person's faith or disbelief?
A) If God intends deprivation while intending faith, that is unjust; if while intending disbelief, it contradicts Mu'tazilite doctrine.
B) If God intends deprivation, He must also intend disbelief, which is impossible.
C) Al-Jubba'i's interpretation is simply a linguistic error with no theological implications.
D) The verse clearly states that God intends misguidance, not deprivation.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's reinterpretation of 'ighwa' fails because it either forces God to will injustice or contradicts his own theological principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever reinterpreted a clear religious text to avoid a conclusion that discomforts you? What was at stake, and did it lead to a contradiction in your beliefs?
?
Al-Jubba'i's dilemma forced him to either accept that God wills injustice or abandon his school. Is there a belief you hold that, when pushed to its logical conclusion, forces you to concede something you resist? Will you examine it honestly today?
?
This lesson shows that redefining terms can trap you in a logical impossibility. In your own life, when you face a tension between a cherished idea and what the sources clearly say, will you submit to the truth or invent a false escape? Make a concrete commitment to choose truth this week.
