Refuting al-Jubba'i on Divine Will Leading Astray
Nasafi refutes al-Jubba'i's interpretation using a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses a dilemma to refute al-Jubba'i's claim that 'ighwa' means deprivation of reward, showing that it leads to either injustice or abandoning Mu'tazili doctrine.

Core Idea
God's will cannot be interpreted in a way that implies injustice or contradiction.

Al-Jubba'i, a leading Mu'tazili, interpreted the verse 'He intends to lead you astray' as meaning God deprives them of reward. Nasafi argues that this interpretation is linguistically unsupported and then traps al-Jubba'i in a dilemma: if God intends to deprive them of reward, does He also intend their faith or their disbelief? Either answer leads to a problem for the Mu'tazili framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Nasafi's argument.
2
Understand al-Jubba'i's claim
Al-Jubba'i claimed that 'yughwiyakum' means deprivation of reward. Nasafi first rejects this on linguistic grounds.
3
Follow the dilemma
Nasafi presents two options: if God intends to deprive them while intending their faith, that is injustice; if while intending their disbelief, it contradicts Mu'tazili principles.
Both options are problematic for al-Jubba'i.
4
See the conclusion
Thus al-Jubba'i's interpretation is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكره الجبائي أن معنى قوله: ﴿ يُرِيدُ أَن يُغْوِيَكُمْ ﴾ أي يحرمكم الثواب فاسد، لأن أهل اللغة لم يعرفوا الإغواء عبارة عن الحرمان والخيبة، على أنا نقول: الله تعالى على زعمكم - يريد حرمانهم عن الثواب ذلك وخيبتهم عن وهو لا يريد كفرهم أو يريد ذلك وهو يريد إيمانهم؟ فإن قال: يريد ذلك وهو يريد إيمانهم فقد أخبر أنه يرد الجور على زعمهم لأنه لو تحقق ما يريد بأن وجد منهم الإيمان وحرمهم عن الثواب وخيبهم عنه كان ذلك عنده ظلمًا، فإنه أراد ذلك فقد أراد ما هو ظلم، وهو يأبى أن يريد الله تعالى ما هو ظلم من غيره لقبح الظلم، فزعم أنه يريد الظلم من نفسه أنه قبيح، وهذه جهالة فاحشة. مع قبيح، وإن قال: يريد ذلك وهو يريد كفرهم ترك مذهبه وانقاد للحق، وكذا تأويل غيرهم أن المراد بقوله: ﴿ يُرِيدُ أَن يُغْوِيَكُمْ ﴾ أي يعاقبكم، وألغى يذكر ويراد به العذاب. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ فَسَوْفَ يَلْقَوْنَ غَيّا ﴾ أي عذابا . وجوابه كجواب تأويل الجبائي أنه يريد أن يعاقبهم وهو يريد منهم الكفر أو يريد أن يعاقبهم وهو يريد منهم الإيمان؟ فبأي الجوابين أجاب فالكلام على ما ذكرنا
And what al-Jubba'i mentioned — that the meaning of His saying, 'He intends to lead you astray' is that He deprives them of reward — is corrupt, because the people of language did not know that al-ighwa' (leading astray) is an expression for deprivation and disappointment. Moreover, we say: Allah Almighty — according to your claim — intends to deprive them of reward and to disappoint them, while He does not intend their disbelief, or does He intend that while He intends their faith? If he says: 'He intends that while He intends their faith,' then he has informed that He commits injustice according to their claim, because if what He intends were realized — that faith occurs from them and He deprives them of reward and disappoints them — that would be, in his view, injustice. Since He intended that, He intended what is injustice, and He refuses that Allah intends what is injustice from another due to the ugliness of injustice. So he claims that He intends injustice from Himself, while it is ugly. This is gross ignorance. Along with ugliness. And if he says: 'He intends that while He intends their disbelief,' then he abandons his doctrine and submits to the truth. Similarly, the interpretation of others that the intended meaning of His saying, 'He intends to lead you astray' is that He punishes them — and they say 'ghayy' and it means punishment. Allah Almighty said, 'Then they will meet ghayya,' i.e., punishment. And its answer is like the answer to al-Jubba'i's interpretation: that He intends to punish them while He intends from them disbelief, or does He intend to punish them while He intends from them faith? Whichever of the two answers he gives, the discussion is as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage refutes two Mu'tazili interpretations of the verse 'يريد أن يغويكم' (He intends to lead you astray). First, al-Jubba'i's interpretation that it means deprivation of reward. Nasafi argues this is linguistically invalid because 'ighwa' does not mean deprivation. Then he presents a dilemma: if God intends to deprive them of reward, does He intend their faith or their disbelief? If He intends their faith, then according to Mu'tazili principles, depriving them of reward after faith would be unjust, so God would be intending injustice, which is impossible. If He intends their disbelief, then the Mu'tazili abandons his own doctrine (since they claim God does not intend disbelief). Then the second interpretation: that 'ighwa' means punishment. The same dilemma applies: if God punishes them, does He intend their faith or disbelief? Either way leads to contradiction. Thus both interpretations are refuted.

Key Terms
إغواء (leading astray)
حرمان (deprivation)
ظلم (injustice)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'ighwa' in the verse means deprivation of reward, as claimed by al-Jubba'i.
Correction: Linguistically, 'ighwa' does not mean deprivation. Moreover, this interpretation leads to a dilemma: either God intends injustice or contradicts His own nature.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of Nasafi's dilemma against al-Jubba'i?
A) God intends their faith and deprives them of reward, which is unjust.
B) God intends their disbelief and deprives them of reward, which contradicts Mu'tazili doctrine.
C) The word 'ighwa' has multiple meanings.
D) Al-Jubba'i was not a real Mu'tazili.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation that 'ighwa' means deprivation of reward is invalid because it leads to either injustice or contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever reinterpreted a clear command of Allah to fit your desires, just as al-Jubba'i redefined 'ighwa'? In what area of your life are you most tempted to twist the meaning of God's words to avoid accountability?
?
The passage forces you to confront that God cannot intend injustice. Think of a recent hardship or disappointment. Do you truly believe God was not unjust to you in that moment? If yes, what would change in how you respond to trials?
?
Nasafi's dilemma shows that any attempt to make God conform to human logic about justice fails if it denies His attributes. What personal belief about God's justice are you holding onto that might actually be a subtle form of judging Him by your own standards? Will you submit to His inscrutable will today?

Refuting the Punishment Interpretation of Divine Will
Nasafi refutes the punishment interpretation using the same dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the same dilemma used against al-Jubba'i also refutes the claim that 'ighwa' means punishment.

Core Idea
The same dilemma disproves the interpretation of 'ighwa' as punishment.

Another Mu'tazili interpretation claims that 'yughwiyakum' means God punishes them, citing the verse 'Then they will meet ghayya' where ghayya means punishment. Nasafi points out that the same dilemma applies: if God punishes them, does He intend their faith or disbelief? Both lead to contradictions.

Guided Path

Some Mu'tazilis said 'ighwa' means punishment, using a linguistic argument from the word 'ghayy' meaning punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكره الجبائي أن معنى قوله: ﴿ يُرِيدُ أَن يُغْوِيَكُمْ ﴾ أي يحرمكم الثواب فاسد، لأن أهل اللغة لم يعرفوا الإغواء عبارة عن الحرمان والخيبة، على أنا نقول: الله تعالى على زعمكم - يريد حرمانهم عن الثواب ذلك وخيبتهم عن وهو لا يريد كفرهم أو يريد ذلك وهو يريد إيمانهم؟ فإن قال: يريد ذلك وهو يريد إيمانهم فقد أخبر أنه يرد الجور على زعمهم لأنه لو تحقق ما يريد بأن وجد منهم الإيمان وحرمهم عن الثواب وخيبهم عنه كان ذلك عنده ظلمًا، فإنه أراد ذلك فقد أراد ما هو ظلم، وهو يأبى أن يريد الله تعالى ما هو ظلم من غيره لقبح الظلم، فزعم أنه يريد الظلم من نفسه أنه قبيح، وهذه جهالة فاحشة. مع قبيح، وإن قال: يريد ذلك وهو يريد كفرهم ترك مذهبه وانقاد للحق، وكذا تأويل غيرهم أن المراد بقوله: ﴿ يُرِيدُ أَن يُغْوِيَكُمْ ﴾ أي يعاقبكم، وألغى يذكر ويراد به العذاب. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ فَسَوْفَ يَلْقَوْنَ غَيّا ﴾ أي عذابا . وجوابه كجواب تأويل الجبائي أنه يريد أن يعاقبهم وهو يريد منهم الكفر أو يريد أن يعاقبهم وهو يريد منهم الإيمان؟ فبأي الجوابين أجاب فالكلام على ما ذكرنا
And what al-Jubba'i mentioned — that the meaning of His saying, 'He intends to lead you astray' is that He deprives them of reward — is corrupt, because the people of language did not know that al-ighwa' (leading astray) is an expression for deprivation and disappointment. Moreover, we say: Allah Almighty — according to your claim — intends to deprive them of reward and to disappoint them, while He does not intend their disbelief, or does He intend that while He intends their faith? If he says: 'He intends that while He intends their faith,' then he has informed that He commits injustice according to their claim, because if what He intends were realized — that faith occurs from them and He deprives them of reward and disappoints them — that would be, in his view, injustice. Since He intended that, He intended what is injustice, and He refuses that Allah intends what is injustice from another due to the ugliness of injustice. So he claims that He intends injustice from Himself, while it is ugly. This is gross ignorance. Along with ugliness. And if he says: 'He intends that while He intends their disbelief,' then he abandons his doctrine and submits to the truth. Similarly, the interpretation of others that the intended meaning of His saying, 'He intends to lead you astray' is that He punishes them — and they say 'ghayy' and it means punishment. Allah Almighty said, 'Then they will meet ghayya,' i.e., punishment. And its answer is like the answer to al-Jubba'i's interpretation: that He intends to punish them while He intends from them disbelief, or does He intend to punish them while He intends from them faith? Whichever of the two answers he gives, the discussion is as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage refutes two Mu'tazili interpretations of the verse 'يريد أن يغويكم' (He intends to lead you astray). First, al-Jubba'i's interpretation that it means deprivation of reward. Nasafi argues this is linguistically invalid because 'ighwa' does not mean deprivation. Then he presents a dilemma: if God intends to deprive them of reward, does He intend their faith or their disbelief? If He intends their faith, then according to Mu'tazili principles, depriving them of reward after faith would be unjust, so God would be intending injustice, which is impossible. If He intends their disbelief, then the Mu'tazili abandons his own doctrine (since they claim God does not intend disbelief). Then the second interpretation: that 'ighwa' means punishment. The same dilemma applies: if God punishes them, does He intend their faith or disbelief? Either way leads to contradiction. Thus both interpretations are refuted.

Key Terms
عذاب (punishment)
غيّ (ghayy, meaning punishment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse means God intends to punish them, and this solves the problem of divine will leading astray.
Correction: The same dilemma applies: if God punishes them, does He intend their faith or disbelief? Both cause problems for Mu'tazili theology.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi refute the interpretation that 'ighwa' means punishment?
A) By saying the word does not mean punishment at all.
B) By applying the same dilemma about intention.
C) By citing another verse.
D) By accepting the interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation that 'ighwa' means punishment is refuted by the same dilemma, showing its inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that some interpret 'God leads astray' as mere punishment, does your heart feel safe from the idea that God might punish you even when you strive to obey? Or do you realize how much you depend on His mercy to even want faith?
?
If God's will and your will ever seem to conflict—like when you struggle with a sin you don't want but commit—where do you turn for assurance: to your own reasoning or to the infinite wisdom of Allah's decree?
?
This dilemma forces you to admit that your understanding of justice is limited. Are you willing to submit to the fact that Allah's actions are beyond your complete comprehension, even when they appear unjust by your measure?

Refuting the Mu'tazili Interpretation of 'Fitna'
Nasafi refutes a Mu'tazili interpretation of 'fitna' and dismisses their objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of 'fitna' in the verse and why Nasafi dismisses Mu'tazili objections as corrupt.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili interpretation of 'fitna' as obligation is refuted by the Prophet's example.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a specific verse and the Mu'tazili misinterpretation of it. He clarifies that 'fitna' refers to punishment or severe trial, not simply religious obligation, and that the Prophet's attitude towards obligation disproves their view. He then dismisses their broader objections as unworthy of response.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its interpretations
Begin by reading the Quranic verse and the two interpretations Nasafi presents for 'fitna'. Notice that he mentions both a punishment interpretation and a testing-by-stoning interpretation before addressing the Mu'tazili view.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili view and refutation
Locate where Nasafi states the Mu'tazili interpretation (that 'fitna' means obligation/testing) and then find his refutation based on the Prophet's lack of distress.
3
Consider the supporting verse
Read the second verse Nasafi cites: 'Those are the ones for whom Allah did not intend to purify their hearts.' Consider how this supports his argument that 'fitna' implies divine will for punishment, not mere obligation.
4
Understand the dismissal of objections
Finally, note Nasafi's statement that the Mu'tazila have many objections but they are corrupt, and anyone with knowledge can see their falsehood. Reflect on why he considers them not worth engaging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يُرِدِ اللهُ فِتْنَتَهُ، فَلَن تَمْلِك لَهُ مِنَ اللَّهِ شَيْئًا ﴾ قيل: فتنته أي عذابه وقيل امتحانه بالرجم والقتل وإرادة ذلك بدون إرادة ما يستوجب به إرادة ظلم وتأويلهم أن قوله تعالى: "فتنته" أي تكليفه وامتحانه خطأ، لأن النبي عليه السلام ما كان يشق عليه ذلك، وما كان يسأل أن لا يكلف الله تعالى عباده وقال تعالى : ﴿ أُولَئِكَ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يُرِدِ اللهُ أَن يُطْهَرَ قُلُوبَهُمْ ﴾. وعند المعتزلة أراد ذلك وفي الآيات كثرة ، ولهم اعتراضات عليها فاسدة لا معنى للاشتغال بذلك لوقوف من له أدنى علم على بطلان تلك الاعتراضات، وقال
And Allah Almighty said, 'And whoever Allah intends his trial, you will not possess for him anything from Allah.' It was said: His trial means His punishment, and it was said: His testing by stoning and killing, and His intending that without intending what necessitates it would be intending injustice. And their interpretation that His saying, 'His trial,' means His obligation and testing is wrong, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, was not distressed by that, nor did he ask that Allah not oblige His servants. And Allah Almighty said, 'Those are the ones for whom Allah did not intend to purify their hearts.' And according to the Mu'tazila, He intended that, and there are many verses, and they have corrupt objections to them, and there is no meaning in occupying oneself with that because anyone with the least knowledge knows the falsehood of those objections. And he said...

Commentary
This passage discusses the interpretation of the verse 'ومن يرد الله فتنته'. It mentions two interpretations: trial as punishment or testing by stoning and killing. It argues that intending trial without intending what necessitates it would be injustice. It then refutes the interpretation that 'فتنته' means obligation and testing, saying it is wrong because the Prophet was not distressed by that. Then it cites the verse 'أولئك الذين لم يرد الله أن يطهر قلوبهم' and notes that the Mu'tazila claim God intended that, but their objections are corrupt and not worth refuting. The passage ends abruptly.

C) The verse explicitly says it means punishment.

Key Takeaway
The correct meaning of 'fitna' in the verse is punishment or severe trial, not obligation, and the Mu'tazili objections are too weak to require refutation.

?

?
The passage dismisses weak objections. Are there objections in your heart against Allah's decrees that you have been harboring, thinking you have an excuse? Will you repent from that and accept His absolute justice?

The Knowledge-Will Dilemma against the Mu'tazila
The author poses a dilemma to the Mu'tazila regarding God's knowledge and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational dilemma that forces the Mu'tazila to admit that God's will must align with His eternal knowledge, and why this exposes the corruption of their doctrine.

Core Idea
God's will cannot contradict His knowledge—otherwise He would will ignorance.

This passage presents a sharp logical dilemma aimed at the Mu'tazila regarding the relationship between God's knowledge and will. The author uses two successive questions to corner the opponent, showing that both denials lead to absurdity, while affirmation leads to the Sunni conclusion. Before we see the argument in detail, reflect on the necessity that a wise being cannot will something contrary to what He knows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two-part question structure and the quick moves between answers and their consequences.
The argument is very compact; each line carries a logical consequence.
2
Identify the two successive questions
First, the author asks about God's knowledge; then, based on the affirmative answer, asks about His will. Trace how the first answer sets up the second.
If the Mu'tazila deny knowledge, they commit kufr; if they affirm, they are forced into the second question.
3
Trace the consequences of each answer
For each possible answer to the second question, note the implication: denying will leads to God willing ignorance (absurd), affirming leads to agreement with the Sunni position.
Pay attention to the terms 'tajhil' and 'hakim'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion and its strength
The author concludes that this dilemma is necessary, rational, and shows the corruption of the Mu'tazila. Consider why this argument is considered strong and how it refutes their position.
The argument is attributed to Abu Hanifa, grounding it in early Sunni authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نسأل المعتزلة فنقول : هل علم الله تعالى ما يكون أبدًا على ما يكون؟ فإن قالوا: لا. كفروا، لأنهم جهلوا ربهم ، وإن قالوا: نعم، قيل لهم شاء أن ينفذ علمه كما علم أو لا؟ فإن قالوا: (لا ، قالوا) (۱) بأن الله تعالى شاء أن يكون جاهلاً، ومن شاء ذلك فليس بحكيم. وإن قالوا: نعم، أقروا بأنه شاء أن يكون كل شيء كما علم أن يكون، وهذا الذي أردناه فهذا هو المحكي عن أبي حنيفة، وهو لازم للقول، وهو المعقول القوي في المسألة، وبه يظهر غاية فساد مذهبهم.
We ask the Mu'tazila and say: Did God Most High know eternally what will be as it will be? If they say: No, they disbelieve, because they have made their Lord ignorant. And if they say: Yes, it is said to them: Did He will to execute His knowledge as He knew or not? And if they say: No, they say that God Most High willed to be ignorant, and whoever wills that is not wise. And if they say: Yes, they affirm that He willed everything to be as He knew it would be. This is what we intended, and this is what is related from Abu Hanifa, and it is necessary for the statement, and it is the strong rational argument in the matter, and by it the utmost corruption of their school appears.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument against the Mu'tazila regarding God's knowledge and will. The author begins by asking the Mu'tazila a question with two branches. First: Did God know eternally what will happen as it will happen? If the Mu'tazila deny this, they commit kufr because they attribute ignorance to God. If they affirm it, they are then asked: Did God will to execute His knowledge as He knew, or not? If they say He did not will to execute it, they imply that God willed to be ignorant, and such a will cannot belong to a wise being. If they say He did will to execute it, they agree that God willed everything to be exactly as He knew it would be. The author states that this conclusion is what the Sunni position intends, citing Imam Abu Hanifa, and argues that it is a necessary and strong rational argument that exposes the corruption of the Mu'tazili doctrine.

Key Terms
علم الله (God's knowledge)
شاء (will)
تجهيل (making ignorant)
حكيم (wise)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the dilemma is purely hypothetical and does not have real force.
Correction: The dilemma is a logical necessity: if God knows all things eternally, His will cannot contradict that knowledge, otherwise He would will ignorance, which is impossible for a wise being.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the Mu'tazila say that God did not will to execute His knowledge?
A) They admit God is wise.
B) They imply God willed to be ignorant.
C) They affirm God's justice.
D) They deny God's existence.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma forces the Mu'tazila to either accept God's ignorance (absurd) or affirm that His will aligns perfectly with His eternal knowledge, thereby refuting their doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems unjust or meaningless, do you truly believe that God knew it eternally and willed it to happen? Does that belief change how you respond to it, or do you still resent it? Ask yourself: Will I submit to His knowledge and will, or will I insist on my own understanding?
?
This lesson argues that it is absurd for God to will ignorance. But consider: In your own life, do you sometimes act as if you know better than God? When you wish something had happened differently, are you not implicitly saying that God's will was mistaken? Repent of that and affirm that His will is always in line with His perfect knowledge.
?
If you truly internalize that God's will perfectly executes His knowledge, does that free you from anxiety about the future? Or does it trouble you? Examine your heart: Are you at peace with God's decree, or are you still fighting it? Make a firm resolve today to trust His knowledge and will completely.

Proof from the Verse about Filling Hell
Nasafi uses Quran 32:13 to disprove Mu'tazila on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse about filling Hell is used to prove that God wills the disbelief of those who end up in Hell.

Core Idea
God's promise to fill Hell proves He wills disbelief.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents a second argument against the Mu'tazila, this time from a revealed text (khabar). Using Quran 32:13, he forces the opponent into a dilemma: either God willed His promise to be false (impossible) or He willed it to be true, which implies He wills disbelief. This argument is a classic example of kalam reasoning from scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the flow of argument.
The passage uses a series of questions to corner the opponent.
2
Identify the key dilemma
Notice the two branches: either God willed His report to be realized while people are believers or while they are disbelievers. Trace the consequences of each.
The first branch leads to attributing injustice to God, which is impossible.
3
Recognize the conclusion
The second branch forces the Mu'tazila to admit that God wills disbelief, which contradicts their own doctrine and aligns with the Sunni position.
The author concludes they have submitted to the truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خبر ثم نقول: تقرير هذا أيضا في الخبر فيقال: أليس أن الله تعالى قال: (لَأَمْلَأَنَّ جَهَنَّمَ مِنَ الْجِنَّةِ وَالنَّاسِ أَجْمَعِينَ ) (۲) فلابد من بلى قيل: هل يشاء أن يتحقق ؟ (۳) أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: لا، فقد زعموا أنه أراد أن يكون كاذبًا في مقالته إذ لا خلاف بينهم أن الكذب كما يتحقق في الماضي يتحقق في المستقبل إن كان بين أهل السنة فيه خلاف ومن هذا قوله فهو خالع ربقة الإسلام عن عنقه. وإن قالوا: نعم. قيل أيشاء أن يتحقق خبره وهم مؤمنون أم يشاء أن يتحقق خبره وهم كافرون؟ فإن قالوا يشاء أن يتحقق خبره و هم مؤمنون قيل : لو حقق خبره فيهم وهم مؤمنون، أكان عادلاً أم ظالمًا؟ فلابد من القول بأنه يكون ظالمًا قيل : فإن أراد شيئًا لو كان تحقق لكان ظالمًا فقد أراد ظلمًا وهو باطل. فإن قال شاء (٤) أن يحقق خبره فيهم وهم كافرون لأنهم شاء أن يتحقق خبره وهو عادل فقد تركوا مذهبهم وانقادوا للحق.
News. Then we say: A confirmation of this also from the report (khabar). It is said: Is it not that God Most High said: 'I will surely fill Hell with jinn and people all together' (Q 32:13)? So there must be a 'yes'. It is said: Did He will it to be realized or not? If they say: No, they have claimed that He intended to be false in His statement, since there is no disagreement among them that falsehood, just as it is realized in the past, is realized in the future—even if there is disagreement among Ahl al-Sunnah about it—and such a statement is one who removes the rope of Islam from his neck. And if they say: Yes, it is said: Did He will His report to be realized while they are believers, or did He will it to be realized while they are disbelievers? If they say He willed His report to be realized while they are believers, it is said: If He realized His report in them while they are believers, would He be just or unjust? There is no alternative but to say He would be unjust. It is said: So if He intended something whose realization would make Him unjust, then He intended injustice, and that is false. But if they say He willed to realize His report in them while they are disbelievers—because He willed His report to be realized and He is just—then they have abandoned their school and submitted to the truth.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a second argument against the Mu'tazila, this time based on a revealed text (khabar), specifically Quran 32:13 where God says He will fill Hell with jinn and people. The author asks: Did God will this to happen or not? If the Mu'tazila deny it, they are accusing God of lying, which removes one from Islam. If they affirm it, then they are asked: Did God will it to happen while the people are believers or while they are disbelievers? If they say believers, then if God fulfills His word while they are believers, He would be punishing believers unjustly, which is impossible. If they say disbelievers, then they admit that God wills the disbelief of those who end up in Hell, which means the Mu'tazila concede the Sunni position that God creates both belief and disbelief. The author concludes that the Mu'tazila have thereby abandoned their own doctrine and submitted to the truth.

Key Terms
الخبر (report/text)
الكذب (falsehood)
شاء (will)
ظلم (injustice)
حق (truth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will for disbelief contradicts His command to believe.
Correction: God's will for disbelief is distinct from His command; He wills disbelief for some while commanding all to believe. This does not imply contradiction.

Checkpoint
If the Mu'tazila say God willed His report to be realized while the people are believers, what follows?
A) God would be just.
B) God would be unjust.
C) God would be truthful.
D) God would be ignorant.

Key Takeaway
God's promise to fill Hell necessitates that He wills the disbelief of those who end up in Hell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn that God wills the disbelief of some, does it make you more fearful for yourself? Have you ever been complacent, assuming your belief is merely your own choice and not sustained by God's will?
?
If God's promise to fill Hell forces the conclusion that He wills disbelief, does that disturb your sense of fairness? Can you trust that God's wisdom is higher than your understanding of justice?
?
The Mu'tazila wanted to defend God's justice by denying He wills evil. But this passage shows that God's truthfulness is more fundamental. Which attribute of God do you tend to emphasize most—His justice, mercy, or power? Are you willing to submit to all of them even when they seem to conflict in your mind?

The Dilemma of Commanding the Known Disbeliever
Objection-and-answer on commanding the known disbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma regarding God's command to someone He knows will never believe, and how the author refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
God's command does not depend on His foreknowledge of the outcome.

This passage presents a classic objection in kalam: if God knows someone will never believe, does He still command them to believe? The author uses a reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazila, showing that their own premises lead to contradiction. We will follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the two horns of the dilemma presented in the objection.
The passage uses a 'if you say... then...' structure.
2
Identify the two options
The objection forces a choice: either God did not command the known disbeliever (Pharaoh, Abu Jahl) or He did. Analyze the consequences of each option as presented.
The first option leads to the absurd conclusion that they deserve no punishment.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila concession
The author claims that the Mu'tazila admit God commands 'what makes Him ignorant.' Find that phrase and see how it is used to trap them.
This is the key to the refutation.
4
See the author's counter
The author argues that if commanding the 'impossible' is permissible (by consensus), then willing it should also be permissible. Notice the rebuke at the end.
The text ends abruptly, but the point is made.

فإن قيل: من علم الله منه أنه لا يؤمن ويموت على الكفر، هل أمره بالإيمان ونهاه عن الكفر أم لا؟ فإن قلتم : لا . فقد زعمتم أن فرعون وأبا جهل ما كانا مأمورين بالإيمان ولا منهيين عن الكفر ، وفيه أنهما ما استحقا اللوم والتعذيب لأنهما ما تركا مأمورًا ولا ارتكبا منهيًا وهو كفر ، وإن قلتم : أمرهما بالإيمان ونهاهما عن الكفر . فقد قلتم : إنه أمرهما بتجهيل نفسه ونهاهما عما فيه تقرير علمه وهذا مما لا خلاف فيه، فإذا جاز بالإجماع أن يأمر بتجهيل نفسه وينهى عما فيه تقرير علمه، ولم يكن ذلك محالاً فلم يجوز أن يريد ما فيه تجهيل نفسه ولا يريد ما فيه تقرير علمه؟ بل يكره ذلك قلنا : أنتم معشر المعتزلة تريدون (1) أبدًا تصحيح المحال بما تصور عندكم من الجائزات أنه (۷) يظهر لجهلكم بحقائق المعاني، ولو عقلتم
For if it is said: Concerning one whom God knows will not believe and will die on disbelief, did He command him to believe and forbid him from disbelief or not? If you say: No, then you have claimed that Pharaoh and Abu Jahl were not commanded to believe nor forbidden from disbelief, and this implies that they do not deserve blame and punishment because they did not leave a command nor commit a prohibition—and that is disbelief. And if you say: He commanded them to believe and forbade them from disbelief, then you have said that He commanded them to make Him ignorant and forbade them from what confirms His knowledge—and this is uncontroversial. So if it is permissible by consensus that He command what makes Him ignorant and forbid what confirms His knowledge, and that is not impossible, then why is it not permissible that He will what makes Him ignorant and not will what confirms His knowledge? Rather, He detests that. We say: You, the community of Mu'tazila, always try to justify the impossible by what you conceive as possibilities, and that appears due to your ignorance of the true meanings of realities. If only you understood...
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The objection poses a dilemma about God's command to a known disbeliever. If the Mu'tazila say God did not command such a person to believe, then Pharaoh and Abu Jahl were not commanded, so they do not deserve punishment—which is absurd. If they say God did command them, then they must admit that God commanded them to do something that 'makes Him ignorant' (since their belief would contradict His knowledge). The author argues that the Mu'tazila already accept that God commands disbelief in some sense, because they accept that He commands things that contradict His knowledge? Actually, the text says: 'You have said that He commanded them to make Him ignorant and forbade them from what confirms His knowledge'—this seems to be the Mu'tazila's own admission. The author then argues that if it is permissible for God to command something that implies ignorance (according to the Mu'tazila's own view), then it should also be permissible for God to will such a thing. The author concludes by rebuking the Mu'tazila for trying to justify the impossible through false analogies, due to their ignorance of true meanings. The text ends abruptly, implying further reasoning.

Key Terms
أمر (command)
نهى (prohibition)
تجهيل (making ignorant)
تقرير علم (confirmation of knowledge)
محال (impossible)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's command must always be compatible with His will and foreknowledge.
Correction: This passage shows that command and will are distinct; God can command something even if He knows it will not happen.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the Mu'tazila say God did not command Pharaoh to believe?
A) Pharaoh would still be punished.
B) Pharaoh would not deserve punishment.
C) God would be unjust.
D) God would be ignorant.

Key Takeaway
God's command does not depend on His foreknowledge; commanding a known disbeliever is valid and does not entail impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you know someone will reject your advice, do you still command them to do right? Why or why not? What does your answer reveal about your understanding of responsibility?
?
God commands the known disbeliever despite knowing they will not obey. How does this challenge your view of God's justice? Will you now act differently when commanding good even if you see no result?
?
If you were to internalize that God's command does not depend on His foreknowledge of your response, how would it change the way you hear His commands and respond to them?

Why Prophets Do Not Always Desire Faith for All
Explains prophetic desire in light of God's foreknowledge with examples from Ibrahim and Nuh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a prophet's desire for faith is limited by divine revealed knowledge, as shown in the examples of Ibrahim and Nuh.

Core Idea
Prophetic desire aligns with God's revealed knowledge, not universal hope.

The question arises: why would a prophet not desire faith for certain individuals? This lesson explores the theological basis for this, rooted in God's foreknowledge and the prophet's awareness of it. We'll examine the cases of Prophet Ibrahim and Prophet Nuh, who, upon receiving divine certainty that some would not believe, ceased to desire their faith. The passage also critiques attempts to justify the impossible through false analogies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the full sense.
Notice the shift from the initial claim to the examples and then to the critique.
2
Identify the two prophetic examples
Find the examples of Ibrahim and Nuh. Note what each prophet knew and how their desire changed.
Ibrahim stopped asking forgiveness; Nuh prayed for destruction.
3
Understand the methodological critique
The author criticizes opponents for trying to justify the impossible using analogies. Understand the proper approach: prove it's possible or abandon it.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this shows prophetic desire is not absolute but based on revealed knowledge, protecting God's foreknowledge and wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل إن النبي عليه السلام كان مريدًا إيمان من لم يعلم أن الله تعالى علم منه أن يكفر في المستقبل، فأما من علم النبي عليه السلام أنه يموت كافرا باختيار الله تعالى فإن الله تعالى علم أنه يموت كافرًا كان لا يريد منه الإيمان، ولهذا تبرأ إبراهيم عليه السلام من أبيه حين تبين له أنه عدو الله ولم يستغفر له ولم يرد إيمانه، وكذا نوح عليه السلام لما بلغه خطاب الله تعالى بقوله: ﴿ أَنَّهُ لَن يُؤْمِنَ مِن قَوْمِكَ إِلَّا مَن قَدْ آمَنَ ﴾ كان لا يريد إيمانهم بعد بل كان يدعو فيقول: رَبِّ لَا نَذَرْ عَلَى الْأَرْضِ مِنَ الْكَافِرِينَ دَيَّارًا على أن هذا منكم تصحيح ما هو المحال بما تصور عندكم بأنه في معنى المحال والمحال لا يشتغل بتصحيحه بالنظائر بل إما أن يتبين بالدليل أنه ليس بمحال، وإما أن يترك عند ثبوت إحالته، والله الموفق.
It was said that the Prophet, peace be upon him, desired the faith of one whom he did not know that Allah, exalted, knew from him that he would disbelieve in the future. As for one whom the Prophet, peace be upon him, knew that he would die a disbeliever by the choice of Allah, exalted—for Allah, exalted, knew that he would die a disbeliever—he did not desire faith from him. For this reason, Abraham, peace be upon him, dissociated from his father when it became clear to him that he was an enemy of Allah, and he did not ask forgiveness for him nor desire his faith. Likewise, Noah, peace be upon him, when the address of Allah, exalted, reached him: 'That none of your people will believe except those who have already believed,' he no longer desired their faith, but rather prayed: 'My Lord, leave not upon the earth any dweller from among the disbelievers.' Moreover, this (i.e., your argument) is from you a justification of what is impossible by what you conceive as being in the sense of the impossible, and the impossible is not occupied with justifying it by analogies; rather, either it is shown by evidence that it is not impossible, or it is abandoned upon the establishment of its impossibility. And Allah is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a statement and argument about prophetic desire for faith in light of God's foreknowledge. The author begins by asserting that the Prophet only desired faith for those individuals whose future disbelief was not known to him. For those who the Prophet knew would die as disbelievers—because God had knowledge of their persistent unbelief—he did not desire their faith. To support this, the author cites two prophetic examples: Abraham, who upon realizing that his father was an enemy of God, stopped seeking forgiveness for him and no longer desired his faith; and Noah, who after receiving God's revelation that none of his people would believe except those who already had, ceased desiring their faith and instead prayed for their destruction. The passage then shifts to a methodological critique: the author accuses the opponents of trying to justify what is impossible (al-muḥāl) by appealing to what they consider analogous but which is not truly analogous. The proper approach, the author argues, is either to demonstrate through evidence that something is not impossible, or to abandon it once its impossibility is established. The passage ends with a prayer for divine guidance.

إيمان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think prophets always desire faith for everyone, without exception.
Correction: This passage shows that prophetic desire is contingent on God's revealed knowledge. When a prophet knows that a person will die as a disbeliever, he does not desire their faith, as exemplified by Ibrahim and Nuh.

Checkpoint
Why did Prophet Noah stop desiring the faith of his people, according to the passage?
A) Because they rejected him after he preached for many years.
B) Because God revealed to him that none of them would believe except those who already had.
C) Because he was commanded to pray for their destruction.
D) Because he knew they would all die as disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic desire for faith is conditioned by divine revelation, not wishful thinking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly submit to God's knowledge and decree when you see loved ones persisting in disbelief, or do you cling to your own hopes against what He has revealed? Will you learn from Ibrahim and Nuh to align your desires with His truth, even when it is painful?
?
When you pray for guidance for someone, are you prepared to accept that God may have already decreed their disbelief? How would you respond if He made it clear to you that they will never believe?
?
Have you ever tried to 'prove' something is possible with God when deep down you knew it was against His explicit decree? This passage warns against justifying the impossible. Are you willing to abandon your own reasoning and submit to His revealed limits, even when your heart resists?

The Purpose of Command and Prohibition for the Known Disbeliever
Responds to the Mu'tazila dilemma about commanding the known disbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna response to the Mu'tazila objection that commanding someone God knows will disbelieve is either futile or entails self-ignorance; you will see that command and prohibition serve to actualize God's foreknowledge of obedience and disobedience.

Core Idea
God's command to the known disbeliever is not for him to obey, but to actualize his disobedience and punishment.

The Mu'tazila raise a powerful dilemma: If God knows someone will never believe, did He command him to believe or not? Either way seems problematic. In this passage, the author reframes the entire purpose of divine command. He argues that God commands and forbids not primarily to elicit compliance, but to realize His foreknowledge. This answer preserves both God's justice and His sovereignty over human actions. Let's explore this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila dilemma
Read the opening objection carefully. The Mu'tazila present two options: either God did not command the known disbeliever (making punishment unjust) or He did (making Him command self-ignorance).
Notice that both options are presented as contradictions in the Mu'tazila view.
2
Examine the two horns
If God did not command, then Pharaoh and Abu Jahl are blameless. If He did command, He commanded what He knows will not happen, which seems to involve ignorance.
3
Author's criticism of Mu'tazila method
The author accuses the Mu'tazila of trying to justify the impossible by false analogies. He says they are ignorant of the real meanings.
This is a rhetorical move to set up his own explanation.
4
Learn the key concept: taḥqīq (realization) of knowledge
The author explains that command and prohibition are for realizing what God already knows. Without command, there would be no disobedience and thus no just punishment.
This is the core of the answer.
5
Distinguish two cases
God commands the obedient to fulfill obedience, and commands the disobedient so that they disobey and are punished. In both cases, the command actualizes foreknowledge.
This resolves the dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: من علم الله منه أنه لا يؤمن ويموت على الكفر، هل أمره بالإيمان ونهاه عن الكفر أم لا؟ فإن قلتم : لا . فقد زعمتم أن فرعون وأبا جهل ما كانا مأمورين بالإيمان ولا منهيين عن الكفر ، وفيه أنهما ما استحقا اللوم والتعذيب لأنهما ما تركا مأمورًا ولا ارتكبا منهيًا وهو كفر ، وإن قلتم : أمرهما بالإيمان ونهاهما عن الكفر . فقد قلتم : إنه أمرهما بتجهيل نفسه ونهاهما عما فيه تقرير علمه وهذا مما لا خلاف فيه، فإذا جاز بالإجماع أن يأمر بتجهيل نفسه وينهى عما فيه تقرير علمه، ولم يكن ذلك محالاً فلم يجوز أن يريد ما فيه تجهيل نفسه ولا يريد ما فيه تقرير علمه؟ بل يكره ذلك قلنا : أنتم معشر المعتزلة تريدون أبدًا تصحيح المحال بما تصور عندكم من الجائزات أنه يظهر لجهلكم بحقائق المعاني، ولو عقلتم لسعيتم في بيان جواز ما ذهبتم إليه ودفع الإحالة عنه لا في تسويته وتجويزه مع ثبوت إحالته بما هو في الظاهر نظيره عندكم. ثم نقول: هذا كلام تمسكتم به لجهلكم بمذاهب خصومكم ولو عرفتم ذلك حقيقة لما اشتغلتم به، وذلك لأن الأمر والنهي عند خصومكم ورد لتحقيق ما علم الله تعالى وإن كان يتراءى من حيث الظاهر أنهما وردا لمخالفة العلم، وبيان ذلك أنه تعالى علم بسابق علمه أن فرعون يكفر ويعصى ويعاقبه الله تعالى في النار، وكذا غيره من الكفرة وأخبر بذلك ثم لا يعذب إلا على العصيان ولا عصيان بدون الأمر والنهي إذ لو لم يأمر بالإيمان لما وجب تحصيله ولا حرم تركه ولو لم ينه عن الكفر لما حرم تحصيله ولما وجب تركه ولو لم يجب الإيمان ولم يحرم الكفر لكان لا يعاقب الكافر ولا يملأ منه جهنم، فلا يتحقق ما علمه وأخبر به من إدخاله الجنة وإكرامه إياه بالثواب وأمر ونهى من علم منه المعصية لا ليأتمر وينتهي بل ليترك الائتمار والانتهاء فعاقبه الله ويدخله النار، فيتحقق ما علم وما أخبر به، ولولا الأمر والنهي لما تحقق ذلك فكان أمره ونهيه لتحقيق ما علمه وأخبر به، وإنما يكون الأمر على ما زعمت المعتزلة أن لو كان أمر الكافر ليطيعه لا ليعصيه فحينئذ يكون الأمر والنهي لتجهيله وتكذيبه، وأما إذا كان ذلك ليعصي لا ليطيع فكان لتحقيق ما علم وأخبر لا لتجهيله، وصار الحاصل أن من علم الله منه أهل طاعة أمره ليطيع لتتحقق الطاعة إذ لا تحقق لها بدون الأمر، ومن علم منه المعصية أمره لئلا يفعل بل يعصي إذ لا تحقق للمعصية بدون الأمر، وما وقع في أفواه متفقهة زماننا أن فائدة الوجوب بلا داع تلقنوه من المعتزلة فيتكلمون به جهلاً
For it may be said: Regarding one of whom God knows that he will not believe and will die in disbelief, did He command him to faith and forbid him from disbelief or not? If you say: No, then you have claimed that Pharaoh and Abu Jahl were not commanded to faith nor forbidden from disbelief, and this implies that they did not deserve blame and punishment because they did not abandon something commanded nor commit something forbidden, which is disbelief. And if you say: He commanded them to faith and forbade them from disbelief, then you have said: He commanded them to self-ignorance and forbade them from what confirms His knowledge—and this is uncontroversial. Since it is permissible by consensus for Him to command self-ignorance and forbid what confirms His knowledge, and that is not impossible, then why is it not permissible for Him to will what involves self-ignorance and not will what involves confirmation of His knowledge? Rather, He dislikes that. We say: You group of Mu'tazila constantly try to justify the impossible by what you consider permissible, because it appears to you due to your ignorance of the realities of meanings. If you had intelligence, you would strive to explain the permissibility of what you hold and to remove impossibility from it, not to equate it and permit it while impossibility is established, by what is apparently similar to it in your view. Then we say: This is a statement you have clung to out of ignorance of the doctrines of your opponents; if you had truly known them, you would not have occupied yourselves with it. That is because, according to your opponents, command and prohibition came for the realization of what Allah, exalted, knows, even though outwardly they seem to come contrary to knowledge. The explanation of that is that He, exalted, by His prior knowledge knew that Pharaoh would disbelieve and disobey and that Allah, exalted, would punish him in the Fire, and likewise other disbelievers, and He informed of that. Then He does not punish except for disobedience, and there is no disobedience without command and prohibition. For if He had not commanded faith, its acquisition would not be obligatory nor its abandonment prohibited; and if He had not forbidden disbelief, its acquisition would not be prohibited nor its abandonment obligatory. And if faith were not obligatory and disbelief not prohibited, then the disbeliever would not be punished nor would Hell be filled with him. Thus, what He knew and informed of—namely, admitting him to Paradise and honoring him with reward—would not be realized. So He commanded and forbade those whom He knew would disobey, not for them to obey and refrain, but for them to abandon obedience and refrain from refraining, so that He punishes them and admits them to Hell, thereby realizing what He knew and informed of. Had it not been for the command and prohibition, that would not be realized. Thus, His command and prohibition are for the realization of what He knew and informed of. The command would only be as the Mu'tazila claim if He had commanded the disbeliever to obey him rather than to disobey; then the command and prohibition would be for self-ignorance and falsification. But if that is for him to disobey rather than to obey, then it is for the realization of what He knew and informed of, not for self-ignorance. The upshot is that one of whom God knows that he is of the people of obedience, He commands him to obey, so that obedience is realized, for it has no realization without the command. And one of whom He knows that he is of the people of disobedience, He commands him so that he does not do (i.e., so that he disobeys), for disobedience has no realization without the command. And what has fallen into the mouths of the pseudo-jurists of our time—that the benefit of obligation is without a cause—they have learned from the Mu'tazila, and they speak ignorantly.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structured argument. It begins with an objection from the Mu'tazila: For someone God knows will not believe and will die a disbeliever, did He command him to faith and forbid him from disbelief or not? If He did not command, then Pharaoh and Abu Jahl were never commanded and thus do not deserve punishment. If He did command, then He commanded self-ignorance (since He knows they will not comply) and forbade what confirms His knowledge—supposedly an impossibility. The Mu'tazila conclude that commanding while knowing non-compliance leads to contradiction. The author's answer reframes the entire purpose of command and prohibition. He first criticizes the Mu'tazila for trying to justify an impossible scenario by false analogies. Then he explains that, according to the Ahl al-Sunna, command and prohibition are not primarily for the purpose of being obeyed by those who are known to disobey; rather, they are for the realization (taḥqīq) of God's foreknowledge. God's prior knowledge included that certain individuals would disobey and be punished. For that punishment to be just, there must be a command and prohibition so that their disobedience is actual and blameworthy. Without the command, faith would not be obligatory nor disbelief prohibited; thus, the disbeliever would not be punishable, and God's foreknown decree of punishment would not be realized. Therefore, God commands the known disbeliever not for him to obey, but for him to disobey, thereby actualizing the punishment. The author distinguishes: if God commanded intending obedience, that would be self-ignorance; but He commands intending disobedience, which realizes His knowledge. The final sentence criticizes contemporary pseudo-jurists who speak of 'obligation without a cause'—a concept learned from the Mu'tazila, showing ignorance.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
علم
طاعة
معصية
تحقيق
تجهيل
محال
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's command always intends compliance, and commanding someone known to disobey is futile or unjust.
Correction: God's command can intend disobedience to actualize His foreknowledge and just punishment, not to elicit compliance.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, what is the purpose of God's command to someone He knows will disobey?
A) To make them obey and avoid punishment.
B) To realize His foreknowledge of their disobedience and consequent punishment.
C) To test their faith even though He knows they will fail.
D) To provide an excuse for punishing them arbitrarily.

Key Takeaway
God's commands and prohibitions are not always for compliance; they serve to realize His foreknowledge of both obedience and disobedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever think that Allah knew you would sin anyway, so why bother trying? How does this lesson's explanation about the purpose of commands change your view of your own responsibility in that sin?
?
Allah's command to you is not just a suggestion—it is a means by which your obedience or disobedience is actualized. Are you living as if every command and prohibition you encounter is a real opportunity to either fulfill or defy God's foreknown decree for you?
?
If you knew that a particular command of Allah is impossible for you to obey (like some claim about belief), would that make you give up effort? Reflect: does this lesson free you from striving or deepen your awe of Allah's wisdom in commanding even what He knows you might fail?

Objection and answer on divine pleasure and will
Imam Nasafi responds to an objection about divine pleasure and will regarding faith and disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi theologians defend the consistency of divine will and pleasure regarding faith and disbelief.

Core Idea
Divine approval of disbelief would contradict its ugliness.

The objection presented here challenges the coherence of Allah willing both faith and disbelief while only approving of faith. The response, drawing on both Ash'ari and Maturidi arguments, clarifies that approval is not about existence but about moral evaluation. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical structure: the objection claims that divine approval of disbelief would necessitate ignorance, while approval of faith verifies knowledge.
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
The answer begins with a general remark that this is like a previous objection about will. Then it presents two distinct responses: one from Al-Ash'ari and one from the Maturidi masters. Notice how each addresses the objection differently.
3
Compare the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions
Al-Ash'ari's answer focuses on interpreting the Quranic verse as specific to believers, while the Maturidi answer explains approval as deeming something good. Understand how both resolve the apparent contradiction.
4
Understand key terms
Focus on the terms رضا (approval), محبة (love), استحسان (deeming good), and استقباح (deeming ugly). The Maturidi argument hinges on the idea that approval means deeming good, and since disbelief is inherently ugly, Allah does not approve it.
5
Reflect on the logical consistency
Ask yourself how divine will for disbelief can coexist with divine displeasure toward it. The answer lies in distinguishing between willing an act and approving of its moral quality. This preserves both divine knowledge and justice.

Source Text

فإن قالوا: أليس أن الله تعالى لا يرضى بالكفر ولا يحبه ويرضى بالإيمان ويحبه، فلما جاز أن يرضى بما لو تحقق لأوجب تجهيله ولا يرضى بما لو تحقق لكان فيه تحقيق علمه؟ فكذا في الإرادة جاز ذلك، قيل: وهذا مثل السؤال الأول أن إرادة تصحيح المحال بما تصور عندكم أنه نظير ذلك المحال. ثم نقول: إن الأشعري يقول : إن الله تعالى يرضى بوجود الكفر من الكافر قبيحا وكذا يجب وجود الكفر قبيحا وقوله تعالى : ( وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الْكُفْرَ ) المراد به المؤمنون دون الكفرة حملت على الخصوص بالدليل فعلى هذا اندفع الإلزام، وعلى قول مشايخنا - رحمهم الله كل واحد منهما أعني الرضا والمحبة لتحقيق ما علم لأن الرضا بالشيء استحسان له وكذا المحبة واستحسان الفعل يخرجه من أن يكون منهيًا، ومن أن يستحق عليه صاحبه العقوبة فلم يستحسن الكفر الذي علم وجوده بل استقبح وكره على ما يقتضى وجوده، إذ هو لن يتصور إلا قبيحا ليكون سببًا للعقوبة وإدخال النار فيتحقق ما علم الله تعالى وما أخبر ، والله الموفق.
For if they say: 'Is it not that Allah Almighty does not approve of disbelief nor love it, and He approves of faith and loves it? So why is it permissible that He approves of that which, if realized, would necessitate ignorance of Him, and does not approve of that which, if realized, would contain verification of His knowledge? Likewise in will, that is permissible.' It is said: 'This is like the first question, that the will to rectify the impossible is based on what you imagine to be analogous to that impossible.' Then we say: Al-Ash'ari says that Allah Almighty approves of the occurrence of disbelief from the disbeliever as ugly, and likewise the occurrence of disbelief must be ugly. And His saying, 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' (Quran 39:7) means the believers, not the disbelievers; it is taken as specific by evidence, so the objection is repelled on this basis. According to the saying of our masters (may Allah have mercy on them), each of them, i.e., approval and love, is for the realization of what is known, because approval of something is deeming it good, and likewise love, and deeming the act good removes it from being prohibited and from its doer deserving punishment. Thus, He did not deem good the disbelief whose existence He knew; rather, He deemed it ugly and disliked it according to what its existence requires, for it can only be conceived as ugly to be a cause for punishment and entry into the Fire, so that what Allah knew and informed of is realized. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding divine pleasure (rida) and love (mahabbah) for faith versus disbelief. The objectors argue: if Allah does not approve of disbelief and does not love it, but approves of faith and loves it, then how can it be said that He wills disbelief? They claim that approving of disbelief would necessitate ignorance (since disbelief contradicts knowledge of Allah), while approving of faith would confirm knowledge. They extend this to will (irada). The answer begins by stating that this is similar to a previous objection about willing the impossible. Then the author distinguishes between two positions: first, the Ash'ari view that Allah approves of the existence of disbelief as ugly, and that the verse 'He does not approve for His servants disbelief' refers only to believers, not disbelievers, thus the objection is repelled. Second, the Maturidi view of the author's masters that approval and love are for the realization of what is known. Approval of something means deeming it good; deeming an act good removes it from prohibition and punishment. Since disbelief is known to exist, Allah does not deem it good but rather deems it ugly and dislikes it, because it can only be ugly to serve as a cause for punishment and Hellfire, thereby realizing what Allah knew and foretold. Thus, divine pleasure and will are consistent with the actual evil of disbelief.

Key Terms
الرضا
المحبة
الكفر
الإيمان
الإرادة

الاستقباح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine approval of an act implies that the act is inherently good.
Correction: The passage clarifies that approval (rida) is deeming something good (istihsan), but Allah does not deem disbelief good because it is ugly (qabih) and leads to punishment, so He does not approve it even though He wills its existence.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi position in this passage, why does Allah not approve of disbelief even though He wills its existence?
A) Because disbelief is inherently impossible for Allah to approve.
B) Because approval means deeming something good, and disbelief is ugly and leads to punishment.
C) Because Allah only approves of acts that are commanded.
D) Because the verse restricts approval to believers only.

Key Takeaway
Divine approval and love are based on moral evaluation, not on the mere existence of an act; Allah wills disbelief for its role in realizing His justice, but does not approve of its ugliness.

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Verse Argument on Divine Will
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite objection using Quran 40:31.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite argument from Quran 40:31 by linguistic analysis and the Baghdad school's own view on divine will.

Core Idea
God's will in the verse means He does not wrong anyone, not that He never wills injustice by others.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God cannot will injustice because the Quran says He does not will it. Nasafi responds by showing that the verse is linguistically ambiguous, and that even on the Mu'tazilites' own premises, the verse does not prove their case. This lesson unpacks his clever refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection
The Mu'tazilites cited Quran 40:31 ('And Allah does not will injustice for the servants') to argue that God cannot will evil. Recognize why this poses a problem for the Ash'ari/Maturidi view that God creates all actions, including evil.
The verse appears to deny that God wills any injustice whatsoever.
2
Examine Nasafi's first reply
Nasafi points out that according to the Baghdad school of the Mu'tazila, God is not described with a real will for His actions; rather, 'He willed' means 'He did'. So the verse would mean 'God did not do injustice', which Nasafi agrees with. Thus the verse provides no proof against him.
This reply uses the opponent's own principle.
3
Analyze the linguistic argument
Nasafi then presents a linguistic distinction: 'I do not will your injustice' means 'I do not will that you be wronged' (by anyone), while 'I do not will injustice to you' means 'I do not will to wrong you'. The verse's phrasing is ambiguous, but Nasafi specifies the latter meaning based on prior proofs.
This shows the verse is consistent with God willing injustice by others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن تعلقهم بقوله تعالى ( وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ ) . قلنا على أصل البغدادين منكم لا يوصف الله تعالى بالإرادة حقيقة، وإذا ذكرت إرادته لفعله فمعناه أنه فعل فيكون تقدير الآية - على زعمهم – وما ظلم الله ظلمًا ونحن هكذا نقول فلم يكن لهم في الآية حجة. ثم نقول : إن أهل اللغة قالوا : إذا قال الرجل لآخر لا أريد ظلمك كان معناه لا أريد أن تظلم أنت من غير تعيين الفاعل، وإذا قال: لا أريد ظلمًا لك كان معناه لا أريد أن أظلمك. ونحن نقول : إن الله تعالى لا يريد أن يظلم أحدًا، على أن أكثر ما في الباب أن هذه اللفظة محتملة للمعنيين جميعًا إلا أنا نعين أحدهما وهو أن المراد منه لا أريد أن أظلمك لما سبق من الدلائل، والله الموفق.
And the answer to their attachment to His saying, the Most High, “And Allah does not will injustice for the servants.” We say according to the principle of the Baghdadis among you: Allah, the Most High, is not described with will (al-irāda) in reality, and when His will for His action is mentioned, its meaning is that He did it. So the estimation of the verse – according to their claim – would be: “And Allah did not do injustice,” and we say this, so they have no proof in the verse. Then we say: The linguists said: When a man says to another, “I do not will your injustice,” its meaning is “I do not will that you be wronged” without specifying the agent; and when he says, “I do not will injustice to you,” its meaning is “I do not will to wrong you.” And we say: Allah, the Most High, does not will to wrong anyone. Moreover, the most that can be said is that this expression is possible for both meanings, except that we specify one of them – that the intended meaning is “I do not will to wrong you” – because of the preceding proofs. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilite objection. They cited the Quranic verse “And Allah does not will injustice for the servants” (40:31) to argue that God cannot will evil or injustice, thus denying that He creates evil acts. Nasafi responds by first noting that according to the Baghdad school of the Mu'tazila themselves, God is not described with a real will (al-irāda) for His actions; rather, saying 'God willed' means He did the act. On that interpretation, the verse would mean 'God did not do injustice,' which Nasafi agrees with. Therefore, the verse provides no proof against him. He then offers a linguistic analysis: when someone says 'I do not will your injustice' (lā urīdu ẓulmaka), it means 'I do not will that you be wronged' by someone else. But when he says 'I do not will injustice to you' (lā urīdu ẓulman laka), it means 'I do not will to wrong you.' The verse's wording (ẓulman lil-‘ibād) is ambiguous. However, based on prior evidence (that God creates all actions, including evil, for wise purposes), Nasafi specifies that God means He does not will to wrong anyone Himself, not that He never wills that someone else wrong another. Thus the Mu'tazilite argument is neutralized.

Key Terms
إرادة
ظلم
بغداديون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Quran 40:31 proves that God never wills any form of injustice, including injustice committed by others.
Correction: The verse only means God does not will to wrong anyone Himself; it does not deny that He may will injustice through secondary agents.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's reply, what is the key linguistic point about the phrase 'لَا أُرِيدُ ظُلْمَكَ' vs 'لَا أُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لَكَ'?
A) Both phrases have the same meaning.
B) The first means 'I do not will to wrong you', the second means 'I do not will that you be wronged'.
C) The first means 'I do not will that you be wronged', the second means 'I do not will to wrong you'.
D) Neither phrase has a clear meaning.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi efficiently refutes the Mu'tazilite argument by showing the verse is ambiguous and consistent with his theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see terrible injustices in the world – oppression, suffering, evil – do you ever wrestle with the thought that God might be unjust? How does this lesson's careful distinction between God not willing to wrong anyone and His permissive will for others' wrongs challenge you to trust His perfect justice even when you cannot see the reason?
?
Are you quick to read verses like 'Allah does not will injustice for the servants' in a way that limits God's sovereignty because it feels more comfortable? What would it mean for your relationship with God if you accepted that His will encompasses all events, including evils, yet He remains utterly just? How would that change your prayers during trials?
?
Nasafi uses linguistic precision to defend a difficult truth. Are you willing to engage deeply with the Arabic of the Quran, even when it shakes your assumptions? What would it cost you to sit with an ambiguous verse until you understand it on its own terms, rather than imposing what you want it to say?

Willing Foolishness Is Not Foolish When It Has a Good Outcome
Nasafi proves that willing foolishness is wise if it has a good consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that an intention to bring about something apparently foolish is not considered foolish if it is connected to a praiseworthy outcome, as demonstrated by prophetic examples and rational argument.

Core Idea
Intention is judged by its outcome, not by its immediate object.

Nasafi tackles a potentially troubling question: can God will something that seems foolish? The answer lies in a broader principle: the moral quality of an intention depends on its ultimate consequence. This lesson unpacks that principle through scriptural examples and logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the core principle
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage. It states that if a foolish intention is tied to a good outcome, it is not foolish. This is the key claim.
This principle governs all the examples.
2
Study the first example (Cain)
The son of Adam (Cain) intended his brother to bear his sin. Though the desire was evil, the statement itself was not foolish because it served a purpose in the narrative.
The author uses this to illustrate that outcome matters.
3
Consider Moses' prayer
Moses asked God to harden Pharaoh's heart so they would not believe. This appears to be willing unbelief, but it was not foolish because it fulfilled divine wisdom.
This shows the same principle applied to a prophet's intention.
4
Analyze the sorcerers dilemma
Moses told the sorcerers to throw. The opponents must call it wisdom or foolishness. If wisdom, they condone sorcery; if foolishness, they must explain Moses' intent. The resolution shows that the intention was for a good outcome.
This is the most complex example, demonstrating the principle through logical dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما لم يتعلق به عاقبة حميدة فلو تعلقت بإرادة السفه لما كانت سفها كما لم يكن من ابن آدم عليه السلام بقوله: ﴿ إِنِّي أُرِيدُ أَن تَبُوا بِإِثْمِي وَإِيْمَكَ ﴾ سفها وإن كانت إرادة سفه، ولم يكن من موسى عليه السلام حيث قال: ﴿ وَاشْدُدْ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ فَلَا يُؤْمِنُوا ﴾ سفها فكذا من الله تعالى، وكذا موسى عليه السلام حيث قال للسحرة ﴿ بَلْ أَلْقُوا ﴾ وقال ﴿ أَلْقُوا مَا أَنتُم مُلْقُونَ ﴾ أكان ذلك الإلقاء سفها أم حكمة؟ فإن قالوا: كان فجعلوا معارضة الرسول في المعجزة بما يأتيه الساحر من المخرقة حكمة وهو كفر، وإن قالوا: كان الإلقاء سفهًا، قلنا: وهل أراد موسى وجود الإلقاء؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد زعموا أنه لم يرد ظهور حجته وإبطال ما يعارضون به حجته وأرادوا بقاء أمر رسالته في حيز الإشكال والتردد، وإن قالوا: نعم. فقد أقروا أنه أراد السفه ولم يصر به سفيها لما كان تحت إرادته ظهور حجته وغلبة دلائله، وفيما نحن فيه يكون تحت إرادة السفه تحقق علمه وخبره فيكون حكمة لا سفها.
What is not connected to a praiseworthy outcome—if it were connected to the intention of foolishness, it would not be foolishness. Just as it was not foolishness from the son of Adam (peace be upon him) when he said: 'Indeed, I intend that you bear my sin and your sin'—even though it was an intention of foolishness; and it was not foolishness from Moses (peace be upon him) when he said: 'And harden their hearts so that they do not believe'—likewise from God Almighty; and similarly Moses (peace be upon him) when he said to the sorcerers: 'Rather, throw' and said: 'Throw whatever you will throw'—was that throwing foolishness or wisdom? If they say: It was wisdom, then they have made opposing the messenger in the miracle by what the sorcerer brings of trickery to be wisdom, and that is disbelief. And if they say: The throwing was foolishness, we say: Did Moses intend the occurrence of the throwing? If they say: No, then they have claimed that he did not intend the manifestation of his proof and the invalidation of what they oppose his proof with, and they intended that the matter of his prophethood remain in the realm of ambiguity and hesitation. And if they say: Yes, then they have affirmed that he intended foolishness but did not become foolish by it, because under his intention was the manifestation of his proof and the dominance of his evidences. And in our case, under the intention of foolishness is the realization of His knowledge and His report, so it is wisdom, not foolishness.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the principle that an intention to bring about something that is in itself foolish (sufh) is not considered foolish if that intention is tied to a praiseworthy outcome. The author begins by stating that any action not connected to a good outcome would be folly, but if it is so connected, then intending folly is not folly itself. He provides two prophetic examples: first, Adam's son (Cain) saying he intends his brother to bear his sin—this was an evil desire but the statement itself was not foolish because it had a beneficial consequence in the narrative. Second, Moses asking God to harden Pharaoh's heart so they would not believe—that was not foolish because it served a divine purpose. The author then addresses a more complex case: Moses telling the sorcerers to throw their staffs. The opponents are presented with a dilemma: if they say that command was wisdom, they equate a prophet's miracle with sorcery, which is disbelief; if they say it was foolishness, then they must explain Moses' intention. If they deny Moses intended the sorcerers' action, then they deny he wanted his proof to prevail; if they affirm it, they admit he willed foolishness but that did not make him foolish because the underlying goal was to manifest his proof. The author concludes that in the current discussion about God's will, the intention of what appears foolish is actually wisdom because it leads to the realization of divine knowledge and prophecy.

Key Terms
سفه
عاقبة حميدة
إرادة
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intending something foolish always makes the person foolish.
Correction: The intention is judged by its outcome; if the outcome is praiseworthy, the intention is not considered foolish.

Checkpoint
What is the key principle demonstrated by the examples of Adam's son and Moses?
A) Intending foolishness always makes one foolish.
B) Intending foolishness with a praiseworthy outcome is not foolish.
C) Prophets never intend anything that appears foolish.
D) Only God can intend something foolish without being foolish.

Key Takeaway
The moral quality of an intention depends on its ultimate consequence, not on the apparent nature of the action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a questionable intention by telling yourself it would lead to a good outcome? How does this lesson challenge that self-justification? What will you do next time you face that internal argument?

When you pray for something that seems harmful or hard (like Moses asking for hardening of hearts), do you trust that Allah's wisdom is greater than your understanding? What specific step can you take today to surrender your judgment to His wisdom?
?
This lesson shows that even a prophet could intend something seemingly foolish for a greater purpose. Is there a situation in your life where you are avoiding a right action because it looks foolish to others? What would it cost you to obey Allah regardless of appearances?

God's Will and the Evil of Kufr
Nasafi presents and refutes a Mu'tazilite objection regarding God's will and kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's refutation of the claim that God cannot will kufr because it is evil in itself.

Core Idea
Denying God's will for kufr leads to declaring kufr good, which is itself disbelief.

This passage presents a key debate: if kufr is inherently evil, can God still will it? The Mu'tazila argued that God cannot will evil. Nasafi responds with a dilemma that turns their own logic against them. Read carefully to see how their position collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the opponents' claim that kufr is evil in itself and that God cannot will it. Notice how they frame the argument.
2
Identify the dilemma
Nasafi presents two possibilities: either God wills the essence of kufr (which they deny) or He does not will it to be evil. Follow each horn and its consequence.
3
Reflect on the outcome
The second horn leads to the absurd conclusion that kufr would be considered good, which is itself clear disbelief. See how the objection is self-defeating.
This shows the importance of maintaining God's sovereignty over all things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: نعم - وهو قولهم - قيل : والكفر قبيح لعينه فإذا الله قبحه فقد أراد عينه إذ هذا ما أنكرتموه، وإن قالوا لا يريد أن يكون قبيحًا، فقد أراد أن يكون من مذهبهم أن ما ليس بقبيح فهو حسن ولهذا زعموا أن كل مباح حسن، وزعموا أن الوجوب أو الندب يقتضي حسنًا زائدًا على أصل الحسن، والقول بأن الكفر حسن كفر صريح.
They said: Yes—and that is their statement—it is said: And disbelief (kufr) is evil in itself. So if God made it evil, then He intended its very essence, for this is what you denied. And if they say: He does not intend it to be evil, then they have intended to be of the opinion that whatever is not evil is good, and for this they claim that every permissible thing is good, and they claim that obligation or recommendation entails an additional goodness beyond the original goodness. And the statement that disbelief is good is clear disbelief.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a refutation regarding God's will and the nature of kufr (disbelief). The opponents (likely the Mu'tazila) assert that kufr is evil in itself (qabih li-aynihi). They argue: if God made it evil, then He willed its very essence, which they deny. The author responds with a dilemma: if the opponents say God does not intend kufr to be evil, then they must hold that whatever is not evil is good, which leads them to claim that all permissible acts are good, and that obligation or recommendation adds an extra goodness. This implies that kufr would be considered good—a statement that is itself clear disbelief (kufr sarih). Thus, the objection is self-defeating.

Key Terms

قبيح لعينه
حسن
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God willing kufr implies He approves of its evil, so He cannot will it.
Correction: The lesson shows that God's will for kufr is about its existence, not approval of its evil nature. The Mu'tazilite position leads to declaring kufr good, which is worse.

Checkpoint

B) They must consider kufr good, which is disbelief.
C) They are correct about God's will.
D) God's will is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Denying that God wills kufr because it is evil forces one to admit that kufr is good, which is itself disbelief.

What would it cost you to fully embrace that God's will is perfect, even when it includes things you dislike?
