4 min
God's Grace and the Absence of Obligation to Give It
Nasafi states that God's withholding of a particular grace does not imply negative attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's withholding of a specific grace that would cause disbelievers to believe does not imply any deficiency in His attributes.

Core Idea
God is free from obligation to grant any benefit, even if it would cause belief.

This lesson examines the first part of Imam al-Nasafi's argument that God is not obligated to provide the most beneficial. The passage begins by acknowledging that God has the power to grant a grace (luṭf) that would lead disbelievers to willingly choose faith, yet He does not do so. Imam al-Nasafi immediately clarifies that this withholding does not make God miserly, foolish, unjust, or tyrannical. Let's read the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the four negative attributes denied of God
The passage denies that God is miserly (bakhīl), foolish (safīh), unjust (jā'ir), or tyrannical (ẓālim). Find each term in the Arabic.
These are specific accusations that might arise from God's non-grant.
3
Reflect on why these denials are necessary
Think about what each term implies about God's character and why denying them preserves divine sovereignty.
Miserliness would be holding back what is due; foolishness would be irrational action, etc.
4
Connect to the broader Sunni doctrine of al-Aslah
Understand that this denial sets the stage for the main point: God is not obligated to provide the most beneficial.
This lesson is the foundation for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الحق أن في مقدور الله تعالى لطفا لو فعل ذلك بالكفار لأمنوا اختيارا، ولم يفعل بهم ذلك ولم يكن بأن لم يعطهم ذلك بخيلاً ولا سفيها ولا جائراً ولا ظالما، ولو فعل ذلك لكان منعمًا متفضلاً لا مؤديًا ما عليه، وإنه إذا لم يعطهم ذلك فقد منعهم ما هو الأصلح لهم، وكان إعطاؤه إياهم ذلك اللطف أصلح لهم من ترك الإعطاء، ويجوز أن يفعل بالعبد ما ليس بمصلحة له وإعطاء المصلحة ليس بواجب على الله تعالى ولا إعطاء الأصلح، وليس لما في مقدور الله تعالى مما به الصلاح للعبد غاية ليس ورائها ما هو أصلح مما فعل.
The people of truth said: Indeed, in the power of God Almighty there is a grace which, if He did it to the disbelievers, they would surely believe willingly. He did not do it to them, and He was not, by not giving it to them, miserly nor foolish nor unjust nor tyrannical. If He did it, He would be a benefactor and gracious, not one fulfilling an obligation. And if He does not give them that, He has prevented them from what is most beneficial for them; and giving them that grace would have been more beneficial for them than withholding it. It is permissible for Him to do to a servant that which is not beneficial to him; granting benefit is not obligatory upon God Almighty, nor granting the most beneficial. There is no limit to what is in God's power that constitutes benefit for the servant, beyond which there is nothing more beneficial than what He does.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni (Ahl al-Haqq) doctrine regarding al-Aslah (the most beneficial). It begins by acknowledging that God has power over a specific grace (luṭf) which, if applied to disbelievers, would cause them to choose belief freely. However, God does not grant this grace, and this non-grant does not imply that God is miserly (bakhīl), foolish (safīh), unjust (jā'ir), or tyrannical (ẓālim). If He were to grant it, He would be a gracious benefactor (mun'im mutafaḍḍil), not one discharging a debt. The passage then clarifies that withholding this grace does not constitute depriving them of what is most beneficial; although giving it would indeed be more beneficial than withholding it, God is not obligated to provide benefit (maṣlaḥa) or the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ). The text further asserts that God may do to a servant something that is not beneficial for him, and that granting benefit is not obligatory upon God. Finally, it concludes that there is no limit to what God can do in terms of benefit for the servant—beyond what He actually does, there is no further obligation to do what is even more beneficial. The passage systematically defends divine sovereignty and freedom from any obligation to maximize created benefit.

أهل الحق
بخيل
سفيه
جائر
ظالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God withholds a possible grace, He is unjust or miserly.
Correction: The Sunni view holds that God is not obligated to grant any benefit, so withholding it does not make Him unjust or miserly.

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Nasafi deny about God when He withholds the grace?
A) He is powerful
B) He is merciful
C) He is miserly, foolish, unjust, or tyrannical
D) He is wise

Key Takeaway
God's withholding of a grace that could cause belief does not imply any flaw in His attributes, because He is not obligated to grant it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God owes you something—a blessing, guidance, or an easier path? When you consider that God is not obligated to give even the grace that would make you believe willingly, how does that reshape your gratitude and humility before Him?
?
When you see others who disbelieve or struggle with faith, do you ever find yourself questioning God's justice, thinking He should have given them more? What does this passage demand of you when those thoughts arise?
?
If God's withholding of grace does not make Him miserly, foolish, unjust, or tyrannical, then how do you respond when He withholds something you desperately want? Will you trust His perfection, or will you let your heart accuse Him of being less than what He is?

The Non-Obligation of al-Aslah (The Most Beneficial)
Nasafi argues that God is not obligated to provide benefit or the most beneficial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God is not obligated to grant benefit or the most beneficial action to His servants.

Core Idea
God is free from any obligation to maximize creation's benefit.

Continuing from the previous lesson, Imam al-Nasafi now directly addresses the core issue of al-Aslah. He explains that even if God were to grant the grace, it would be a favor, not an obligation. The passage then establishes that God may do what is not beneficial for a servant, and granting benefit is not obligatory. Finally, it states that there is no limit to possible benefit beyond what God actually does. Let's examine this decisive part.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two possibilities
The passage considers both giving and withholding the grace. Notice how each scenario is described differently.
Giving: benefactor; withholding: not fulfilling an obligation.
2
Understand the distinction between benefactor and obligation
The phrase 'منعمًا متفضلاً لا مؤديًا ما عليه' is key. It means God is gracious, not discharging a duty.
This distinction is central to the argument.
3
Grasp the permissibility of non-beneficial actions
The text says God may do what is not beneficial to a servant. This reinforces divine freedom.
Think of examples like trials or delays of rewards.
4
Appreciate the unlimited nature of possible benefit
The final sentence states there is no limit to what God can do for benefit, but no obligation to go beyond His actual decree.
This closes the door to any claim that God must do more.

The Mu'tazili Proof for al-Aslah (The Most Beneficial)
Mu'tazili proof for the obligation of al-aslah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili rational argument that Allah must always do what is most beneficial for His servants, based on analogy with human wisdom.

Core Idea
If a wise being can benefit others without cost, they must do so—and Allah is the wisest.

The Mu'tazila built a powerful rational case for the doctrine of al-aslah, claiming that Allah's wisdom and generosity necessitate that He always chooses the best course for His creation. In this lesson, we examine their central argument, which draws on analogies from human conduct to establish a universal principle of wisdom. Understanding this proof is essential for grasping the Mu'tazili worldview and the Sunni response to it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili argument carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument is constructed from a general principle about wise beings.
The passage uses dense rational language—take your time.
2
Identify the two analogies
The argument uses two analogies: one about a commander and his obedient servant, another about reconciling an enemy. See how both illustrate the same principle.
These analogies are meant to show that withholding the means to obedience would contradict wisdom.
3
Examine the application to Allah
The argument then applies the principle to Allah, concluding that He must always provide the most beneficial means, including sickness and health, to lead servants to obedience.
Consider why the Mu'tazila think this conclusion is inevitable given Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة المعتزلة في قولهم الأصلح: وشبهتهم التي يعتمدون عليها أنا وجدنا الحكيم إذا كان أمرًا بطاعة محبًا لها مريدًا، فلن يجوز أن يمنع المأمور ما يصل به إلى طاعته إذا كان قادرا على أن يعطيه ذلك، وكان بذله إياه لا يخرجه من استحقاق الوصف بالحكمة ومنعه لا ينفعه. وكذلك إذا كان له عدو يدعوه إلى موالاته ويجب رجوعه إلى طاعته فلن يجوز أن يعامله من الغلظ واللين إلا بما يعلم أنه أنجع فيما يريد منه، وأدعى له إلى ترك ما هو فيه من عداوته، فإن عرض له أمران من الشدة والغلظة والملاينة والملاطفة يعلم أن أحدهما أدعى لعدوه إلى المراجعة والإنابة، والآخر دون ذلك ففعل الأدون وترك أن يفعل الأصلح الأدعى وكلاهما في قدرته عليهما بمنزلة لا يضره بذلهما ولا ينفعه منعهما، كان عند الحكماء جميعًا مذموماً خارجاً عن استحقاق الوصف بالجود والحكمة. فلما كان هذا فيما بينا على ما وصفنا، وكان الله عز وجل قادرا رحيما جوادًا عالمًا بمواضع حاجة عبادة آمرا لهم بطاعته وترك عداوته والرجوع إلى ولايته لا يضره الإعطاء ولا ينفعه المنع ولا يلته منه ذم، علمنا أنه لا يفعل بهم إلا أصلح الأشياء لهم في دينهم وأدعى لهم إلى طاعته سقما كان ذلك أو صحة لذة أو ألمًا ، آمنوا أو كفروا ، أطاعوا أو عصوا قال الله تعالى: وَبَلَوْنَهُم بِالحَسَنَاتِ وَالسَّيِّئَاتِ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَرْجِعُونَ وقال تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا فِي قَرْيَةٍ مِّن نَّبِيِّ إِلَّا أَخَذْنَا أَهْلَهَا بِالْبَأْسَاءِ وَالضَّرَّاءِ ). دل ذلك أنه يعاملهم بمختلف الأحوال على ما يرى الأصلح لهم والأدعى إلى الحق. وربما يوضحون هذا بمن أنجز ضيافة لرجل وأمره بحضوره وأراد ذلك، وعلم أنه لو دعاه ببشر وملايته لحضر وحصل مراده، ولو فعل ذلك بغلظة وعبوس لم يحضر ، يجب عليه أن يدعوه ببشر وملاطفة ولو عامله بضد ذلك لما كان حكيمًا، وصار منع ذلك بمنزلة منع التمكين من الحضور، ومنع التمكين عن فعل ما أمره به وذا ليس بحكمة بل هو سفه فكذا هذا. وربما يقولون لو أعطى العبد ما في مقدوره من اللطف انتفع به ولم يتضرر الله تعالى.
The doubt of the Mu'tazila concerning their saying al-aslah: And their doubt upon which they rely is that we found the wise person, when he commands an obedient deed, loves it and wills it, it is not permissible for him to prevent the commanded one from what leads him to his obedience if he is able to give it to him, and his giving it does not remove him from deserving the attribute of wisdom, and his prevention does not benefit him. And likewise, if he has an enemy calling him to his alliance and his return to his obedience is obligatory, it is not permissible for him to treat him with harshness or gentleness except with what he knows is most effective for what he wants from him, and more conducive for him to leave his enmity. If two matters of severity and harshness and gentleness and kindness occur to him, he knows that one of them is more conducive for his enemy to return and repent, and the other is less than that, and he does the lesser and leaves to do the more beneficial and more conducive, while both are within his power in a position that their doing does not harm him and their prevention does not benefit him, he would be, according to all the wise, blameworthy and outside of deserving the attribute of generosity and wisdom. Since this is as we described, and Allah Almighty is powerful, merciful, generous, knowing the places of need of His servants, commanding them to obey Him and leave enmity and return to His allegiance, giving does not harm Him nor prevention benefit Him, and blame does not attach to Him [؟], we know that He does not do with them except the most beneficial of things for them in their religion and most conducive for them to His obedience, whether that be sickness or health, pleasure or pain, whether they believe or disbelieve, obey or disobey. Allah Almighty said: 'And We tried them with good things and evil that perhaps they would return' and said: 'And We did not send into a town any prophet except that We seized its people with misery and hardship.' That indicates that He treats them with various conditions according to what He sees as most beneficial for them and most conducive to the truth. And perhaps they clarify this with the example of a man who prepared a feast for a man and commanded him to attend and wanted that, and he knew that if he invited him with cheerfulness and gentleness he would attend and his purpose would be achieved, and if he did that with harshness and frowning he would not attend, he must invite him with cheerfulness and gentleness, and if he treated him with the opposite of that, he would not be wise. And preventing that becomes like preventing enabling attendance, and preventing enabling of doing what he commanded him, and that is not wisdom but foolishness; so likewise this. And perhaps they say: if He gave the servant what is in His power of grace, he would benefit from it and Allah would not be harmed.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazili argument (shubha) for the doctrine that Allah must always do what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants. The argument is based on analogy with human behavior. It begins by stating that a wise person who commands an action and desires its fulfillment cannot withhold the means by which the commanded person can perform that action, if he has the ability to give them and it does not undermine his wisdom. Similarly, if a wise person has an enemy whom he wishes to reconcile, he must use the most effective means—whether harsh or gentle—to achieve that reconciliation. If he knows one approach is more effective but chooses a less effective one while both are equally possible and costless, he would be considered blameworthy and not truly wise. Applying this to Allah: He is all-powerful, merciful, generous, and knows His servants' needs. He commands obedience and desires it. He is not harmed by giving nor benefited by withholding, and no blame attaches to Him. Therefore, the Mu'tazila conclude that He must always do the most beneficial things for His servants in religious matters, whether through sickness or health, pleasure or pain, faith or disbelief, obedience or sin. They support this with Qur'anic verses (7:168, 7:94) that indicate Allah tests people with varying conditions to lead them back to the truth. They further illustrate with an analogy: a host who invites a guest must use a friendly tone if that ensures attendance; otherwise, he would be acting foolishly. Thus, Allah must provide all possible grace (lutf) to enable obedience, since giving grace benefits the servant and does not harm Allah.

Key Terms
الأصلح
اللطف
التكليف
الحكمة
الجود
العدو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazili argument is often taken to imply that Allah is under an external obligation, limiting His absolute power.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue that it is a necessary consequence of His wisdom and generosity, not an external compulsion. For them, wisdom inherently requires choosing the best course.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazili argument presented, why must Allah always do the most beneficial?
A) Because He is compelled by His essence.
B) Because a wise being cannot withhold benefit when it costs nothing and achieves a desired end.
C) Because the Qur'an explicitly commands it.
D) Because without it, humans would have no reason to obey.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that a wise being must provide the most beneficial means to achieve a desired end, and since Allah is perfectly wise and able, He must always do al-aslah for His servants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a wise human must provide the means for obedience, how do you respond when you know the right action but fail to act on it? Does your inaction imply a lack of wisdom in you, and what does that say about your sincerity in desiring obedience? Will you commit to removing any obstacles you place in your own path to obedience?
?
The Mu'tazila claim that Allah must do what is most beneficial, even through pain and sickness. Think of a trial you faced—can you honestly see it as al-aslah for your deen? If not, what does that reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom? Will you ask Allah to help you see His wisdom in your trials?
?
If you were the host inviting a guest, you would choose a friendly tone to ensure his attendance. Are you treating others with the same wisdom, using the most effective means to guide them to good, or do you sometimes act harshly even when gentleness would be more effective? Will you be more mindful of how your actions affect others' obedience to Allah?

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
The Core Analogy: Choosing the Optimum
Analogy of a wise person applied to God's obligation to do al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite analogy of a wise person choosing the most effective means and how it applies to God's obligation to do the most beneficial for His servants.

Core Idea
A wise being always chooses the superior means; God must do the same.

The Mu'tazilites argue that a wise person, when faced with two options, naturally chooses the one that is most effective and beneficial. Since God is perfectly wise and generous, He must also choose the most beneficial course for His servants. This passage presents the foundational analogy and its application to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy of the enemy
Read the first part where the author describes a person calling an enemy to allegiance. Notice the condition that both options are equally available and harmless.
2
Identify the judgment of the wise
See that if the person chooses the inferior option despite knowing the superior one, he is blamed by the wise as lacking generosity and wisdom.
3
Apply the analogy to God
Read how the analogy is applied to God: since God is powerful, merciful, generous, and unaffected by giving or withholding, He must do only the most beneficial for His servants.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that God's actions include varying conditions like health, sickness, belief, and disbelief, all for the sake of compelling obedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك إذا كان له عدو يدعوه إلى موالاته ويجب رجوعه إلى طاعته فلن يجوز أن يعامله من الغلظ واللين إلا بما يعلم أنه أنجع فيما يريد منه، وأدعى له إلى ترك ما هو فيه من عداوته، فإن عرض له أمران من الشدة والغلظة والملاينة والملاطفة يعلم أن أحدهما أدعى لعدوه إلى المراجعة والإنابة، والآخر دون ذلك ففعل الأدون وترك أن يفعل الأصلح الأدعى وكلاهما في قدرته عليهما بمنزلة لا يضره بذلهما ولا ينفعه منعهما، كان عند الحكماء جميعاً مذموماً خارجاً عن استحقاق الوصف بالجود والحكمة، فلما كان هذا فيما بينا على ما وصفنا، وكان الله عز وجل قادرا رحيما جواداً عالمًا بمواضع حاجة عباده آمرا لهم بطاعته وترك عداوته والرجوع إلى ولايته لا يضره الإعطاء ولا ينفعه المنع ولا يلحقه منه ذم، علمنا أنه لا يفعل بهم إلا أصلح الأشياء لهم في دينهم وأدعى لهم إلى طاعته سقما كان ذلك أو صحة لذة أو ألماً، آمنوا أو كفروا، أطاعوا أو عصوا. قال الله تعالى: وَبَلَوْنَهُم بِالحَسَنَاتِ وَالسَّيِّئَاتِ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَرْجِعُونَ (٢) وقال تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا فِي قَرْيَةٍ مِّن نَّبِيِّ إِلَّا أَخَذْنَا أَهْلَهَا بِالْبَأْسَاءِ وَالضَّرَّاءِ ) (٣). دل ذلك أنه يعاملهم بمختلف الأحوال(4) على ما يرى الأصلح لهم والأدعى إلى الحق، وربما يوضحون هذا بمن أنجز ضيافة لرجل وأمره بحضوره وأراد ذلك، وعلم أنه لو دعاه ببشر وملايته لحضر وحصل مراده، ولو فعل ذلك بغلظة وعبوس لم يحضر، يجب عليه أن يدعوه ببشر وملاطفة ولو عامله بضد ذلك لما كان حكيمًا، وصار منع ذلك بمنزلة منع التمكين من الحضور، ومنع التمكين عن فعل ما أمره به وذا ليس بحكمة بل هو سفه فكذا هذا، وربما يقولون لو أعطى العبد ما في مقدوره من اللطف انتفع به ولم يتضرر الله تعالى ولو لم يعطه لتضرر العبد وما انتفع الله تعالى بالمنع، ومن منع غيره ما لو أعطى لانتفع به غيره ولو لم يعط لتضرر به غيره، ولا ضرر على المانع بالإعطاء، ولا منفعة له بالمنع لعُدَّ نهاية في البخل والسفه، والقساوة والله منزه عن الوصف بهذه الصفات.
And likewise, if one has an enemy whom he calls to his allegiance and whose return to obedience is obligatory, he may not treat him with harshness or gentleness except with what he knows is most effective in what he wants from him, and most likely to make him abandon the enmity he is in. For if two matters present themselves to him—severity and harshness versus softness and gentleness—and he knows that one of them is more likely to make his enemy return and repent, while the other is inferior, and he does the inferior and omits to do the more beneficial and more compelling, while both are within his power and are such that neither giving them harms him nor withholding them benefits him—then he would be universally blamed by the wise, falling outside the description of generosity and wisdom. Since this is as we have described, and since Allah Almighty is powerful, merciful, generous, knowing the places of need of His servants, commanding them to obey Him and abandon enmity and return to His allegiance—and giving does not harm Him nor withholding benefit Him, nor does any blame attach to Him—we know that He does not do with them except the most beneficial of things for their religion and the most compelling for them to obey Him, whether that be sickness or health, pleasure or pain, whether they believe or disbelieve, obey or disobey. Allah Almighty said: 'And We tested them with good things and evil so that they might return' (7:168), and He said: 'And We did not send a prophet to any town but We seized its people with adversity and hardship' (7:94). That indicates that He treats them with varying conditions according to what He sees as most beneficial for them and most compelling to the truth. Sometimes they clarify this with the example of a man who prepared a banquet for another and ordered him to attend, desiring that, and knew that if he invited him cheerfully and gently he would attend and his purpose would be achieved, but if he did so with harshness and frowning he would not attend—then he must invite him cheerfully and gently, and if he treated him otherwise he would not be wise. And preventing that is like preventing the enabling of attendance, and preventing the enabling of doing what he commanded is not wisdom but foolishness. So too is this. Sometimes they say: If God gave the servant what is within His power of grace, the servant would benefit and God would not be harmed; and if He did not give it, the servant would be harmed and God would not benefit from withholding. And whoever withholds from another what, if given, would benefit the other, and if not given would harm the other, while the giver suffers no harm in giving nor gain in withholding, is counted as extreme in stinginess and foolishness and cruelty. And Allah is free from being described with such attributes.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite argument for the doctrine of al-aslah, i.e., that God must do what is most beneficial for His servants. The author begins with an analogy: if a person has an enemy whom he calls to allegiance and obedience, a wise person would use the most effective means—whether harsh or gentle—to achieve the desired return, choosing the superior option if both are available and neither harms him. If he chooses the inferior, he is blamed by the wise as lacking generosity and wisdom. Then the author applies the analogy to God: since God is powerful, merciful, generous, and knows His servants' needs, and since giving does not harm Him nor withholding benefit Him, He must do only the most beneficial for them in religion, compelling them to obedience through various conditions (health, sickness, pleasure, pain, belief, disbelief, obedience, disobedience). Two Quranic verses are cited to support that God tests people with good and evil to lead them back. The passage then provides a second analogy: a host who invites a guest knows that a cheerful invitation will bring the guest, but a harsh one will not; thus a wise host must invite cheerfully. Similarly, God must provide the best means (lutf) to guide His servants. If He withholds it, He would be like a stingy, foolish, and cruel person, but God is free from such attributes. The overall argument is that if God does not provide the most effective grace to bring His servants to obedience, He would be acting contrary to wisdom and justice, which is impossible for God.

Key Terms
الأصلح (al-aslah)
الجود (al-jud)
الحكمة (al-hikmah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is forced to do the best by external compulsion.
Correction: The argument is based on God's own wisdom and generosity, not external force.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite analogy, what is the consequence of choosing the inferior option when the superior is equally available?
A) The person is praised for moderation.
B) The person is blamed by the wise as lacking generosity and wisdom.
C) The person is considered neutral.
D) The person is rewarded for patience.

Key Takeaway
God's perfect wisdom and generosity necessitate that He always does what is most beneficial for His servants' guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face illness or pain, do you genuinely believe that this is the most beneficial thing for your faith? If your heart resists, what does that reveal about your trust in God's wisdom?
?
The analogy describes a person calling an enemy to return. In what ways have you been an enemy to God through disobedience? Can you see the specific harshness or gentleness in your life as precisely what you need to return? What would it look like to respond to it as such?
?
If God only does what is most beneficial for your religion, then what is the most beneficial thing for you to do right now? Are you avoiding it because it seems difficult? What would it mean to embrace sickness or health as from Him for your good, and how would that change your actions today?
